THE RED PAGE .
A. Russian Neo-Pagan.
FROM Russia, perhaps, will come, one day,
a genius of Letters able to do something :
more than repeat and refine the thoughts 1
of the dead, and the Slav people produce the 1
next supreme Poet. European writers even of the
first rank have for long been only graceful spinners
of words, fashioning familiar gold into more dainty
shapes. But in Russian literature there are to be
found indications of a rugged strength and virile
originality in style and thought which make for ;
genius.
Dimitri Merejkowski, the latest Russian novelist ;
to command the attention of translation into 1
foreign languages, gives more promise of real
greatness than either Tolstoi or Dostoievski or
Gorki. He has more imagination, more human ,
sympathy, more breadth of thought than any of '
the three. There is something of the torrential 1
Pindaric quality in his vast pictures of life, which 1
seize the emotions and hold them breathless. Yet 1
he lacks almost all the technique of the novelist’s 1
art, and throws the riches of his mind before the
reader, as a drunkard in his cups scatters pell-
mell the viands from the banqueting board to ■
the floor. The “Forerunner,” Merejkowski’s
latest and so far best work, aims to give
on one canvas the whole story of the Italy
of the Renaissance—crowding together in the
picture the Borgias, the Sforzas, Michaelangelo,
Raphael, MachiaVelli, Savonarola, and Leonardo
da Vinci. The personality and philosophy of the
last is the only thread of coherence throughout—
the unhappy story of his splendid, though in
most respects futile, career. On that is strung
incident after incident in barbaric confusion —
the rise and fall of princes and cities, the
careers of painters and peoples, pictures of the
Vild luxury, the bestial dissoluteness, the un-
canny ventures in Black Art of medieval Italy.
The Renaissance of Paganism, and the toilets of
Beatrice of Milan; the philosophy of mathe-
matics, and the amours of the Borgias ; the ser-
mons of Savonarola and wine-house brawls; Machi-
avelli’s terrible teachings and his low intrigues
—all these and more, much more, are presented in a
phantasmagoria which makes the mind reel as
with strong wine. Yet withal the picture,
though crowded, confused, kaleidoscopic, is real.
There is not a mere aggregation of facts, a piling
up of the dry bones of knowledge. Unlike a
Macaulay, spreading his laborious peacock tail,
Merejkowski gets the atmosphere of the Italy of
the Renaissance. He seems to have lived in the
days whose terrible tale he tells—as it were, with
gasping breath and mind confused by the multi-
tude of its impressions. And his heroic figure,
Leonardo da Vinci, stands out from the welter of
personages and incidents, with real distinctness,
living and grand. Less clearly but still palpably,
shows the motif of the book, the argument between
Christianity and Paganism, between Revelation
and Science.
That is a subjeer oA which Europe in the 19th
century heard much. After some centuries of
undisturbed, if but little genuine, belief in Christi-
anity, a whole host of critics sprang up in a night.
Nietszche, Heine, Wagner, Schopenhauer, Whit-
man, the English Hedonists, a section of devout
religionists of whom St. George Mivart was the
type, and many others, began (all at once it might
almost be said) to declare, suggest, or hint
that the great minds of Greece and Rome
were not deep sunk in abysmal ignorance so far
as spiritual philosophy was concerned, that there
was much reason in a Pantheism, which, after all,
might be construed as a symbolical interpretation
of the one Divine mind finding expression in
different ways. The 19th century saw, in short, a
revival of “ Paganism ” —a frank avowal of Hedon-
ism in some writers; an effort on the part of a
great Roman Catholic philosopher to reconcile the
views of the most dogmatic of Christian faiths
with a belief in God worshipped in the form of
Venus, Jove, or Bacchus; and
professed in many quarters to the Christian ideal
as unnatural, unreasonable, and therefore im-
moral. This movement was precisely matched
during the Renaissance in Italy—though the
churches were then stronger to combat heretical
opinion than now, and the facts which present-day
destructive critics of orthodoxy may cite from the
researches of science were not then available.
Merejkowski, who evidently sympathises with
Hedonism (a lofty, not a sensual, Hedonism—
the ideas of a Marcus Aurelius, or of an
Epictetus) traces minutely the Pagan revival
which marked the efflorescence of Italian
thought in the 15th and 16th centuries, and
he makes Da Vinci (the painter of “The Last
Supper”) its prophet and forerunner. Vinci is
the personage, the discussion of Christian ideals
and their opposites the purpose, of the book.
Whilst in its whole effect “The Forerunner”
ts chaotic and disordered, the detail work is always
fine. In some cases it may be unnecessary, but it
is never in itself paltry, This, for instance, of a
sermon by Savonarola.
Giovanni saw a man in the black and white Dominican
habit girdled with a rope, who slowly ascended the
pulpit-stair and removed his cowl. His face was emaci-
ated and yellow as wax, his lips thick, his nose aquiline,
his forehead low. His left hand fell weakly on the desk,
his right he raised clutching the crucifix; and silently
with burning eyes he looked upon the trembling and
expectant crowd. Profound silence reigned, in which
each man could hear the beating of his own heart. The
eyes of the monk glowed increasingly, till they were like
fiery coals ; but he still kept silence, and the strain of
waiting became unendurable. It seemed that in another
moment the crowd would burst into screams.
Yet the calm became more deep, more awful ; till,
suddenly rending the silence,came the terrible, lacerating,
superhuman cry of the friar
“ Ecee, ego adduco aquas super terram (Behold I bring
a flood upon the earth !) ”
A shudder passed through the crowd, raising the hair
from the head. Giovanni paled ; he fancied the earth
quaking, the cathedral arches about to fall. Beside him
the stalwart tinker was shaking like a leaf, his teeth
chattering. The head of the feeble carpenter had sunk
backward on his shoulders as if he had received a blow,
his face was shrivelled, his eyelids closed.
What followed was not a sermon but a delirium, which
took hold of those thousands of people and shook them
as a storm shakes the withered leaves. Giovanni listened,
scarcely understanding. Detached phrases reached his
ear:—
“O Florence! O Rome ! O Italy ! Past is the time of
songs and of feasting ye are sickened unto death. Lord;
Thou art witness, that with my words I would have
averted this ruin ! But I can no more. I have no words
more. I can but weep, and run over with my tears.
Mercy ! Mercy ! O merciful Lord ! Alas ! my poor
people ,! Alas ! my Florence ! ”
He opened his arms, and the last words had sunk to a
scarcely audible whisper. They passed over the crowd
and died away, like the rustle of wind in the leaves—a
sigh of infinite pity.
Pressing his white lips on the crucifix, he knelt and
burst into sobs. The sermon was ended. The slow,
heavy organ-notes rolled out, persuasive and immense, in-
creasingly solemn and terrible, like the sound of the
mighty ocean.
A woman’s voice cried “ Misericordia 1 ”
And thousands of voices answered, calling one to
another ; and like corn stalks bowing before the wind,
the people fell upon their knees, line upon line, wave
upon wave, crowding upon, striking against each other,
like a flock of sheep panic-struck at the advance of a
storm ; and the long, agonising wail of penitents, upon
whom pressed the terror of immediate ruin, rose to
Heaven, mingling with the pealing of music, shaking the
ground, the marble pillars, and the vaults of the
cathedral.
“ Misericordia! Misericordia ! ”
Giovanni also sank .to his knees, sobbing.
At that moment, raising his face, wet with tears, he
saw at his side the tall, upright form of Leonardo da
Vinci. The artist, leaning carelessly against a column,
held in his right hand his unfailing sketch-book ; with
his left he was drawing; how and then he glanced at the
pulpit as if hoping to see once more the head of the
preacher.
A stranger, and surrounded by the terrified crowd,
Leonardo maintained a superb composure. In his cool,
blue eyes, on his thin lips, tightly compressed like those
of a man of minute observation, there was the same aloof-
ness and curiosity with which he mathematically measured
the body of the Aphrodite. At sight of him the tears
dried in Giovanni’s eyes, and the prayer was silenced
upon his lips.
Leaving the church he followed the artist and asked
permission to see his sketch. Leonardo demurred, but
presently handed the boy his sketch-book. And Giovanni
saw a frightful caricature ; not Savonarola, but an old and
hideous devil in the dress of a monk, like the preacher in-
deed, but as if disfigured by self-inflicted and torturing
penance, his pride and his desires still unsubdued. The
lower jaw protruded, wrinkles intersected the cheek, the
neck was twisted and black as a mummy’s, the bushy,
beetling brows, the rabid glance scarce preserved a
semblance of humanity. All that was dark, terrible, and
superstitious, all which gave Savonarola into the power
of the deformed, tongue-tied visionary Maruf, was ex-
pressed by Leonardo in this sketch ; brought out with
neither anger nor pity, but with an imperturbable and
impartial clear-sightedness.
And Giovanni remembered his words: “ L’ingegno dell’
pittore vuol essere a similitudine dello specchio.” (The
genius of the painter should be as a mirror, reflecting all
objects, and colours, and movements, itself ever trans-
parent and serene.)
Machiavelli as seen by Merejkowski:—
The man had great bony hands, copiously dyed with
ink, and a wart on one finger. There was little dignity
in his air ; he was lean and narrow shouldered, about 40
years of age, and with sharp, irregular features. Some-
times when he was speaking he would look over the head
of his interlocutor, as if peering into space like some long-
sighted, rapacious bird. In his restless movements, in
the feverish flush of his swarthy cheeks, above all, in the
intentness of his large, grey eyes, there was evidence of
smouldering fire within. The eyes themselves were
malicious ; yet at times, in their sardonic smile, in their
cold displeasure, there was an expression of weakness
almost pathetic.
Niccold Machiavelli continued to pour forth his
notions ; and Leonardo marvelled at the strange mixture
of truth and error in his talk, at his audacity, and his
slavish appeal to the authority of the ancients.
Much of Machiavelli’s conversation in “ The
Forerunner” is transferred bodily from “The
Prince.” This is more original:—
A little later, Lucio, who was passing through Fano on
his way to Ancona, came in for a visit, and Machiavelli
spoke to him of the execution.of Don. Ramiro de Lorqua.
“To divine the real reasons for the actions of a ruler
like Cassar Borgia is almost impossible,” he said, “ but as
you ask me what I think of this deed, I will tell you.
Till its conquest by the duke, Romagna was under the
yoke of a number of petty tyrants, and full of disorder,
plundering, and violence. To end this state of tur-
bulence Cassar appointed his astute and faithful servant,
Don Ramiro, as his lieutenant. This man accom-
plished his task; he inspired the people with a
salutary terror, and established perfect tranquility
throughout the country, but he did it by a long series of
cruel punishments. When the prince saw that his object
was gained, he determined to destroy the instrument of
his severity. Don Ramiro has been seized, on the ground
of extortion, and executed ; his dead body lies exposed
to public view. This terrible spectacle has at once grati-
fied and awed the people. The duke’s action has been
wise, for he has reaped three clear advantages. First, he
has slain the tyrants; secondly, by condemning Ramiro,
he has dissociated himself from his lieutenant’s ferocity,
and so has gained a character for gentleness ; thirdly, by
sacrificing his favorite servant, he has set an example of
incorruptible equity.”
in a low, dry voice, with expression-
less countenance, as if stating his reasoning on some
theorem.
“ From your own words, Messer Niccolb,” cried Lucio,
“ I perceive that this supposed equity is the excess of
villainy 1 ”
Sparks of fire appeared in the secretary’s eyes, but he
looked away and spoke as coldly as before.
“ It may be so,” he assented, “ but what of it ? ”
“What of it? Would you approve such scoundrelly
statecraft ? ”
“Young man, you speak with the inexperience of youth.
In politics, the difference between the way men should,
and the way they do act, is so great, that to forget it
means to expose yourself to certain ruin. For all men
are by nature evil and vicious; they are virtuous only
for advantage or through fear. A prince who would
avoid ruin, must at all hazards learn the art of appear-
ing virtuous ; and he must be or not be virtuous as the
case may require. He must disregard all uneasiness of
conscience as to those secret measures without which the
preservation of power is impossible ; for upon accurate
knowledge of the nature of good and evil, it is clear that
the power of a prince will often be undermined by his
virtuous actions and augmented by his crimes.”
Lucio again protested. “Reasoning thus,” he cried,
“ anything would be permissible, and there is no wicked-
ness which you could not justify ! .*
“ That is so,” replied Machiavelli, with perfect serenity
The Russian writer makes to live his huge gallery
of characters. With wonderful care Merejkowski
has studied his facts. In truth “The Forerunner,”
were it not remarkable for the daring of its
symbolism, the high imaginativeness of its charac-
terisation, would be admirable for the evidently
patient and humble care in research of its author.
It is a mine of facts and a mine of poetry ; a
treasure of history and a daring essay into the
Secrets of the Unknown. Loose in construction
(much more so than that great romance Les
Miserables), showing plainly a mental confusion,
a groping after elusive conclusions, “The Fore-
runner ” is withal the work of a great writer.
F.R.
“C.C.C.” regarding some points of N.S. W.
Library administration:
To whom is the public of N.S.W. indebted for the pre-
sent chaotic state of the Sydney Free Library? Some
little time ago, I had occasion to refer to a collection of
French fables, chansons, folk-songs, &e., published under
the title of “ Drdleries Joyeuses,” which, though duly
catalogued, was not to be found on the Library shelves.
On reference to an attendant-; I found that the volume,
together with several others, which were not considered
“ ver y n >ce ”(I use his own words), was kept in a special
part of the building, not accessible to the public. It was
with great difficulty I got the hook, and only on promis-
ing not to keep it too long and to return it directly to the
attendaht. So far as I could see. there was nothing in
the work that would warrant its being placed on an index
of prohibited books. If it be an improper production,
those who are responsible for the purging of the Sydney
Library have used very little discrimination in their
task. They have strained at some very insignificant
gnats and swallowed enormous camels. In the very first
room one enters, these gentlemen could now find what,
to them, would be as choice a collection of ancient
and modern filth and obscenity as could be
found anywhere in a midsummer day’s search —Martial,
Juvenal, Lucian, Balzac, Dubois, Gautier, Latour are all
there, naked and unashamed ; whilst hot far away the
amateur of the erotic can find, bound up with an innocent
looking “Cornelius Nepos,” the unutterably filthy
“Satvricon” of Petronius Arbiter. Why the censors
should draw distinctions between “ Droleries Joyeuses ”
and the authors I have mentioned is best known to them-
selves. To the average person it is quite inexplicable,
it cannot be denied for a moriient that the pages of many
Latin, French, and English authors are disfigured by
much that is objectionable, but that constitutes no valid
reason why the public, whose property they are,
should have any difficulties placed in the way of
reading them. There is probably no finer description of
a Roman banquet extant than that given by Petronius,
and perhaps nothing more licentious than the same
author’s story of the orgies at the house of Quartilla, and
both, from a literary standpoint, are valuable. Mingled
with the dirt and garbage, there is much fine gold which
ihe literary chiffonier would not fail to pick out. In the
case of foreign authors, no great danger to the public
morals need be apprehended, because the great majority
of those visiting the Library would not be able to read
them. But what is the object of all this manipula-
tion of the literary property of the public'? It
cannot surely be the protection of the young per-
son’s morals, seeing that the modern sex novel,
which he or she is at liberty to buy and
read ad libitum, is quite as objectionable—actually
and suggestively—as anything I have named. Take, for
instance, “ A Daughter of the Vine.”
The only raison f,tre of this contemptible book is an
illicit infant conceived under singularly objectionable cir-
cumstances, and with such hog-wash the book markets of
the world are literally flooded. Again, how many parents
in this Commonwealth would care to deny their children
free access to the Holy Scriptures, and where is there
such a cloaca of obscenity as the Old Testament? Or
bow many of the Rev. Panargus O’Blareaways who
afflict this long-suffering community would dare
to read certain portions of those same Scriptures to
a mixed audience, or, for that matter, to any audience ?
But let anyone propose that the Bible be expurgated
or locked up, and these same O’Blareaways would raise
a howl that would shake Sydney to its foundations. If
those responsible for the management of our Public
Library would gather all literature of the. kind indicated
into a separate room, and there arrange it in sections of
the various languages in which it is written, they could
then easily discriminate as to who should have access
thereto, if such discrimination were thought necessary,
which, for the most part, it would not be. Such a course
would serve a double purpose. It would be a distinct
boon to a small but worthy portion of the reading public,
and tend towards the better preservation of some very
valuable books, amongst others a magnificent collection
of Delphin Classics. As things now are, the reader is
confronted with a perfect wilderness of Latin, Greek,
French, German, Italian, and English authors mixed up
in endless confusion, and often without the least attempt
at classification. What gems of literature may be cribbed
and confined in the Librarian’s chamber of horrors can
only be conjectured.

* ‘ Nymsie ” writes :
Judging by the initial two columns of his latest yarn,
“His Brother’s Keeper,” now running in Melb. Age,
Henry Lawson insults the intelligence of the large army
of Australian readers when he thinks it necessary to
explain the meaning of commonplace “ bush” terms—
the following example:—“He was ‘ringer’ (fastest
shearer) of the shed at Piora Station one season, and made
a decent cheque, and within a fortnight after the shed
‘ cut out ’ (finished shearing) he turned up at home in a
very bad state from drink.” But, maybe, this yarn was
edited for home consumption, and reaches Australia as a
syndicate venture.
Just so; the blame is the editor’s rather than the
author’s.
Angus and Robertson, Sydney, will shortly pub-
lish a book of reminiscences of the late Dean
Cowper, who saw so much of the early life of
N.S.W., but—alas for the literary value of his
observations ! —saw it with so pure and colorless
and conseiehce-bound a soul.
London Daily Chronicle hits off “ Smiler ”
.Hales :
And to a reader in sound health and average brain-
power Mr. Hales is terribly tiring. He is not a bore.
That word just misses him, though as a man of one idea
he stands within its danger. His tiresomeness is that of
the man who speaks always in a shout, and would send
our fingers to our ears even if he were an archangel telling
eternal truths at the top of his voice.
The trustees of the Melbourne Public Library
have granted themselves the privilege of being
able to borrow books. Result: people wishing to
use the library are often inconvenienced in not
being able to get a book, which, it happens, a
trustee has out on loan, and for which they must
wait until the trustee is finished with it The
principal offenders are a student of metaphysics,
and an expert in gore. Another grievance against
this institution is the unenlightened methods of
the book-selection committee, who refuse to get
the works of the leading European writers. Some
time ago they refused to get D’Annunzio’s works,
Huysman is altogether unobtainable, while of
Maeterlinck there are only a couple of volumes.
“H.”: Bailey, of Festus, has gone at last.
Sixty-five years ago he produced that really
great work, and it virtually exhausted him.
Fortunately he was a rich man, and he lived on
his nice estate called Bagatelle. Tennyson said,
apropos the first edition of Festus, that he could
not trust himself to pen his enthusiastic appreci-
ation of it. So with Buhver, and many of the
highest contemporaries. Bailey went on tiddling-
up and spoiling the poem all his life. The thing
is to get the very first edition. I cannot get one
farther back than 1867, with plenty of spoils of
course. The original Festus, scoured of garnish-
ments, will have another run, in its white heat.
Bailey bloated it out to about six times the first
size.
J*
Thursday Island “Governor” John
Douglas, in a recent article in the Nineteenth Cen-
tury, is certainly interesting. Douglas tells bow,
in the year 1851, he roused the ire of William
Charles Wentworth by suggesting that if all the
white population went to the goldfields they could
send for Chinese. Mr. Douglas further asserts that
“ even Dr. Lang, the great agitator and advocate
of British immigration, admitted that we might
have coolies from British India, seeing that
Moreton Bay was a semi-tropical country, and
coolies might help to grow cotton for Manches-
ter.” As to the early Polynesian traffic, Mr.
Douglas is very frank: “The enormities of the
slave trade were reproduced with variations
which only feebly differentiated it from its
African prototype.” He might also have added
that the mortality among the Polynesian slaves
on the Queensland plantations is a feature in
which the Bananaland slave business is decidedly
ahead of the old American system. The slaves
on American plantations increased and multi-
plied. They die-off in Queensland. The Thursday
Island Filipino, Mr. Douglas tells us (when
sufficiently well off) “orders a wife from the con-
ventual schools of Macao” —just as he would buy
a pig or a sheep or a sack of rice. Mr. Douglas is
convinced that “the vast area of Northern Aus-
tralia cannot be peopled by Europeans alone.”
Just as was Dr. Lang that Moreton Bay would
have to be cultivated by coolies.

Editor Kelly, of Wellington (M.L.) Times, will
shortly publish a book of verse, “ Heather and
Fern.” Mr. Kelly’s verse is favorably known to
Bulletin readers.
Some talk about reproducing in book form, in
Sydney, a selection of the fugitive work of the
late Robert Richardson. Richardson published
three or four books in England, and died regret-
ting that he parted with the copyrights there.

Re retaliatory death-penalty. Euripides (though
not a recent authority) is worth quoting:
The wisdom of our ancestors ordained
That he, who had the guilt of blood upon him,
Be not allowed the sight, the walks of men,
By banishment atoning, not by death ;
Else one roust always be to death devote,
Who hath the last pollution on his hands.
Kipling’s next volume of stories is to be illus-
trated with his own drawings.

TO CORRESPONDENTS.
“ Max S." : Not suitable.
“ The Neat Ant ” : Thanks ; but you don’t quite make
your meaning clear (discussing “ Eve Triumphant,”
30/8/’O2), except on this point: “ How long prehistoric
man followed the brute force line of reasoning in dealing
with women we may not be able to certainly know, but
scrambling down the centuries into the articulateness of
historic man we find him, quite recently, enjoined by law
to desist from using anything ‘ thicker than a man’s
thumb ’in the bringing up of woman. Brought up thus
on sapling, woman perhaps found it convenient to be
man’s woman. To-day, with the- historic and the pre-
historic sapling laid to rest, woman finds herself under
another, and scarcely less effective, form of persuasion.
In these days of increased cost of living, no man can sup-
port the women of his establishment, even if they be few.
Women must, therefore, whether she chooses or not,
scramble for her own living; and, with most ways of
making a living so difficult of entrance by her, she is
obliged to give her best attention to the one offering least
resistance. To succeed in this particular line of business,
woman must be man’s woman—whatever that may be—
still.”
“ J. 8.” (Q.) asks “ the name of a-German poem giving
the legend of a Duke. When he reaches the age of 70 he
is turned out into the street by his own son. The old
father begs for shelter and a crust of bread.; it is refused.
Baldwin, the son, sends his own son (five years old) into
the stable to get a horse rug for the old man and on
returning he brings only a half of the rug, and he says,
‘ One half is enough for the old man ; I will keep the
other half for you.’ Twenty years after the boy sends his
father off too 1 ” [The story is familiar—though the end-
ing we used to know was that the provident care of the
youngster brought remorse to the cruel son—but we
are not aware of its origin.]

WINCHESTER
MODEL 1900 SINGLE-SHOT RIFLE
m
HIS rifle sells at a price within reach of almost every-
body’s pocketbook, but it is made with the same care and
exactness as Winchester rifles that cost five times as
much. The name “Winchester” on this rifle is a
guarantee in itself of its quality. The Model 1900 is a
hammerless take-down, and can be taken apart easily
and quickly. It is made with an 18-inch round barrel,
fitted with open front and rear sights. It shoots .22 Short and .22
Long Rim-Fire cartridges, and Bullet Breech caps. These cartridges cost
so little that one can afford io shoot as many times as he likes as often
as he likes. Some makes of .22 Caliber rifles are a few pence cheaper,
but they are a great deal cheaper in quality than they are in price.
We don’t try to see how cheap we can make this rifle, but how good.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
King Barwon.
With tresses in disorder,
With ravening, reaching arms
I cross the trembling border
To waste your western farms ;
To steal your sheep and cattle—■
The poor dumb beasts that be
Too weak to give me battle,
Too dull to turn and flee !
Ho! Stockmen ! Mount and muster before my
hoofs a-thud!
I come to sweep your lowlands! I —Barwon, in
full flood !
No man shall turn my forces;
No power shall stay my steeds,'
The tawny-maned cream horses
Reined with brown river-weeds !
I lash them south before me—■
I flog them east and west—
Fierce as the storm that bore me
They leap to my behest!
My anger points their pathway ! My lust is i
their blood !
And proud they bear their burden—King Barwon
in full flood !
come with roaring thunder
That stills the whimpering wind
The plains I trample under,
And tear, and toss behind.
Among the garden cedars
And through the bush belars
I flog my foaming leaders
Before my chariot-bars.
The rains have swelled tneir sinews ! The storms
have fired their blood !
Now, fly, ye fear-wan settlers! King Barwon
rides full-flood !
* our steamers, warned of trouble,
Toss by your topmost trees,
And tie their flood-ropes double
Ten feet above their quays !
Your skiffs, torn from the rid jea.
Shall lead my squadrons down,
And leap the white Bourke bridges
And scare Menindie town.
High-piled on your verandahs, flood-rack and
river-mud !
My sword upon your gateways ! King Barwon
rides full-flood !
With roaring ranks extended
I hold your North teamsstiyed,
And hear in starlight splendid
Your tinkling fear betrayed !
Your drovers, South with cattle,
Break wire and turn aside—
They know my line-of-battle,
The Barwon—ten miles wide !
I chase their nibbling thousands ! I choke their
mobs in mud !
I leave their white bones bleaching to mark my
path—full flood !
By crumpled hut and railing,
By twisted house and yard,
By wives and weanlings wailing,
My hate —ye foes ! —strikes hard
Now, by the storm that bore me!
Ye risk your lives who stay !
Swift to the range before me i
While still, there lies a way ! j
So ! gently, gently, horses ! Pride of my ramping
stud !
What foes shall stand before us—King Barwon in
full flood ?
WILL H. OGILVIE.
The Wreck.
The bare dunes cry of a mystery,
Where the rotten ribs protrude.
Like a dead man’s hand from the shifting sand
Stretched back to the silent wood ;
And the lank red weed, when the seas recede,
Drops foam, as a hand drops blood.
When the red gleams swim on the outer rim
Of the sea, and the night-ghouls fly,
The truculent tide in the sea-gorge wide
Rolls grey ’neath the rough grey sky ;
On the tumbled bar some ragged star
Reflects like a drowned man’s eye.
And the old wreck calls when the damp dusk I
falls
On the swamp, and the slow mists creep
To the sedge-girt pond and the fen beyond
From the chill of the heavy deep ;
And a muttered word and a curse half-heard
Pass out as a sigh in sleep.
The blackened bulk of the skeleton hulk
In a blacker night is lost,
And the querulous wind in the tamarind
Is the sob of a soul-worn host
From the foetid pit where the dead men sit,
Who died on a haunted coast.
The brown skull roils by the brown sand holes,
And bleaches and bakes and dries,
And the dry jaws shake, and the stealthy snake
Crawls through the sightless eyes ;
And the rising tide fills the sea gorge wide
Where the lost ship’s lost crew lies.
J. A. ALLAN.
“ Yfisa ” : Fiji is the paradise of spiders.
During the rainy season the houses swarm with
them. During the night they meander all over
the house, capturing and slaying cockroaches.
From the bedside to the bathroom in the morning
one crosses a field of dead cockroaches. But soon
each crack and hole vomits myriads of small ants,
and the cockroach corpses are tugged away to the
ants’ larders. The weird, pink spiders, with
lavender eyes, which the fusel-oil drinker in the
bush shanty tries to catch and put in bottles, are
found here in reality, lurking in one’s shoes, or
clinging to the bath-towels.

A LIVER’S VAGARIES.
Nervous Weakness and Exhaustion.
A Great Change for the Better.
The Case of
Mrs £. SHEWANJ
(B? OCR REPORTER.)
Mrs. Elizabeth Siiewan. of No. 8 Little
Ed warti-street, Pyrmont, was recently in-
terviewed by a press representative, and
gave her views on various matters of in-
terest.
“ I am given to understand that you
created a great surprise amongst your
neighbours a little while back,” said the
newspaper man. “ Will you kindly tell me
what it was all about ? ”
“ With the utmost pleasure,” was the
hearty reply. “It was by shaking off a
long-standing comp aint that I surprised
everybody, myself included, for I had been
suffering for many years from dyspepsia.
The reason why mv recovery was unex-
pected was because I had been under the
care of several doctors, taking their medi-
cines till I positively loathed the sight of it,
besides being treated as an out-door patient
at a hospital for five months, and no good
came from all the physics which Had been
administered to me. What else could I
think than that I was incurable ? And to
be in such a hopeless predicament was really
awful. Earth and heaven were both blotted
out of my existence, as the inetfioacy of the
medical treatment I had received left me
alone in the world with nothing but physi-
cal suffering to endure. For years there
was scarcely a day passed but that 1 felt
intensely tired and so weary and weak that
I had to let my work go undone because I
was unable to do it, and often I could hardly
get about for the terrible pains in nay chest
and between my shoulders. |lt seemed as if
something heavy was preventing me from
getting my breath, and if the weather was
at all warm the smothering sensations were
frightfully distressing. The whole of my
system was out of order, the consequence
being that I was hardly ever without a de-
pressing headache, and a feeling as if my
eyes were swollen also troubled me. When-
ever I made an effort to do a little work the
exertion made me feel lightheaded, and at
times I was afraid to go outside the door on
account of giddiness, which used to seize me
and make me powerless to stand on my feet.
Another reason why I deemed it prudent to
keep as still as possible was that any exer-
tion gave me palpitation of the heart. It
might be through getting up from a chair
rather too quickly, or, perhaps, through a
little agitation caused by sounds in the
street that the palpitations came on ; but in
every instance I felt greatly distressed, to
say nothing of the fears about what might
be wrong with me which entered my mind.
So many people in this country die from
various affections of the heart that anything
wrong with that organ is naturally alarm-
ing, but I now know that it was my liver
which cause., those serious symptoms to
appear. ”
“ Were there any other liver symptoms in
evidence ?"
“ Well, the gall-like taste in my mouth
and the furred condition of my tongue
pointed to the fact that my internal organs
required renovating, whi'e to digest my food
was an utter impossibility. Try what I
would in the form of light diet, it was all
the same, after eating being violent spasms
of wind in the stomach which almost drove
me frantic, and often I felt inclined to roll
on the floor in the excess of agony, which
was making my life insupportable. The
food I had eaten was not taken from choice,
because I had no appetite for it, and it was
this continuous loss of appetite which
grieved me, as I knew that I could never get
strong so long as I was unable to take
nourishment to give me strength. And as
my vitality wasted away, so did my loins
and limbs ache. I felt as sore as if I had
been beaten from head to foot, and no won-
der that my bones were racked with aches,
for my body and brain seldom got any rest.
Sleepless nights I passed in profusion, al-
though if I slept at all I dreamed in such a
terrifying manner that wakefulness would
have been far preferable. What a nervous
wreck I became, to be sure ! My nerves
were so weak and highly strung that if any-
body knocked at the door I nearly jumped
out of my chair, and then it took them a
long time to get steady again. A dread of
something happening tormented my mind
both day and night, but did I not have good
reason to be like that,- considering that m*y
disease stood defiantly against my medi-
cines ? ”
“As you are so well now, Mrs. She wan,
that statement is scarcely correct.”
“I mean up to the time of taking
Clements Tonic ; then everything changed
for the better. I made a great mistake in
not heeding the advice of many of my friends
who had repeatedly urged me to take
Clements Tonic ; but I thought if doctors
could not cure me, nothing could. at
last I became persuaded, Clements Tonic
soon showed what a mistaken notion this
was, for after taking two or three bottles I
felt quite different, my improved appearance
being the subject of general comment
amongst my neighbours. The appetite I
gained through the medium of Clements
Tonic was, to say the least of it, remark-
able. I was always ready for my meals,
and how could I enjoy them ? That dull
and heavy feeling after eating passed away,
and when my food ceased turning to wind
and creating pain, I could soon go about in
comfort amt do a little work, for I always
woke up feeling fresh and vigorous after the
sound sleep which came through using
Clements Tonic. In time my chest and
head pains disappeared, and nothing gave
me greater satisfaction than the banishment
of my heart troubles. I could walk about
quite briskly without there being any pal-
pitation or dizziness, the only inconvenience
which I experienced after three weeks’ use
of Clements Tonic being from nervousness,
for I still trembled a little if I had been ex-
erting myself very much ; but in a few days
later that trouble was entirely remedied,
and it has never come back again. My
digestive system wa3 thoroughly restored to
order, and from that day to this I must say
that the weary aches in my limbs have been
strangers. The acute pains in my head and
shoulders were also carried away by
Clements Tonic, which regulated my in-
ternal organs to a nicety. Such a catalogue
of human suffering as mine was could not
have been more severe, yet Clements Tonic
relieved me of every pain, besides pouring
into my system the invigorating and disease-
curing qualities it possessed, so that I was
left with the full vigour and health of my
younger days. The return of that alacrity
and cheerfulness of spirits that I was wont
to have made my recovery complete, and I
am not likely to forget that it is to Clements
Tonic that my gratitude is owing.”
“ V\ ill you make that admission through
the press ? ”
“ Willingly, for I recognise the fact that
many people are suffering in despair like I
was, and for their benefit you may publish
my testimony in any way you choose.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Em/.abetii Shbwan, of 8 Little Edward-street,
Pyrmont, Sydney, in the State of; New South
Wales, Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly
and sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account
of my illness and cure by Clements Tonic ; and also
contains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements-which I give voluntarily, without re-
ceiving any payment; and I make this solemn
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Pyrmont, this tenth
day of March, one thousand nine hundred and two,
before me,
JAS. MoDOUGALL, J.P.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Weslvalia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Inter provincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 27, 1902.
The Irvine Government’s Slimy Devices.
The Victorian electoral campaign has started
in earnest, and the Irvine-Shiels-Bent
Ministry is going on its cloven hoofs up and
down the State, and dragging its demoralised
tail to and fro on the surface thereof, as ‘ ‘ the
great Retrenchment Government.” In a
sense the Irvine-Shiels-Bent Government
is wise as well as slimy in its day and gener-
ation. The Parliament which it closed up
so violently a few days ago had another year
to run, and if the Ministry had faced the
electors a year hence instead of to-day many
things would have happened in the interval.
The Irvine Government, by the end of 1903,
would either be the Ministry which had pro-
duced another great and disastrous deficit,
which would have made it unpopular with all
classes ; or the Ministry which had imposed
a general land-tax, and possibly an extra
income-tax and an absentee-tax and addi-
tional probate duties, and retrenched heavily
as well, in order to avert the deficit, and
these taxes would have made it unpopular
with the class on whose support it principally
relies. It would either have broken with its
own crowd by really attempting to re-enact
the Factories Act and by really trying to
reform the House of Landlords, or have
quarrelled with the other crowd and shown
itself a hopelessly unreliable combination by
not doing these things. The Government
which can get itself re engaged for three
years on the strength of what it is going
to do has a much easier job than the one
which does things first and applies for a re-
engagement afterwards. The Irvine Cabinet
started its new Factories Act, its Parlia-
mentary Reform scheme and its Re-
trenchment policy. Then it closed up
before it offended anybody by carrying
these measures, or anybody else by not
carrying them. -At the psychological
moment, it came in out of the wet.
• * * •
It goes to the country now as the brilliant
Ministry which is going to get a quart out of
a pint pot. According to Treasurer Shiels,
the deficit will probably prove to be as much
as £700,000, and the Government appeals to
the electors as the brilliant combination
which found this out, and the strong com-
bination which is not alarmed at the dis-
covery, and the wise combination which is
going to put it all to rights. It has
endeavoured to partly relieve the feelings of
the democratic section of the community
by not proposing such heavy retrenchment
as it might have done, and it has appealed to
the soul of the affluent section of the com-
munity by not proposing a general land-tax—
in fact, by not definitely proposing any
new taxation whatever ; and it has appealed
to all sections by its courage in facing the
awful financial situation—though all it has
really done so far consists of the fact that
Bent has used coarse language to the railway
employees. Hardly anybody has noticed so
far that Irvine and Co., up to now, only
propose to provide for £332,000 out of a
possible £700,000 shortage, and as the bulk
of the retrenchment proposals can only
extend over about seven months of the year
at best, the actual saving is likely to be much
less than that. If the general election had
been postponed till after next June, the
public would have noticed the fact by
that time, so the Irvine Ministry wisely
applies for a three years’ re-engagement
at once. Its miserably inadequate savings
are only proposed for one year, and
what is to be done next year Irvine
doesn’t say. Probably, he doesn’t know.
Therefore, he is well-advised to ask for a
steady job and a good long lease of office
before the question turns up. And the
shocking problem about meeting the
rest of the deficit—whether by £300,000
or £400,000 of extra taxation, or by
doubling or trebling the present retrench-
ment proposals, or the worse problem about
not meeting the deficit at all, but having,
after all, as large a shortage as Peacock
had, will be fairly before the public in a very
few months. By letting the late Parliament
run its course Irvine might have gone to the
country as the Strong Man who had grappled
with this huge financial spectre and laid it to
rest. He didn’t crave for that honor,
however. He prefers to be sent back with a
solid majority and three years’ breathing
time as the Strong Man who is going to
grapple with the spectre.
* * *
Irvine, by his own showing, is a hideous
financial fraud. According to his own
Treasurer’s figures he will probably, after all
his retrenchments are effected, have as bad a
deficit as that which wrecked Peacock last
year. Unless Shiels’ estimates are a huge
imposture designed to bring discredit on his
predecessor, there is still looming either a
great amount more retrenchment, a great deal
more taxation, or a great smash. If Irvine
hadn’t rushed to the country before Shiels’
budget was delivered he would have had to
take one of these alternatives with him, for
the budget must have gone into figures, and
figures are disastrous to the pint-pot variety
of Government. His vague promise to save
the country by getting a quart out of the
vessel above-mentioned would have gone to
pieces in the light of actual statistics. He
would have appeared either as a shiftless in-
capable, or a man with a startling programme
of new taxation, or a person with a highly
unpopular scheme for wholesale and retail
retrenchment. But if he can secure a reliable
majority now, as the man with the pint pot
in his strong right hand, it. won’t matter so
much when the revelation does come. Irvine
will be comfortably fixed by then.
* * * «P
A month hence, or two months hence,
other awkward things would have happened.
The House of 48 Rich Landlords would have
rejected the Factories Act and the Council re-
form scheme by then, and Irvine would have
been asked onall sides what steps he was going
to take to force the arrogant four dozen Tories
to surrender. If he said he was going to take
no steps at all, his career as an alleged
reformer would be at an end, and he could
no longer cadge for the democratic vote. If
he said he was going to take such measures
as would bring an arrogant and grossly selfish
oligarchy to its knees, he would thereby
break with his Tory following for good, and
throw all his own traditions overboard.
Moreover, he would have to specify the steps
he proposed taking, and that is a matter
which requires the brain of a bigger warrior
than anybody in the Irvine combination.
As things are, he simply promises to send
up these measures, and speaks as if their
acceptance by the Council was a matter of
course. If asked what steps he will take
if they are rejected, he can always say
that it is wrong to assume that the House
of Landlords will resist the will of the people
—just as if the House of Landlords ever did
anything else ; and appeal to the community
not to breed strife by premature threats ;
and assure them vaguely, as the man who is
going to get a quart out of a pint pot, that if
the occasion arises he won’t be found want-
ing. He can say, in a spirit of generosity,
that as the Council didn’t reject the Fac-
tories Act last session, it is wrong to cast
aspersions on that body, which is probably
better than it looks. Also, he can allude to
the spirit of “ British fair play.” Irvine’s
astuteness in arranging to be re-elected
before the battle, as the general who is going
to win, is praiseworthy—in quarters where
that kind of astuteness is appreciated.
* * * *
So Irvine appeals to the Democratic sec-
tion of Victoria as the man who proved his
sincerity by introducing a new Factories
Act and a most valuable measure for the
reform of the Upper House, and who can be
relied upon, by reason of that record, to do
the square thing again. And the Tory sec-
tion, knowingthatit will rejectboth measures,
doesn’t object. He appeals to the Tories
again as the man who doesn’t propose
a general land-tax, and to the Democrats as
a man who has at least made the retrench-
ment scheme less severe than it might be ;
and to all sections as the person who is going
to square the finances without imposing
many new burdens, by making £200,000 or
£300,000 of savings pay off £600,000
or £700,000 of shortage. And he gives his
cordial assurance that he won’t be afraid to
borrow plenty of money, whereby he holds
out to all parties gratifying prospects of a
new boom. It is all a poor, silly little bag
of tricks, but the public is accustomed to
being fooled by such bags and similar con-
tents ; and the prospects of the Irvine
Government coming back with a strong
majority are not at all bad.
• • • •
As a matter of fact, unless a strong and
honest financial genius arises, the prospects
of Victoria are not very far removed from
desperate. It plunged into the Boer war
without making any provision whatever to
cover the extra outlay, and now the war is
over, and the Drayton Grange is back, and
the Jingo enthusiasm is as dead as last year’s
bubonic rat, but the sordid bill remains. It
granted old-age pensions—the one bright
item in its recent political history—without
making any provision to supply the money.
Federation demolished its border customs
revenue, and no one had the courage or
honesty to propose the imposition of new
taxes to replace the ones thus extinguished.
The abolition of the tea duty and a few other
imposts reduced its revenue from foreign
customs, and no one had the courage or
honesty to propose new taxes to make this
loss good either. Under a hideously foolish
Act, passed by a Tory Government a genera-
tion ago, Victoria pays close on £350,000 a
year incivilservice pensions—an outlay which,
in proportion to population, almost equals the
whole military and naval expenditure of
three of the Great Powers of Europe. There
was a deficit last year of £437,000. There
is an anticipated deficit this year of nearly
£700,000, which is not half covered, even
on paper, by the Irvine Government’s
“reform” proposals. The biggest public
asset, the railways, are notoriously going to
wreck and rust for want of sufficient repairs.
Every year a larger amount of loan money
is being used for revenue purposes in order
to disguise the situation, and though, in
this respect, Victoria is not nearly so bad
yet as any of the other States, still its posi-
tion is bad enough. If the Victorians
had the courage to tax themselves as the
English people do, there need be no deficit,
no misappropriation of loan money for
unproductive works, and, with reasonable
economy, no borrowing at all. But it is
the custom here to speak as if Australians
were a species of Chosen People whom it is
sacrilege to expect to pay taxes as other
people do, and a certain carefully-nursed
and petted melancholy and self-pity settles
down on the community when it is expected
to pay its way. Great Britain added 10s.
per head per annum to its previously heavy
taxation during the recent war trouble.
Irvine and Co. have once threatened, in a
far-off way, to add about Is. 2d. per head to
Victoria’s very light taxation to meet the
present crisis, but they added that it was
seriously to be hoped that so stupendous a
calamity would be avoided, and nothing has
since been heard about the subject. In fact,
the present policy of the Irvine Govern-
ment is to get back with a majority that will
make it reasonably safe for three years
before anything very definite is heard about
any subject.
The N.S.W. Opposition.
The change of leadership in the N.S.W. Opposi-
tion is not in itself of very great importance.
Opposition boss Carruthers is only another
Opposition boss Lee, with a perky self-confidence
instead of a solemn self-confidence, and the
Opposition policy will still remain as before—to
get into office somehow, anyhow. But the change
indicates certain tendencies in State politics which
are of great moment. When the consummation
of Federation left N.S.W. Parliament with
diminished powers, it also left it with more free-
dom and more revenue than it ever had 4 before.
The transfer to another arena of the wearisome
fiscal issue, and of other points of govern-
ment, the passing of a tariff which, whilst
it gave a greatly increased revenue to
N.S. Wales, also imparted a very distinct stimulus
to its manufacturing industries —all combined to
give a uniquely favorable opportunity for a State
politician with definitely progressive and yet
economical ideas. The matters still remaining in
State, as differentiated from Federal control,
were, after all, the most vitally important to the
citizen. There was ample scope for progressive work,
and—unique circumstance —there was enough
money for progressive work. As a rule, when the
opportunity is given to a new man to try his hand
at reform, it is because a course of extravagance
and muddle has reduced the finances to an almost
desperate state. But John See had the oppor-
tunity and the money—an increased revenue pro-
vided for him by the Federal tariff which,
properly used, might have worked marvels.
And there are indications that at first John
SEE'had some idea of carrying on upon reform
lines. He broke away definitely from the
disgraceful newspaper bond***' 1 into which Mr.
Bill Lyne had led the Cabinet, and pave soma
promise of shaping as a strong sane man. But
that soon changed. When he was Dibbs’ Trea-
surer, John See had learned the fatal gift of com-
pliance. As Premier, he was the follower rather
than the leader of stronger spirits in his party,
and was soon involved in that career of wild
extravagance which is only now being curbed by
stem necessity.
* * • *
When Premier See felt first the pressure of
various unruly sections in his following, he had an
idea of finding a way to comparative independence
in a coalition. Carrutheks, perhaps the most
representative man on the old Freetrade side of
House, was willing, and a coalition Cabinet
seemed certain until the Freetrade press put a
veto on the proposal. There was ridiculous talk
of “ preserving the fiscal issue ” in State politics,
of “ keeping intact the great Freetrade party ” in
N.S.W. —for what purpose except that it might
be kept under the thumb of the Freetrade papers
it is hard to guess—and confident predictions that
Premier See “would not last two months.”
Because he had favored the coalition proposals
Carruthers was debarred from the leadership of
the alleged “Freetrade” party, and the painfully
ordinary Lee was chosen as its chief. But John
See and Co. kept in. No matter what its mis-
takes or extravagances, the See Cabinet held the
fort. Now the Freetrade press sees that the old
game is up—that it is impossible to keep a great
Freetrade party going which can have no more
effect on Freetrade than the Grand Lama of
Thibet. So Lee resigns, and Carruthers steps
in, and it is officially announced that the fiscal
issue is sunk in the deep, deep sea, with a 10-ton
boulder at its heels to keep it down, and that the
Opposition is willing to welcome Protectionists to
its fold as well as Freetraders.
* • * *
And what next ? It may confidently be ex-
pected that Mr. Carruthers will first make a
strenuous effort to turn Mr. See out, and failing
that, will suggest a coalition. As parties (or
rather groups and sections) are in the N.S.W.
Assembly, a coalition seems to be the certain final
development ; a coalition of the “strong men” of
the Protectionist party—the strong men whose
political muscles are as bulging hills of iron
through the active exercise they have taken in
carrying this country to the pop shop, and the
“ strong men ” of the Freetrade party, who have
similarly-developed sinews. Premier See and his
Owe’Sullivan, with their wide and varied experi-
ence of borrowing and spending, will sit in the
same tent with Carruthers and Brunker, whose
abilities in the same direction are equally well-
known, with hearts that borrow as one and minds
bent upon the same 1.0. U.
• * * ♦
This, in one sense, will be bad for the Labor
Party and the radicals generally in the N.S.W.
Assembly. A coalition, which was independent
of the Labor Party, would not tolerate any dicta-
tion from that quarter. It would count noses care-
fully, and, seeing the safety of such an attitude,
would take on an air of lofty dignity. Not only
would the Labor Party find its proposals for
future labor legislation treated with contempt; it
would probably have to stand by and see much
that has been already gained jettisoned. To that
extent the coalition would be a bad thing for the
Radical cause in State politics—the cause which is
to an extent (though not to anything like a satis-
factory extent) represented by the Labor party.
But in other directions there would be a great
gain. The road would be cleared for the forma-
tion of a genuine Radical reform party—an
organisation without a single blank I 0 U in its
pocket-book, or a single concession swindle hidden
in its coat-tail. The Labor party could come
forward and fill the gap. It could do so. Perhaps
it would do so ; perhaps not. But certainly some-
body would ; for the instinct of honesty is not
dead, even in Parliament. This much would be
secured. All the old extravagant mob, the fakirs
of finance, the pawnshop brigade, would be
gathered under one banner. Hitherto they have
been ranged under two banners and alternately
held office, the one in opposition always yelling
dismally at the dishonesty of the one in power,
and then, when brought by a turn of the wheel
on to the Treasury benches, continuing the same
dishonest practices.
* * * *
The Labor Party in N.S.W. has in the past
been a sort of condottiere force, selling its support
to the one gang or the other for concessions of
labor legislation, and foolishly neglecting to insist
that these concessions should be made financially
stable. (When the coalition comes it will very
possibly find that the State “ cannot afford ” many
of these hardly-wrung measures and the Labor
Party will see the vanity then of neglecting the
financial side of reform). In pursuit of this
policy it has held the candle for some strange
devils, and assisted much wicked wantonness of
administration. With a coalition it will have no
more excuse for supporting evil that good
may come and it will be forced to defin-
itely declare for economy and reform, or to
sink its identity entirely in the mass of the
coalition’s followers. Whatever the decision of
the Labor Party, the State would benefit in a little
while by the formation of some party with honesty
as its platform. The Bulletin would welcome a
coalition as defining the issues and giving one neck
to all the old extravagance, ineptitude and corrup-
tion —and it feels inclined, therefore, to view with
a kindly eye Carruthers’ accession to his new
dignity.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Next Wednesday.
The Victorian general election, which eventuates
next Wednesday, is really the first skirmish in
what will be a long and probably bitter fight. To
those who can read between the lines, and who
can gauge the value of the names behind the move-
ment, the Irvine “Reform” platform is merely
one step in a clearly-defined, well-arranged con-
spiracy to bear down wages, _ to undo existing
labor legislation, and to solidify the position of
the utterly nefarious House of Landlords. Backed
by the hard, unscrupulous lying of the Melbourne
—and most of the country—press, and by the
more insidious methods of suppress, the Victorian
Tory party has already got rid of the hated Fac-
tories Act, and is, if the electors allow themselves
to be dragged into it, in a fair way to get a reduced
Parliament with abnormal country representation,
in which the unprogressive squatter and cockie will
run the show. Public service reduction is onedetail,
the further cutting down of the starved Education
system (on which Robert Reid had already begun
to try his hand) is another. And to carry out this
programme the electors are being shamelessly
bamboozled. Premier Irvine promises a resusci-
tated Factories Act, Constitutional Reform and a
double dissolution with a Parliament elected on
the new basis, all inside twelve months. So a
semi-sober individual promises to eat his hat, or a
small boy bets a thousand pounds, or Parkes


promised local government! Irvine is really a
marionette in the hands of those behind the
Kyabram ghost. This much for the honesty of
Irvine’s intentions re tlie Factories Act:
Cameron, Shiels and the unspeakable Bent have
sat as a committee to choose the Government
candidate in each electorate, and they have in-
variably chosen the nominee of the Citizens’
Reform League—and the nominee of the
Citizens’ Reform League, in practically
every instance, was, till just now, a savage
opponent of the Factories Act and to every kind
of progressive legislation. Nearly a hundred
sudden and temporary converts are to follow
Irvine in his campaign, Irvine himself being the
suddenest and most. temporary convert of the
crowd, and Bent hardly pretending to be even
that. And, on the other side, Peacock, who
ought to be leading the Liberal Party, has appar-
ently lost his head, and is laughing incoherently
in odd corners of the State ; and the Labor Party
is the only real force left to oppose Kyabram and
co. The one thing that any good Victorian can
do now is to avoid the Age candidate, and the
Argus candidate, and the Irvine candidate as
much as possible; to vote the Labor ticket where
there is one; to vote the Peacock ticket where
there is one, and where there is no direct Labor can-
didate ; to do whatever he can to unite the scat-
tered forces of Democracy ; and to swear off the
Kyabram conspiracy and all its works.

The Tendency of Coal-ships to Founder.
Many people will be much comforted in mind at
the decision of the N.S.W. Court of Marine
Inquiry that all was right as right could be with
the steamer Quiraing, which went out from New-
castle (N.S.W.) with a big load of coal, and
shortly afterwards went to the bottom with all
souls. The Court found that she was fitted all
right, manned all right, loaded all right, in the
very pink of condition, as safe as safe could be,
and her loss was merely one of the freaks of
Fate. (The fact that this kind of prank is more
often played on coal-ships than on other classes of
vessels is merely owing to the inexplicable work-
ings of Chance.) It is true that at the Marine
Court Inquiry several persons (merely common
seamen, however) gave sworn evidence that they
considered the vessel unsafe; in some eases they
had left their jobs on her because they feared that
she was not seaworthy. To the general public
this evidence (even though it was contradicted by
various officers) would at least suggest a suspicion
in regard to the boat. Sailors are riot, as a rule,
timid, nervous persons, and jobs are so hard to get
novVadays that they are not thrown up causelessly.
But to the N.S.W. Court of Marine Inquiry it
was “quite evident” thattheQuiraing was perfectly
safe. And it is not going beyond the mark to say
that it is quite evident to the N.S.W. public that
our system of safeguarding the sailor’s life, from
Marine Board Inspection to Marine Court In-
quiry, is perilously close to being farcical. Inspec-
tion prevents nothing, inquiry punishes or con-
demns nothing, and the sacrifice of human life
continues. Admitted that there will always be
accidents at sea, which no human thought can
prevent, what puts the present system most dis-
tinctly on trial is that a certain class of boats is so
specially chosen for the blows of Calamity. The
passenger vessel doesn’t founder with all hands
with a fraction of the frequency that the
coal vessel does. With equal precautions the
risk should be equal. But most certainly the
precautions are not equal; and that is where there
is need for remedy. The exact conditions govern-
ing the inspection, &c., of vessels which do not
carry passengers are not, of course, divulged by
Marine Board Inspectors, Courts of Marine
Inquiry, &c. If set down nakedly they would
probably look too nearly like an approach to homi-
cide. .Some idea of them may be gathered from
the experiences of the steamer Balmain, recently
condemned as unsea worthy at Auckland :
The steamer took a cargo of coal from Newcastle
(N.S.W.) to Gisborne, on discharge of which she proceeded
to Whangarei and took in a supply of bunker coal pre-
paratory to leaving for Kaipara to load timber for Sydney.
It was then discovered that the boiler tubes were leaking
so badly that the engineers would not take the risk of
proceeding to sea in the steamer before proper repairs
were effected, and consequently the Balmain came down
to Auckland for that purpose. After the engines and
boiler had been fixed up to the satisfaction of the Govt.
Inspector of Machinery, the steamer’s clearance papers
were granted by the Customs, and she was ready to leave
for Kaipara. The crew, however, then stepped in and
demanded a survey of the hull. A preliminary survey
showed that one of the plates in the steamer’s stern was
in a very bad state ; in fact, so bad that the inspector at
once decided that the steamer must be docked, so that a
oomplete survey inside and outside should be made. The
Balmain was floated into dock on August 8, and the sur-
veyors, after a thorough investigation, decided that the
crew’s complaint was justified, and have reported to the
Marine Department that the steamer is unseaworthy.
One is not justified in deducing a general rule
from a single example ; else it would be clear that
the only method by which a crazy cargo boat can
be made seaworthy is a strike of the crew, and
that so long as the crew is too hungry or too
desperate to strike the boat goes to sea unchecked.
That possibly is not the general rule; it is
evidently the case sometimes. Apparently,
judged by the Balmain case, a cargo vessel can
wander about the Australian coast in an utterly
worn-out state without the marine authorities
having the least knowledge or suspicion of the
matter. If the Balmain had sunk on its way to
Maoriland it would seem that the marine authori-
ties could have truthfully sworn, in its case also,
that it had left this side, so far as they knew, in a
perfectly sound state.
India.
Bombay meteorological department predicts a
bad season over a considerable part of India, and
another famine. All things are comparative, of
course, and a bad season in India would be re-
garded as a dazzlingly good one in Australia—but
tliat is a side issue. In a European sense, India
is a thinly-peopled country —far more thinly-
peopled than Austria-Hungary, Belgium, France,
Germany, Italy, Holland, Switzerland, England
—in fact, its population is only a quarter
the density of that which lives and thrives in some
prosperous European countries. It is about half
as thinly peopled as Japan, which, though barren
by comparison with India, is prosperous, and
f rowing rich, and which doesn’t have famines.
ndia is a highly fertile country, with a good
average rainfall a country which grows,
even in a bad year, more food than it wants
for its own use. _ The difference between
it and Japan lies in the fact that India
is under British rule and Japan isn’t—that India,
under British Freetrade ideas, has lost its manu-
factures, and been driven back to the “great
natural industries,” as M'Millan call them, while
Japan is a manufacturing state—and that India’s
banks, railways, debt, and financial institutions
are owned in England, while Japan’s are not.
Indian famines arise from the fact that the crops
have failed in some district or districts, and the
beggared populace, where a man’s average earn-
ings are barely three-farthings a day in good
times, has no money to buy the food that grows
abundantly in other provinces. Since Indiabecame
British territory, it has been covered with rail-
ways, built largely by English companies, to
which a certain rate of interest is guaranteed ; so
the people are taxed to make up the interest, and
the taxes go to England. India is taxed to pay
pensions for innumerable ex-officials resident in
England. It is taxed to pay for wars in Afghanis-
tan and elsewhere. Its banking profits go to
England. Its local shipping—once very exten-
sive—has been practically absorbed by England.
Its high officials are all English, and take their
savings to England. And it isn’t allowed to do
anything to prevent this grabbing of everything
by England. India now has to pay England, in
one form of tribute and another, some thirty
millions a year. It was first plundered by the
English East India Co. and its satellites to an
extent commonly estimated at 500 to 1000 million
pounds—plundered to such an extent that almost
the whole wealth and manufactures and commerce
and banking of England date from that great
steal. Then it was called upon to pay enormous
interest for the loan of a little of its own
money. Its manufactures were destroyed by
the English Freetrade system, and it has to
pay its tribute mostly in agricultural produce, for
almost its whole population has been forced on to
the land. After the tribute is paid there is barely
enough left to Jive upon in good years, and in bad
years there isn’t enough. For in the worst famine
year the English creditors demand their interest,
the English officials their pensions and salaries,
the English railway co’s. their guaranteed divi-
dends, and enough produce must be exported to
pay these, no matter who starves. In the days of
native rule the surplus grain of an extra good year
remained in the country, so that there was still
abundance in the next bad year ; now the surplus
goes to England to pay interest on foreign debt or
profits to absentee capitalists, and when there
is no surplus, then the necessary food of
the people goes. In the first quarter of
the 19th century, when India was mostly under
native rule, there were five famines, each covering
a very small area. They were mostly caused by
war, and the deaths from hunger were about a
million. In the last quarter of the 19th century,
when India was all under British influence, and
when there was no war, there were eighteen
famines, most of them spread over a large area.
Among these eighteen were included the four
worst famines in Indian history, and in twenty-five
years one-tenth of the population (or 26 millions)
died from hunger. The visible causes are Free-
trade, the destruction of native industries to
make room for imports, and the influx of foreign
capital—in other words, the growth of the absen-
tee creditor and the consequent drain of interest.
India to-day, with famine as a yearly visitant
instead of an occasional horror, is just what
George Reid and William M‘Millan and Cook
and Pumpkin Smith and Conroy and Jos. Symon
are trying to make of Australia.

The Rio Log'e Kanaka.
The Rio Loge Kanaka hoax has fallen through.
When this vessel was about to start for the islands
with a few tons of time-expired Kanakas, Premier
Philp wept tears of great bitterness over a fancy
picture, drawn by himself, of the returned Kanaka
landing only to be butchered by his savage rela-
tives —the ones the missionary has been con-
verting for the last 50 years. Philp pictured
the nigger imploring the captain to take him
back to Queensland, and the captain not able
to do it because of the alien restriction laws, and a
good Christian Kanaka being forced ashore to
certain death because of Barton, and his blood
being on the head of Deakin. Philp wrote letters
about it in the name of “ common humanity ” and
such-like moral sludge, and when Deakin asked
why the Philp crowd, which had for 30 years
been throwing the islander ashore to be killed
by any hostile nigger, became so anxious
about him notv, it was resolved in cheap
nigger circles that Deakin was without bowels
of compassion. At last the Rio Loge got away—
and landed all its niggers without any trouble.
The savage relatives waiting on the shore to
butcher them were a myth, and the necessity for
bringipg them back for their own safety was a
myth, and all the Philp snuffle was a myth. The
vessel returned to report that it had a quiet
voyage, and left its time-expired islander on
his peaceful isle where the ameliorating influences
of the gospel are spread around by the local mis-
sionary, and it found the collection going on as
usual. All the same the Philp Government will
bring along its next next anti-Federal lie just as
if it hadn’t been bowled out over this one.

The Federal Capital.
Seldom does a week pass now without the savagely
parochial section of the Sydney press—that sec-
tion (principally the Telegraph) which loathes
the democratic composition of the Federal Par-
liament, which acts as commercial and political
pimp to the gang of absentee importers, and
hates the Federal Government’s policy of en-
couraging Australian industries to the detriment
of its shoddy friends, and which is raving mad at
the prospect of having to pay postage instead of
cadging on the community—publishing a jaun-
diced article with the above heading. The capital
is its stock parochial lie and howl nowadays. The
terms of the Federal compact are that a Federal
city is to be established in N.S. Wales, at a
distance of 100 miles or more from Sydney. It
is to remain there for all time, and there is an
awful stretch of long and dreary time ahead—4o,ooo
years may only see it fairly begun. Till the new
capital is fixed the Parliament is to meet in
Melbourne. These terms were only put in the
Constitution after N.S. W.’s Freetrade politicians
had, by Act of Parliament, nullified the first
Referendum vote—the one whereby the people
accepted Federation and expressed their willing-
ness to come in on the same terms as the other
States. If they hadn’t nullified that vote, the
Parliament would probably be sitting at Sydney
to-day, and Sydney would probably be the
permanent Federal Capital to-day. Sydney
was barred simply because N.S. W.’s Free-
trade politicians first of all insisted on bar-
ring all the other capitals. The terms were
special ones, negotiated for by N.S.W. Freetrade
politicians, and the other States conceded them to
the spoilt child of the Union. No person in his
senses, however, expected that the new Federal
capital could be constructed on the instant. It
had to be selected ; the area decided upon; the
Federal Parliament and the N.S.W. State Parlia-
ment had to pass the necessary enactmeuts.
Above all things, the money had to be found, and
before any large financial proceedings of that
kind could be effected the tariff had to be
passed, so that the Federal Parliament might
know what money it had. And every time
an attempt was made to pass a few extra
clauses of the tariff and make a little extra pro-
gress, the Telegraph’s own M‘Millan and
Pumpkin Smith arranged a stonewall, and the
Telegraph held forth proudly next day about
how the effectiveness of their stonewalling tactics
had disconcerted Kingston and made progress
impossible. The Telegraph itself has offered no
plan for raising the necessary money for construct-
ing the Federal capital, and the only plan offered
—that of George Turner, who suggested finding
the necessary funds by the adoption of the
Canadian banking system—is bitterly denounced
in its columns. It has fought savagely against the
selection of the site which its own special com-
missioner reported to be far the best—Monaro ;
and thus helped to prevent any progress.
And so 15 months—nearly one session—have
gone by without a great Federal city being
built when there was no money to build it with.
About one-thirtieth of the time that N.S.W. has
spent considering its city railway, and its North
Shore bridge, and its Local Government Bill, has
elapsed without this much bigger work being done.
And to the Telegraph’s diseased intelligence,
warped by the prospect of Australian industries
springing up, and by the fear of not being allowed
any longer to cadge on the poor for its gratis
postage and its subsidised newspaper trains, the
fact that no Federal city has been built in 15
months is evidence of a deep and wide-spread
conspiracy too horrible to mention. The bulk of
the people of New South Wales take no notice of
the jaundiced and impossible ravings of their
silly journal, but it has the effrontery to declare
that its attitude—the attitude of a soured spinster
squabbling at a meeting of the Old Cat Society—
is the attitude of New South Wales. And that is
an assumption which needs to be vigorously
refuted. New South Wales isn’t all soured old cat.

The Vindication of Lyne and See.
Australia is just now a little more financially
discredited than usual, and N.S.W.’s debt in par-
ticular has almost touched the monumental figure
of £75,000,000, while the need for a few more
millions is very urgent indeed. In these circum-
stances there has been an indignant rallying up of
worn-out public daddoeks, and Ltne and See and
O’Sullivan got up almost simultaneously to ask
what there was to complain about. Some speci-
mens follow:
Mr. Lyne said that when he read and heard statements
about the terrible debt and insolvency of the State he felt
angry that they should be made, and felt sorry that they
should fall on the shoulders of Mr. O’Sullivan, who was
the best Minister for Works this State ever had. The
statements about the indebtedness wer' untrue, and it
was not necessary to cry out about s.. .iding money,
seeing that there was no practical debt, a.:.T nothing like
the debt of Great Britain.
Mr. O’Sullivan said there had been no waste of money
in New South Wales, and he intended to carry out other
public works such as railways, artesian bores, sewerage
works, etc. He 1 contended that the present Government
had saved the State. They had the plague and other
calamities to contend with, and fought manfully against
such odds and came out on top.
Mr. See : Is there any one act that the Government has
done during the whole of its administration that any man
dare undo if he had a chance? Bare those braves who
stand up and indirectly condemn me day by day,
challenge me in my place in Parliament with having done
one specific act which would warrant Parliament in
putting me out of office ? I throw down that challenge 1
Let them come forward with a specific charge of extrava-
gance in regard to public works which were carried out
with the support of Parliament. Then why all this
sophistry and platitude about the indebtedness of New
South Wales ? Since the inception of Responsible Govern-
ment, every shilling of money spent on public works is
money in the main well spent, and to talk about our
national debt as though we were overburdened by it is
merely the claptrap of those who do not understand. I
say that we should measure our national debt by our
national assets, and if some great syndicate, such as
Pierpont Morgan’s, could buy our national assets on the
basis of 25 per cent, advance on what they have cost,
would not they be glad to get them !
Truly the valor of these men is appalling ! Yet
John See, who is so anxious to have just one
definite charge brought against him so that he
may cast the fragments of it back in the accuser’s
teeth, knows that his Government has been
specifically accused a score of times (it is hereby
accused over again to make extra sure) of about the
meanest and dirtiest political swindle and account-
faking in N.S.W. history—the appropriation as
revenue of the rents of unpaid-for properties in
the resumed area of Sydney, while the correspond-
ing liabilities were charged to loans. And appar-
ently wild horses won’t drag the skulking See
Government out of its hole or orifice or barrel to
answer the charge. Even when the matter was
alluded to by the Auditor-General there was no
open reply, though certainly a Bill came crab-
fashion and sideways into Parliament, which Bill
proposed to give the Ministry certain powers of
suppressing the Auditor-General in future. Also,
no one should know better than See that no
Morgan syndicate would be atall likely to buy this
country’s public works at even half what they cost,
unless some very large extra powers of fleecing the
public were thrown in. Men like Morgan don’t,
as a rule, invest their money at If or 2 per cent.,
which is what John See’s silly proposition
amounts to. This habit of asserting that Aus-
tralia isn’t too deep in debt because See or Dreary
Bill Lyne is prepared to give his unsupported
assurance that parties unknown would gladly buy
our public works at more than they cost, is more
than a trifle wearisome. The alleged parties never
turn up. Lyne and See know no more about
what Morgan, whom they probably never saw,
would do, than anybody else knows, and
though their assurance about his readiness
to buy a job lot of gaols and things at
more than first cost is worth, if genuine, as
much as (and no more than) anybody else’s
opinion, they are interested parties, and they are
accusing a very hard-headed speculator of flat
lunacy. In any case, as Australia can’t sell its
roads and gaols and harbors to Morgan, even if
he wanted them, the subject isn’t worth talking
about.
