Parson Dill’s Crusade.
Pastor Dill Macky, of Sydney, the shockingly
credulous, kind-hearted, vain, anti-Rome crank,
who can be imposed upon by any escaped nun or
other crude conspirator, announces to the press
that funds have been raised to send him on a lec-
turing tour throughout N.S. Wales, in order that
he may stir up sectarian bitterness and organise
the “ Protestant vote ” for the next general elec-
tion. And, as a token of great gladness, Pastor
Dill Macky’s solemn Scotch house on Church-
hill is now a-flutter with gaudy streamers, and the
Scarlet Woman is in for a bad time. The Bulle-
tin gives Mr. Macky credit for really believing
that there is a danger of Rome capturing Australia,
and getting up an Inquisition, and putting the
issue to him straight to eat fish on Fridays or
take the consequences. The pity of it is that
men of otherwise good character should be found
so silly as to lend themselves to the altogether
disgraceful work of stirring up religious hatreds
and making Christianity a matter of malice, envy,
and uncharitableness. The plea that the Roman
Catholics of the State are organised for political
purposes is palpably an absurd one ; no one can
give the address of such an organisation, or its
officers, or its working hours, or any definite
basis on which to ground a belief in its
existenceand, in mundane affairs at any
rate, that thing isn’t which you can’t prove is
Furthermore, at the Federal Convention elections’
when the chance was given to Roman Catholics
to show that they were influenced by church ideas
in politics, through the candidature of Cardinal
Moran, they refused to be sufficiently
attached to their creed, in a political sense,
to give the head of it even a tenth of their
franchises. If every R.C. had given one vote
of his ten to Cardinal Moran, that gentle-
man would have headed the poll ; but he
failed to get even a place among the first
dozen candidates. That would prove to any one
not blinded by bigotry that “the origanised R.C.
vote ”is a myth. Yet the Protestant Defence
Association is to be formed to counteract that
“organised vote” and to make the blackguarding
of one’s fellow-Christian the chief issue in politics.
If the blackguarding were all, The Bulletin
would be quite content. The things one sect says
about another are, in themselves, indecencies of
very small importance. The trouble is that when
politics begin to turn on the sectarian issue the
man who knows something of our finances, and
wants to administer them honestly, is shunted
aside to make room for the man who can curse his
fellow-Christian in a louder key; and a capacity
for being indignant against dead Popes brings
men into charge of Public Works, Lands, Rail-
ways, and other things they know nothing about.
When some fool Treasurer loses the country half-
a-million over some loan bungle or some badly-
devised railway it is poor consolation to know
that he is such a good DiLL-MACKY-ite that
he can’t sleep at night for his rage against the Scar-
let Woman. Any individual who urges the electors
to choose its members on religious grounds—any
religious grounds—instead of on their political
cap .city and honesty is a traitor to the country.
It may not matter to Parson Dill if the ill-
governed State slithers down a great precipice'
into the sea of bankruptcy. Prob ibly he knows
so little about mundane politics that he wouldn’t
know what bad government meant if he found it
lying with a label on it in his collection plate.
Possibly he doesn’t care what happens to the State
so long as his own private grudge and bigotry are
satisfied. But all people are not so constituted.
Just now a Government that will.abolish foreign
loans is worth moi-e to N.S.W. than one that will
curse the Scarlet Woman or any other alleged red
female for 40,000 years, and the scheme for dis-
tracting attention from the country’s dreadful
financial position by throwing bricks at dead and
gone images is wholly mis-timed.
♦ ;
The policy on which Premier Irvine goes to the
Victorian electors is, word for word, the same
vague twaddle on which every other Australian
Premier goes nowadays. This is it in his own
words:
To reform che Constitution in such a manner as to
secure simplicity and economy in the working of Parlia-
ment, and to increase the efficiency and representative
character of both Houses.
To effect permanent economies throughout the whole
domain of Government expenditure, with the view of
enabling a larger share of the proceeds of taxation to go
towards opening up and developing the resources of the
State.
To proceed with prudence, but without timidity, in the
construction of such public works as will enure to the
permanent benefit of the people by increasing the wealth-
producing capacity of the country.
The skeleton in every case consists of about ten
words: “prudence,” “resources,” “develop,”
“economy,” “country,” “judicious,” “public
works,” “opening up,” are all that are really
wanted. Then the flesh of verbiage is put on the
bones of repetition, and the grin of tautology on
the jaws of platitude and the thing is done.
The man who imported to Australia that phrase
“ developing the resources of the country ” brought
in a worse curse than the rabbit. It is the excuse
for big loans, deficits, thriftlessness, waste, and
log-rolling; it covers all manner of steals ; and it
is the whole mental stock-in-trade of stodgy,
tenth-rate hangers-on to the tail of politics.
There are at least 100 dull mediocrities in Austra-
lian politics who would never have got there if
they hadn’t disguised their mental barrenness by
saying they believed in developing the resources
of the country.
Victorian Minister for Railways, Thos. Bent,
opened his mouth at Nhill last week to say this :
Premier Peacock had brought in the Shops and
Factories Act, which had been the ruin of the community,
and he had done everything possible in it to make it
impossible for certain men to live at all.
And the Irvine Government, of which the un-
speakable Bent is so fitting a member, was all the
while strenuously declaring that its first purpose
was to re-enact this same Shops and Factories
Act. Truly a “strong” Government—a Govern-
ment no sane citizen would get to leeward of.

The Tasmanian Ministry, though kept pretty
busy dodging no-confidence motions, has lately
found time to introduce a new Sale of Liquor Bill,
which contains provisions dealing with the tied-
house trouble. As the Cascade Brewery Co. has
got 90 per cent, of Hobart pubs, securely leg-
roped, the trouble in that dreary village is alleged
to be of considerable dimensions. The “untying”
clauses are on Maoriland lines, and as brewer
Tattersall doesn’t tie his own pubs, and objects
to any other brewer doing so, the proposal is likely
to become law. All bonds, agreements, pro.
notes, cheques, etc., entered into for pub.-tying
purposes are to be declared void, and the brewer
and publican wishing to arrange matters will need
to do so on the nod. And a nod, which is the
thing that a blind horse is said to find as good as a
wink, can’t be put in a bank safe as security.
Maoriland Farmers’ Union persistently barracks
for the freehold tenure as against the lease in
perpetuity. Probably for the occupier of the soil,
once he has paid for the soil, the fee simple is the
best. But thousands of him never manage to pay
for it, and they mortgage and re-mortgage and
spend a dreary life working off a dead horse.
What they pay in interest alone would, very often,
have paid the rent for a life time on the lease in
perpetuity, and the original capital and borrowed
principal been available for stocking and working
funds. Also, the whole community has an interest
in the land. There is no reason why the land-
robbers of Hawkes Bay, for instance, who obtained
thousands of acres of lovely country in return for
a few red blankets, or for the honor done to some
Maori chief by taking his best-looking daughter as
a temporary tart, should be allowed to escape scot
free, when clean, honest white men who came
after have to pay rent for ever and ever. Maori-
land has found it necessary for the national wel-
fare to buy out, at the cost of hundreds of thou-
sands of pounds, estates, considerable portions of
some of which were obtained in return for a few
scores of red blankets, or a few years living with
half-naked, savage brown women, some of whom
had eaten human flesh. The most persistent bar-
racking for the freehold system comes from some
of. the eminently respected gentlemen who ob-
tained their fee simple by those quaint old devices.

“ Mrs. Goldstein, ”by John F. Sheridan
and Pat Finn (music by Weierter), produced at
Sydney Criterion last Saturday, is a slight and
uneven production, over which the melancholy
mother-in-law joke holds full sway. It opens in
a stylish drawing-room, where a large number of
nice girly boys are saying good-night to Arthur on
general principles. Later on, Celia Mavis, who is
married to Arthur (otherwise Charles Kenning-
ham), requests that Ma shall be asked down to see
the Melbourne Cup, which Arthur refuses, grace-
fully distracting her mind from the subject by a
duet. The act is taken up with more requests
from Celia for Ma, and more solos or duets by
Way of changing the subject. The orchestra at
this epoch gets excited, and expresses its views on
mothers-in-law in general by some storm music.
As a consequence of all this, Celia and Heba
Barlow, who is a neat Yankee girl in bloomers
and an American accent, plot together to intro-
duce ma-in-law under another name, which
results in the advent of John Sheridan as a
middle - aged lady in evening dress. Sheri-
dan, disguised as a mother-in-law who is dis-
guised as someone else, happens for awhile in a
numerous sort of way, just as John has happened
in so many other dresses before, and the comedy
ends up at the Flemington races, which are mostly
cinematograph, though a calm, live horse appears
at the end as an indication that Arthur has won
the race. The race has taken place, according to
the cinematograph, in a sudden burst of darkness.
• * * »
The comedy badly wants filling-out. It
needs to take food and drink and strengthening
medicine, and then to wait behind the scenes till
it has grown up a little. Minnie Hooper’s grace-
ful dancing does good service, and the jockeys,
johnnies and bookies who gyrate at Flemington
can safely be commended; but the piece stops
with a jerk, like a horse that has come suddenly
against a wall in the dark, and the performance is
over so soon that the gallery is entitled to ask
threepence worth of its money back on that count
only. There are few catchy tunes in the produc-
tion, nothing the audience can take away with it
—not even the umbrella left behind by the lady
who ran for the last tram, because the audience
can catch the second last tram quite easily. John
F. Sheridan looks quite himself again in gorgeous
female raiment, and when he assents to the pro-
position that it might be better for the world if
there were more women like him, the gallery
takes the observation in good part. The delight
of Arthur on finding that this bright and loud-
voiced lady is his dreaded relative seems rather
overdone. Mrs. Goldstein impresses you as the
sort of mother-in-law it would be well to give to a
friend, and visit occasionally. Charles Kenning-
ham, who is in good voice, manages to put a little
reality into the legend of the man who is afraid of
his wife’s parent, but it is a hard struggle. Miss
Cecilia Mavis is not always at her best, her voice
suffering occasionally from that tired feeling.
Miss Heba Barlow does her little Yankee part
successfully, though the bloomer-clad American
girl with the twang is another aged joke. The
comedy relies a good deal on its racing flavor and
its view of Flemington.

“The Chinese Honeymoon” is expected
to end its career at Sydney Royal early next
week. Considering the present disturbed state of
China, the management might have worked up a
second edition wherein a Boxer uprising slew the
mother-in-law and scattered her monkey and the
admiral’s whiskers to the four winds of heaven,
but the opportunity has been missed. The Royal
advertisement retreats in good order, stating to
the last that the “Chinese Honeymoon” is
“ the most charming and romantic comic-
opera of the century.” When an adver-
tisement departs from the solid truth it may
as well depart with all its might, and the
above statement has some points about it.
At the same time it would be interesting to know
what view the Full Court would take if somebody
sued for the return of his ss. on the ground that
he paid for the most charming comic opera of the
century and the article wasn’t delivered. The
Bulletin would like to hear G. H. Reid for the
plaintiff holding forth on the necessity of being
strictly accurate in everything.
** # *
“ The Belle of New York,” as interpreted
by Williamson’s Musical Comedy co., at Sydney
Palace, is a more cheerful lady now than on the
opening night. With more confidence on the
part of the players, there is an all-round brighten-
ing of the performance. The audience is given a
clearer impression of the queen of comic opera,
who, having been married nine times, guesses she
understands the etiquette of the business ; of the
President of the Cohoes Moral Regeneration
Society, who believes in keeping the young folks
out of mischief so that the old folks can get
into all the mischief that is available; and
of the other bizarre characters. Chorus and
orchestra do not show any noticeable improvement,
because they reached high-water mark from the
first in what is really very difficult work. On the
whole Sydney Palace is a bright haven of rest and
amusement just now. There is not a decrepit
joke or a mother-in-law allusion in the whole
course of three hours’ fun.
* * * *
Sydney Tivoli put up an almost completely
new programme last week—the new-comers being
Wallace Brownlow, the Wilton Bros., Dora
Taylor, Tod Calloway, the Nawns, and the Mon-
troses —and not a weak “ turn ” amongst them.
Mr. Brownlow is in excellent voice just now, and
his two songs, “Gunga Din” and “Mandalay,”
though set to a very poor accompaniment,, show
his soft baritone to good advantage. Miss Dora
Taylor is a dancer of the “classic” type, and
trips daintily on her ten commendable
toes. Tod Calloway, a Sydney-grown come-
dian, returns from London-with a novel palaeo-
lithic-man song, which has a good deal of
humor. The wonderful acrobatic business of the
Montroses suffers from the fact that practically
all the novelty of it has been worn off by the
Craggs and the Florenz Family. The Wilton
Bros., however, contribute a turn which “caught
on ” at once and which hasn’t been forestalled by
previous humorists. One of the Wiltons is a
rather meritorious clown, and both do astonishing
things on the horizontal bar. Mr. and Mrs. Tom
Nawn’s Irish-comedy sketch is of somewhat the
same character as that of Callahan and Mack—not
a howling burlesque of the Hibernian in his home,
but a well-studied, quietly-humorous bit of life.
The sketch is set in a very charming Irish land-
scape ; and Mrs. Nawn is a colleen with a fine
pair of eyes.
# * * *
Bland Holt’s “ Riding to Win,” with the
real water and the real dog and other accessories
after the fact, continues to interest a large section
of the Sydney public which loves its melodrama
much mixed. The title of this Lyceum drama
doesn’t do any work till the last act, when the
scenery rushes past Baker on his way to find that
the darkest hour is just before the dawn. This is
an improvement, however, on the previous play
over which the Colors billed as Flying did not fly
at all, or else flew to such a limited extent that
they passed unnoticed.
* * # *
Mrs. G. B. Lewis has arranged to celebrate
her professional ,Tubilee with a benefit performance
at Melb. Royal on Friday, Oct. 3, the night before
Maggie Moore and co. come to hand. The show
starts at 7 30, and sundry things are £oing to
happen. “ Ingomar,” played for the chief parts
by Mrs. G. B. L.’s pupils, is the principal item on
the bill, and this will bring forth Miss Unie
Russell as the early Greek damsel who lures the
hairy barbarian into respectable society. Miss
Russell can’t help looking and speaking the part
of Parthenia angelically, furthermore she is the
brightest and Greekest amateur actress in
Melbourne. Mrs. Lewis is to jubilee as Peg
Woffington in a scene from “Masks and Faces,”
and the interesting occasion will conclude with a
drawing-room reception on the stage under the
management of a general committee. The list of
the committee, beginning with George Coppin, is
about a yard long. Coppin is one of the few
people who know for certain that G. B. W.
Lewis’s well preserved wife is old enough to
celebrate a 50th anniversary of her theatrical
debut.
* * * *
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) the well-built
drama of the superhuman ’tec and the baffled
Moriarty has fairly got public attention
under arrest. “Sherlock Holmes” is a lurid,
fascinating fiction that grabs a scoffer by the neck
and shakes him into an acknowledgment of its
merits. It suggests any number of policemen
behind the scenes, yet no stereotyped member of
the Foorce obtrudes his blue uniform and regula-
tion boots into the trouble. Even when “the
Inspector himself” is stated to be downstairs
capturing criminals on the strength of information
received, and supplying material for puff para-
graphs in the evening papers, the dramatist
spares the audience the pang of beholding
that familiar friend once again. Sherlock
is the only agent of the Law who appears
in the play, and he differs from all previous
stage detectives in most respects. Some-
times he might be mistaken for a long, lean,
intellectual undertaker looking for a lost corpse
in his sleep, but, as a rule, he is a companionable
wizard addicted to tobacco. He has an amiable
side to his character. He stop to analyse
his reasons for falling in love with the queer,
tearful Trilby girl who possesses the good old
Papers. He just falls right there, and is left to
make his analysis after marriage. Also, he reveals
a kind feeling for his remarkable boy-in-buttons.
» * * *
Sherlock is made of softer stuff than Pro-
fessor Moriarty. The Prof., as Athol wood plays
him (with greatly improved elocution) is * a
Moriarty of 65 or so, and these bad old men are
tough as crocodiles. Anyone who plays tricks on
the Professor is immediately confided to one
Craigin for removal. Craigin (Augustus Glover)
enjoys his employer's complete confidence. When-
ever a little job of killing is required, the Prof, puts
his lips to the tube and says, “ Craigin for that."
Nevertheless, the chief executioner fails miserably
the only time the audience sees him at work. He
ought to hide in the ante-room where the girl has
been planted, and bang Sherlock over the head
when he tracks the damsel to her lair. But
Craigin fools about in the open until his intended
victim knocks the lamp over. Owing to his absurd
procrastination and lack of slimness, Craigin is
arrested before he can get the blood of Sherlock
on his soul. All things considered, he deserves
about 75 years’ penal servitude, with extras.
* * * *
Other Melbourne shows were beginning to
show signs of age last Saturday night. “The
Lady of Ostend ” had grown old enough to
announce her approaching retirement from the
Bijou, in favor of “Tom, Dick and Harry.”
Wednesday of this week was the date appointed
for the new bill to come into force, so this journal
assumes that the three strawberry blonde
facsimiles aforesaid have already become involved
in misunderstandings with three young members
of the opposite sex. “Tom, Dick and Harry” is
a farce which cuts its confusion pretty thick, but
is essentially harmless, which last remark may be
taken to mean that none of the red-headed men
does anything calculated to bring the color of his
hair to modesty’s cheek.
* * * *
At the Opera House there is a further
revival of the Florenz Troupe, and a first appear-
ance of Mark Anthony, a stranger of humorous
tendencies. Also, Fanny Powers—not so little as
she used to be—is assisting to fill in the space
which isn’t set apart for Sandow. The last nights
and matinees of the Strong Man are passing away
one at a time, and it is understood that he
will withdraw his impressive outlines from Mel-
bourne observation and proceed elsewhere with
his heavy weights before the end of next week.
Meanwhile, a demand for Sandow has been raised
by the meek section of the community, which
takes its pleasures prayerfully, and objects to im-
peril its hereafter by setting foot in a theatre. A
matinee at, say, the Masonic Hall would supply a
Christian want—a short introductory address,
followed by Sandow, the meeting to conclude with
the Doxology.
## * *
The Australian press in general, with
many adjectives and great wealth of detail, has
kindly notified readers of The Bulletin that the
first Melba concert at Melb. Town Hall did not
happen on Sept. 24 as originally decreed. The
event was postponed until Saturday of this week
(27th), and all subsequent dates have been put
back two or three days in accordance with the
first postponement. To say anything further
about Madame Melba in this issue would be a
superfluous labor of adoration. Moreover, it
would probably seem irreverent in comparison
with the following extract from the Argus :
Madame Melba and her party travelled from Albury in
the car which was used by their Royal Highnesses the
Duke and Duchess of York, while in Victoria. Driver
Fitzgibbon was in charge of the train, with Guard Hanna
in van, and Conductor “ Denny ” Donovan looked after
the comforts of the occupants of the Royal car. Break-
fast was served in the dining-saloon by the So-and-So Caf6
staff, under Mr. Musgrove’s instructions; and the
drawing-room, beautifully decorated, made a delightful
boudoir from which Madame Melba, surrounded by
bouquets, acknowledged the cheers of the people at the
different stations.
** # *
The Musgrove Dramatic Co. is quitting
the Princess’s this week with the earnest intention
of returning in time for certain races at Fleming-
ton in November. “ Mice and Men,” or, perhaps,
“Sweet Nell,” will bring the season to a close.
Anyway, there is to be a special Melba matinee
revival of “Sweet Nell’’for one afternoon, when
the enthusiasm will doubtless be great.
*# * #
On Saturday the rough, but fruity, voice
of Frank Thornton will be heard in the land once
more. The first time Thornton and his present
co. appeared in Melbourne they came with a
comedy that was more farcical than funny, but
this time they produce themselves in “ A Little
Ray of Sunshine,” which is less farcical, likewise
“ sparkling and unique.”
* * *
The Royal changes the bill on Saturday.
“A Woman of Pleasure” has repented her indis-
cretions for three weeks, and as the Anderson co.
has only another week to fill in the management
will seize the sad opportunity to stage “The
World Against Her ” for five farewell nights. As
the years roll on “ The World Against Her ” gives
promise of achieving the proud, tear-stained
position long time sacred to ‘ ‘ East Lynne. ”
* * *
Charles Holloway has completed a long
and successful season in Adelaide—four weeks at
the Royal, and two and a half more at the'Tivoli—
and the latter house has, for the moment, closed its
doors. Holloway himself finished up in a blaze of
triumph as the manly parson in “Shall We Forgive
Her ? ” Whatever jesting remarks may in this
column have been made on his dramas, they have
been distinguished by good all-round acting, and
mounting of the best, and Adelaide has supported
them with enthusiasm. At the Royal, “Robin
Hood ” was well received for the melody that is
De Koven’s, even though the humor that should
be H. B. Smith’s had mostly to be supplied by
Lauri, and by Ward’s Upper Lip. At present
Lauri is doing better with a large drugged
policeman in “ The Circus Girl,” and Miss Lulu
Evans has been dropped into a cast already
strong in singers. Said cast is also exhibiting
much dancing talent, for which “Robin Hood”
gave it no opening at all.
No sooner is the Vic. Factories Act a dead
letter than the sweater begins to turn the screw.
At a meeting of the Melb. Anti-Sweating League,
t’other day, it was stated that already had
slaughtermen received notice that they were to
have a reduction of 10s. per week in their wages ;
whilst other employers had announced reductions
of ss. and over. Already, in scores of instances,
men had been sacked, and their places filled with
cheap boys. It was also said that one of the
largest retail establishments in the city had given
its employees notice, as soon as the Act lapsed,
that in future they were not to be allowed any
weekly half-holiday. Some large employers
are loyally adhering to the provisions of the dead
Act, but when they begin to feel the deadly com-
petition of the sweater the would-be humane
employers have nothing for it but to follow suit.
In their haste these wage-cutting bosses have
already lost Irvine and co. a good many votes.
A Victorian pressman sums up the situ-
ation :
The Victorian Tory conspiracy to burst up the Factories
Act, bear down salaries, and block progressive legislation
is meeting with success. The fact is due largely to the
cowardice of the democratic leaders. The conspiracy
was hatched in Flinders-lane. Then the two morning
dailies were taken into partnership, and the so-called
reform agitation started in all the glory of black-letter
headlines. By a trick the Peacock Ministry was turned
out of office, and by a further trick Irvine and the doleful
Shiels were put on the Treasury benches. A third trick
secured the dissolution of Parliament. The Factories Act
went to pieces with the dissolution, and then the Age and
Argus set about scaring the people and the democratic
leaders. By much shrieking and lying they frightened
all reason out of the people, and Peacock and his alleged
Liberals crept away like so many whipped curs. A day
or two after the dissolution half the men who had voted
against the Irvine Ministry on the fatal division came
sneaking around to get Ministerial support. Irvine came
out with a pompous manifesto, full of fine phrases about
the ruin of the State and the necessity of Responsible
Government. The democratic leaders crept away some
more, and unless the democratic portion of the community
wakes up before polling-day Victoria will, for the first
time in 20 years, be completely handed over to the Tories.
Democrats were gulled by the fair promises of Irvine that
he would do nothing to endanger the Factories Act. They
now know what Irvine’s word is worth, and should vote
accordingly. They should not forget that while Irvine
talks so much about Responsible Government he is hand-
ing the Government of Victoria over to the newspapers.
The reform movement was created, organised, and worked
to the profit of the two big daily newspapers, and Irvine
has bowed down and worshipped it. If Irvine comes back
with a big majority, as is almost certain, it means
government by the Age and Argus, and that is Tory
rule of the very worst type,
The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The People’s Prudential Assurance Co.
(Sydney) completed its sixth year on 31st August, 1902.
It is one of the many little proprietary life offices which
sprang up, mushroom-wise, in recent years ; but, though
it also struggles at the back-door of the working-man for
the threepenny industrial premium, its principal profits
seem to be made out of the sick benefit and bottle-of-
medicine business. The present co. took over the business
of a previous institution, and -the £3500 paid for it con-
stitutes the goodwill item (now in process of writing-off)
alluded to below :

The most notable feature of the ease is that expenses are
growing in a remarkable fashion. In the first four years
of the co.’s existence expenses did not equal claims ; for
the last two they are distinctly in advance of claims. In
1902, for the first time, the expenses were swelled by the
payment of a 5 per cent, dividend (£217) to shareholders,
but even allowing for this the situation presents these
remarkable features:
Expenses in Proportion to Total Income.
1897 .. £44 1 8 per cent. 1900 .. £4l 0 8 per cent.
1898 .. 39 16 3 „ 1901 .. 48 8 3
1899 .. 39 13 6 „ 1902 .. 62 0 5 „
If the dividend is left out, expenses for 1902 run to £5O
6s. lOd. per cent., and this increase in the proportional
cost of running the business, as the business grows
larger, is not what might fairly be expected in a well-
regulated office.
» * * *
Like many other institutions of its kind, the
People’s Prudential fills no visible want save the want, on
the part of certain individuals concerned, of decent
billets and a promising investment for their money.
When the first valuation was made, in 1901, it had 177
ordinary life policies (the fact that an obscure concern
with very little capital and struggling along mainly in the
medical benefit business should be able to get them,
shows the casual fashion in which people assume that
one office is probably just about the same as another),
2123 medical benefit policies, 19 accident policies, and
4036 industrial and miscellaneous. The industrial part of
the show is the usual sorrowful bargain by which the
thriftless person who can’t save up a half-year’s premium,
but must have the 2d. called for every week, gets very
little indeed for the money. The miscellaneous includes
interesting family events in the Gamp line of business,
medical attendance, and the other things that Oddfellows,
Buffaloes, and Rechabites deal in. Altogether, this 00.
fishes for the small premium in many waters, but when
all the expenses are paid, there doesn’t seem to be enough
left to make the transaction a very promising one for
its customers.

The Victorian Permanent Building Society has nominally
£227,902 of capital still intact. How much it would have
if values were written down to their real present figure is
another question. The investments represent, on paper,
£516,543, and when £516,543 (alleged) of property doesn’t
earn enough to pay. interest on £288,630 of outside
liabilities, the bulk of them carrying interest at only 2£
per cent., these assets can’t be taken too seriously.
• « • «
Still, bad as things are with the Victorian Per-
manent Building Society, they have been a great deal
worse, and the improvement is very material. This, for
instance, is the amount owing to depositors, mortgagees,
and bank during the last few years :
Owing to Depositors, Bank, and Mortgagees.
1897 .. .. £383,689 1900 .. .. £313,838
1898 .. .. 338,966 1901 .. .. 301,177
1899 .. .. 322,551 1902 .. .. 288,630
The liabilities have been reduced by £95,059 in the past
five years, and the loss on the year’s operations is £5361
less than it was in 1897. If this rate of progress
can be kept up, and if the depositors renew again
at 2£ per cent, (their present agreement expiring
in 1903), the Victorian Permanent should be able
to save a good deal for its shareholders out of
the wreck. But it looks like requiring years
of patience to put the institution fairly on its feet
again, and if it was wound up now, supposing the earn-
ings to be any criterion of present values, it seems as if it
would realise no more than enough to pay off the de-
positors and mortgagees. Whether it would realise even
that much on present showing is a matter that it would
be hardly safe to swear to.
• * * *
Whispered that Vic. Treasurer Shiels’ latest
proposal, if the savings don’t meet the deficit—and, of
course, they won’t—is to levy income-tax on all incomes
over £lOO a year. That will touch up the deluded
workers who vote for Irvine’s “ reform ” ; but it will then
be too late to argue with the Assembly, and the 48 Fats
will pass such a measure with enthusiasm—not as a tem-
porary Act, either.

* * » •
Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*
* * * *
A revolution in smelting furnaces seems pro-
bable. The aid of gasoline has not been recognised.
Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, supplies
forms to suit assayers, which are marvellous in their
efficiency.*
* * * *
The Government of New South* Wales have
entered into a contract for the manufacture, supply, and
delivery of 13 miles of Mephan Ferguson’s patent Lock-
ingbar or Rivetless steel pipes. These are to be 32 inches
diameter, in 25 feet lengths, and will be used for convey-
ing water from Nepean River to Prospect Reservoir. This
type of pipe has been used by the Government of South
Australia for water-supply purposes for some time past,
and has given great satisfaction ; also by the Govern-
ment of Western Australia for the Coolgardie water
scheme, for which about 320 miles of Lockingbar pipes
have been delivered.*

A Sure Fortune.— The best investment in
Australia is buying land at Esperance, W.A., the natural
port of the Kalgoorlie goldfields. With a railway,
Esperance will become one of our busiest ports, as the
trade of the goldfields will pass through it. A permanent
survey is now being made ; the railway is a certainty.
Get in early and make a fortune by purchasing a few
blocks before the great rush sets in. Price, £2O a block.
Terms, £1 a month. Write for plans and particulars to
The Esperance Land Company, Nicholson Chambers,
Swanston-st., Melbourne.*

* * * *
The Gardner Electric Eock Drill which has
been lately introduced into Australia is being very
largely adopted in the mines of the United States, and
is giving great satisfaction. This electric drill is filling a
long felt want, now that electricity is being so largely
adopted for all mining purposes. The Gardner Electric
Drill is very simple and easily handled, and drills as fast
as the air drill, and costs much less to instal in a mine,
especially in out of the way places. It requires only one
fifth of the power required to operate an air drill doing
the same work, so the saving in fuel soon pays its cost.
The Gardner Drill at the North Gippsland Proprietary
mine, Victoria, has been in use for eight months, and
done all the drilling for a tunnel 1500 feet long in very
hard country, and the manager speaks highly in praise
of it. The Tarcoola Blocks Co., South Australia, has
ordered a complete equipment for 6 electric drills and
lighting plant for the mine, to be worked by an oil engine
of 16 horse power. The Sole Australian Agents, the
Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne, will
supply catalogues and estimates on request.*

M.L. Govt, has at last decided to quench a fire
that has been burning in a coal-seam up the Waitohu
river, near Reefton, for 20 years. The coal deposits there
are valuable, and ’tis intended to run a water-race into
the burning seam.

The Bulletin’s old and unpleasant acquaintance,
the Independent Order of Foresters life assurance
concern, of Canada, has lately been carrying on a long
and straggling controversy with the S.M. Herald about
the sufficiency of its rates to keep it solvent, and other
intricate matters. These things are not much understood
by the masses, so the controversy isn’t likely' to do
much in the Way of keeping them out of the 1.0. F.
concern. Points whioh the insurer can understand very
clearly are these :
(1) The small insurer is by far the most frequent
variety.
(2) To him, at age 25, the 1.0. F. concern offers a £lOO
policy without profits, and with no surrender value, for
£ll6s. 6d. a year. At that figure he is admitted into a
concern which, according to the recognised rules of
actuarial science, has a huge deficiency.
(2) The A.M.P. offers, at age 25, a £lOO policy without
profits for £1 13s. 7d. a year, and the A.M.P. has no
deficiency in its assets.
These are the points which the insurer should keep
before his eye all the time. It doesn’t matter that the
1.0. F. calls part of its charge “ court dues ” instead of
premium ; the policy-holder has to pay the amount all
the same. So far as concerns the small insurer, who is
also the most frequent insurer, the 1.0.F.’s boasted cheap-
ness is just a falsehood. And while taking more money
from the policy-holder than the A.M.P. does, it also
spends so much more in expenses or other charges that
there is less left to accumulate towards the payment of
the policy than in the A.M.P.’s case. To the small policy-
holder the 1.0. F. offers the blessed privilege of paying
more money for a great deal less security.
The writer still comes, occasionally, across that
unspeakably cheeky advertisement with which the
bounding 1.0. F. first stormed Australia. It set forth in
parallel columns the 1.0.F.’s rates for a policy that carried
no bonuses (but it didn’t mention that its policy
carried no bonuses), and what it said were the
average. rates charged by Australian companies
for policies with bonuses, and asked the public
to consider the difference and the cheapness of
the 1.0. F. And, not content with setting its policy with
no bonus and no surrender value, against the Australian
offices’ policy with a bonus and a surrender value, it
supplied a set of alleged rates for the Australian
offices, which were considerably higher than the real
rates. If there had been more cohesion among the Aus-
tralian offices legal proceedings for slander or getting busi-
ness by false representations might have followed, but
they didn’t. A good, comprehensive Adulteration Act,
with general power to deal with anybody who sells any-
thing by misleading representations has been a long-felt
want in Australia.

It doesn’t appear for certain that the £150,000 capital
above-mentioned was ever really written off- not that it
makes any difference whether the concern professes to
have £250,000 capital to set against £470.345 of losses, or
£lOO,OOO capital to set against £320,345 of losses. Either
way there is an admitted shortage of £220,345, even
when all the capital is written off. By its own showing
the balance-sheet of MU,can Bros, and Rigg may be con-
densed as follows :
Liabilities. Assets.

Mortgage .. .. 5,720
Other debts.. .. 63,641
£542,833 £542,833
That is the situation, even if the whole capital is written
off, and as a case of frank and cheerful insolvency it is
interesting. Of course, the concern need not necessarily
remain insolvent; it has already reduced its deficit by
over £32,000, and it may clear off the rest in time. But
it is an interesting situation in the meantime.

September rains have much brightened the crop
outlook in S.A., and, for once, the Treasurer’s budget
estimate of the wheat yield is likely to be below the
mark. Usually, it is a bushel or so too much, which
makes a difference when 2,000,000 acres are sown.

Marshall’s Paddington Brewery, Limited
(Sydney), up to 31st July, 1902 :

1900 .. 5958 6 „ „ 1789 4169 17,182
1"1 •• 5976 6 » *. 1782 4194 21,376
1902 .. 4561 6 „ „ 1782 2779 24,155
On the surface of things, this looks like splendid pro-
sperity. Despite the falling-off in profits this year
the earnings represent £l5 7s. 2d. per cent, per annum
on the paid-up capital of £29,701, and for 1901 they repre-
sented over 20 per cent. These reserves (reserve fund,
£20,000, and undivided profit, £4155) have grown up like
Jonah’s gourd till they nearly equal the capital—and,
with all this, things don’t look well. In the first
place, the principal asset is described as “Buildings,
Land, Plant, Goodwill and Furniture, £56,345.” An old-
established business like this should have, before now
written off goodwill out of profits. If goodwill isn’t
written off, the exact amount of this paper asset should at
least be shown. And whatever the amount may be, it
requires to be deducted from the nominal reserves before
the real reserves can be arrived at.
-Another point is that, despite the increase of
the nominal reserves, the co. seems to be accumulating
no liquid assets—in fact, it seems to be growing shorter
of liquid assets year by year. The stock, buildings, &c.,
are not increasing to any great extent—all the increase
of reserves is represented by the growth of the item
4-u Su n? anc * these are growing so remarkably
that the possibility of a good part of the debts proving
difficult of collection can’t be overlooked. Instead of the
solid credit bank balance of a few years ago there is now a
substantial and increasing overdraft. The writer has not
the complete figures for 1900 by him, but the following
are of some interest: °
Credit Bank
Sundry Debtors. Balance.
1897 .. £11,097 .. £2871
!898 .. 16,201 .. 1730
18" - • 18,811 .. 1196
1900 _ Overdraft.
1901 .. 26,751 .. 2325
1902 .. 30,606 .. 2883
The increase in the amounts owing to the co. (presumably
the uncollected debts) more than swallows up the profit
that remains afterpayment of thedividend, and a fine large
overdraft is accumulating. Another remarkable feature
is this : When, the co. started its reserve fund (now
fono ng . at £20,000) with an appropriation of £4OOO in
1898, and a similar amount in 1899, it treated it as a
depreciation fund, and deducted it from the amount of
“ Sundry Debtors,” which certainly suggested that there
were heavy losses or probable losses to be provided for.
It was only after 1899 that it began to call it a reserve fund,
and ceased to treat it as if it was required as a provision
for bad or doubtful debts. Whatever the explanation,
there is apparently an increasing scarcity of actual cash,
and a large increaso in the amount of uncollected
debts. More than the whole capital is now invested in
“Sundry Debtors.” The fact that the shares of a co.,
which is apparently earning 15 to' 20 per cent., remain
persistently below par, is also significant.

Re Coolgardie water supply scheme. It is now
alleged that the dam, instead of impounding 4,600,000,000
gallons a year, as was calculated, only impounds
800,000,000 (less than one-fifth), and the supply, calcu-
lated at 5,000,000 gallons a day, must therefore be cut
down (when the pipes begin to run) to 1,000,000ga1. a day.

mishap which brings them very low down indeed. The
increased dividend is a mystery. It absorbs all the earn-
ings save a miserable £B3 ; yet the institution is loaded
with over £72,000 of mortgages and bank overdraft which
must be paid off before any settled prosperity can be
looked for, and this increase in the dividend makes the

very heavy, and the increase in the dividend doesn’t
promise to accelerate the process of reduction.
* * * •
Definitely announced that the Great Australian
Quicksilver 00. has made arrangements for the erec-
tion of furnaces on its property at Broadwater
(Clarence River, N.S.W.). 00. has been carrying
on prospecting operations for some years, and is alleged
to have a fair quantity of payable cinnabar ore ready for
stoping. The difficulty hitherto has been in the treat-
ment of the ore, several experiments having failed. Why
this should be so is beyond comprehension, as cinnabar
ore is successfully treated in various parts of the world
by very simple processes. Assuming that the plant about
to be erected is successful, the co. will have to face the
more serious difficulty of disposing of the product.
Quicksilver market is controlled absolutely by a combine
which can afford to sell mercury at a price far below
what would be a payable point here. Whether it could
afford it or not, it would probably undersell a local co., so
as to knock it out.
• * * *
Recent rains have been of little or no benefit to
Oobar, the only mine at which work is being carried on
being the big copper mine. Cobar’s difficulty is O’Sulli-
van's opportunity. He has done well by making arrange-
ments for sufficient water to be conveyed from Warren to
keep the blast furnaces running. He is now about to do
something which may be of permanent advantage. He has
been advised by some of the officers of his dept, that there
is a probability of artesian water being struck within 25
miles of Cobar. Other officials ridicule the opinion.
O’Sullivan has determined to promptly put the conflicting
opinions to the test. A bore is to be put down at once in
the locality indicated as being a likely one for artesian
water. The value of a successful outcome to this work
can hardly be estimated, and should water be struck
O’Sullivan will have earned an enduring monument
from a grateful people. Were an abundant supply of
water available there can be no question that more men
would be employed at Cobar than at Broken Hill and the
town of Cobar would become of infinitely greater im-
portance to N.S.W.
* * • *
North Central (Broken Hill) reports that this
property is under offer for £2500. At one time Proprie-
tary Co. would willingly have given more than that
amount for the ground to use it as a slag-dump.
• * * m
Girilambone Co. has made arrangements with a
syndicate for the treatment of the large accumulation of
tailings by a leaching process. It is understood that
negotiations are in progress for the working of the mine
by a syndicate which proposes to separate the copper
pyrites from the siliceous gangue by the magnetic process.
Broken Hill mines: The lead market continues
very sick, but prospects are not quite so crow-black.
Imports to the U.K. for August were 17,065 tons, against
an average of 19,484 tons monthly from January to July.
Australian lead exports to the U.K. in the first eight
months of last year were 41,816 tons ; the slump knocked
them back to 38,070 tons. Others, however kept going,
and put theirs up from 102,105 tons to 115,580 tons. The
United States keep going still ; but the other big pro-
ducer, Spain, has apparently got tired, and is now easing
off—July returns were 8931 tons, against 10,819 tons in
July, 1901. That diminished Spanish competition is the
most pleasant-looking item that has come the way of
Australian silver-lead miners for a good many month's.
* * * »
Nothing much is happening at the Broken Hill
mines; the managers keep themselves warm rasping
down expenses. The B.H. Prop, is still doing good
work sintering its slimes, ancf the principal change
in this co.’s ever-changing methods is the extension of
this process. The Huntington-Heberlein desulphurising
process is now fully installed at the Pirie works, and
Heberlein, who carried it through, has left. The co.
intends to shortly extend its coke plant at Bellambi.
« » • *
The British (Broken Hill), having done a reason-
able amount of development work, has, like all its neigh-
bors, shut down until lead improves, or something else
turns up. By the way, the Junction North and some
others will wonder what has happened to their mills when
they next try to use them. No attempt has been made
to keep them in working order. The Carmiehael-Bradford
desulphurising people have word from P. H. Snow, who
took the patent to Europe, that German experimenters
have got over the difficulties that cropped up this side.
* * * *
Tasmania’s boss gold-mine—the Tasmania—is
likely to be acquired by an English co. It has been
placed under offer on these terms : £6 per share of the
present issue in cash and 120,000 shares in a company of
£750,000, with a working capital of £lOO,OOO. Experts
from London are expected to be at the mine within three
or four weeks. The mine has been one of the most con-
sistent producers in Australia, its record of dividends
being exceeded only by Mt. Morgan and Long Tunnel. The
great drawback of the mine has been the enormous volume
of water to be contended with, the pumping plant being
larger than that on any of the big mines in the Victorian
alluvial districts. It is reported that this difficulty is
being steadily overcome, and that it will soon be possible
to carry on stoping operations in the lower levels where
large bodies of ore have been proved to exist. In view of
the splendid prospects of the property the proposal made
to the English people seems a very moderate one, the
only point on which any question can be raised is as to
the necessity for £lOO,OOO working capital. The mine is
.well equipped and development work is well advanced.
At the outside £50,000 working capital to provide
additional treatment appliances ought to be ample.
» * • *
The Vulcan tin mine, Irvinebank (Q.), is one
which is not heard much of, possibly because it is owned
by comparatively few individuals who are content to
draw good dividends without worrying about the share
market. Last half-year co. paid £4400 in divs. Since its
inception, divs. have totalled £58,800.
* * « •
Great Central Victoria mine, Victoria Hill—
one of the historic spots of Bendigo—has just completed
a crushing of 24 loads for 2840 z. The stone came from a
spur at a depth of 2715 ft.
*■ m * *
The Long Tunnel (Walhalla, Vic.) squabble
continues, and is intensifying in bitterness. The manage-
ment of the Extended Co. seems very much piqued
because the L.T. 00. refused to accept the terms pro-
posed by it for amalgamation, and is now doing
its uttermost to prevent the L.T. Co. from carry-
ing out the permanent work which is necessary
before it can again reach the dividend stage. This work
is the sinking of a new underlay shaft. Owing to the
configuration of the country this shaft has to be located
in such a position as to necessitate part of its course being
in the Extended ground. The Mines Department has
power to grant the L.T. co. authority to sink the shaft
partly through the other co’s ground. A battle royal is
being fought on this point, the Extended Co. bringing all
sorts of influence to bear on the Minister to induce him
to withhold his sanction. He will be a weak-minded
creature indeed, if he allows any such dog-in-the-manger
consideration as is prompting the action of the Extended
Co. to influence him.
* * * *
Kaufmann ia not likely to profit much out of
the Merton’s Reward deal. The first crushing under his
regime returned a trifle over loz. per ton, but the second
was under the oz. mark. Reports from the field indicate
that the prospects are anything but rosy. Merton was a
wise man when he elected to take £llO,OOO in cash for the
prop erty.
» * * *
So far 1000 tons of copper ore, worth £14,000,
have gone from the new Phillips River (W. A.) district to
Wallaroo for smelting. But though the picked stuff sent
away has gone up to 30 per cent, copper with 3 or 4dwt.
gold, the bulk of the ores cannot stand the charges, and
the Mines Department has now on hand a petition for a
Government smelter.
* * * *
The A. E. Morgans syndicate is busy on Green-
bushes (W. A.) tinfield. The biggest job on hand, superin-
tended by G. H. Clarth, is the bringing in of water from
the Blackwood River. The main will be between six and
seven miles long, and a lift of 800 ft. is necessary; the
plant under order will supply a million gallons a day.
Alongside one of the two reservoirs which are being got
ready a smelter is being built; and the syndicate has
already a fair stack of bought ore—buying started the
beginning of August. The whole scheme should be
going by the opening of 1903.
* • • •
Lake View Consols still making desperate
efforts to find fresh pay-ore before complete exhaustion
of present diminishing reserves. Tenders have been called
for driving 200 ft. at the 800,1000, and 1200 ft. levels. 00.
has lent 10-head of its mill to the Elmore Oil Co., which
intends experimenting on a commercial scale with W.A.
ores. This process has been in the experimental stage
for years, and great are the claims made for it as to
efficiency and economy in the concentration of mineralised
ores.
* * *
Euro, Laverton (W.A.), like all the mines of
that discredited district, seems to peter out at a very
shallow depth. During Aug. the mill, through scarcity
of ore, only ran 8 % days, and treated 1251 tons for 5220 z.
Why a 40-head mill was ever erected on such a one-horse
show—which, even at its best, only provided enough ore
for a 16 days’ run a month—possibly G. W. Hall knows.
Hall is credited with having taken up more leases and
paid more fees into the treasury than any other individual
in the State.
* » * *
Ivanhoe (W.A.) has already replaced 10 head of
the old light stamps by heavy ones, and expects to re-
place the remaining 60 head before the year is out. In
spite of the job occupying 20 days, there was no diminu-
tion in the tonnage treated, August figures reading 10,767
tons for 12170 z. The total working costs are given as
41s. Id. per ton, including Bs. 2d. for development. Ex-
cluding London costs and depreciation, £2 a ton is the
low water-mark of Kalgurli mining costs, under existing
conditions.
* * * *
Boulder Perseverance (W.A.) is again in charge
of Manager Ralph Nichols, who has returned after a few
months’ holiday. Mine looking better than ever and ore
reserves must be far ahead of the mill. The underlay of the
Associated’s Tetley lode came into the property at about
400 ft., and is now being opened up at the 500 and 700 ft.
levels. The August output of 16,3110 z. from 12,487 tons is a
record for the mine. The figures of co. are not given, but
the profit must be about £35,000, sufficient to pay 4s. a
share monthly. The next 10s. div. is due on October 10.
• • • •
Kalgurli (W.A.) has been developing very well'
at the lower levels, and reserves are now well ahead of
treatment-plant. At 1000 ft. the ore averages 2§dwt. for
the 50ft. driven. Plant, since being remodelled, is getting
fairly into work, and, during August, treated 3645 tons
for 38720 z. This is much the best run so far accom-
plished. The costs at the mine tot up to 375. 7d. per ton,
which leaves £BOOO, less London exs., for divs., which are
certainly long overdue. Present rate of profit won’t last,
as only 3s. 6d. a ton figures for development and Is. lid.
for repairs, both of which are abnormally small. Ivanhoe,
which treats three times the quantity, allows 11s. 6d. for
these items.
* • « *
Latest developments at the lower levels of the
Associated (W.A.) merely go to confirm statement, pre-
viously made, that the lodes at the 800 and 1050 ft. levels
are absolutely valueless. So far as proved, no pay ore
exists below 600 ft. Reports are being industriously
circulated that the rich Iron Duke chute will be cut at
the 1050 ft. level, but, until continuation is proved at
Oroya’s 8 level, it is premature to predict anything
regarding it. August return is published as 47260 z. from
5119 tons, which will leave no margin for profit.
* * * •
The Ida H., Laverton (W.A.), ia making a
brave show till insiders get out. The latest return from
the mine is 13320 z. from 1160 tons. At the lowest level
the reef becomes poorer and beautifully less. Going south
it is a mere leader till it pinches out altogether. Early
in the year shares were quoted in London at about £4
and have since fallen to 355., so evidently the tap has
been turned on. When the public are left with the
baby to hold, and not till then, will the true state of
affairs be announced, and the usual wail will be heard.

Associated Northern (W.A.) according to
Manager Rodda, will have a 20-head battery, concentrat-
ing tables, cyanide plant and slimes process at work in
six months. It is to cost £40,000. “In the States,” said
Rodda, “ I (jould put up a similar plant for 20,000 dol-
lars.” That would be possible in W.A. if London
directors would give Australian machinery-makers a
chanoe. But the suggestion is too horrible 1 It would so
materially reduce “ com.” which is so dearly loved by
lordly guinea-pigs. Rodda would prefer to continue to
send ore to the smelters but the low grade has to be
treated on the spot. “ There’s over £1,000,000 worth of
rich ore blocked off ready to be stoped,” said the mine-
manager to the writer the other day.
* * • •
Lake Violet Consols, Wilura (W.A.), is chiefly
remarkable for the efficiency of its 10-head battery.
During August, this mill ran 24 days and treated 1330
tons for 4960 z. This figures out at 5.54 tons per stamp
daily, which must be a record for Australia. Crushing
only started in February, with an average of 14dwt. 6gr.,
and this has tailed off so rapidly that it is now only 7dwt.
Bgr. Any further decrease will silence the champion
battery.
* * • *
The remodelling of the Mb. Charlotte and
Hannan’s Reward (W.A.) plant is almost complete, and the
treatment of ore will be resumed shortly. Stoping from
the cross lodes is now in progress, but the rock drills
break down so much mullock that the average value is
bound to go very low, and quite unpayable. It is safe to
predict that the co. will never earn a dividend, and that,
within three months, the mine will again be in the hands
of tributors, rooting out the small rich leaders.

MY LOVE AND I.
[For The Bulletin.]
We strolled beside the sapphire sea—■
The girl whom I loved and I.
The breeze was warm, the sun shone gay
From deep in his cloudless sky.
I thought she loved me in return,
My heart beat joy-ful-ly ;
And O ! how glad I thought the song
Of the never silent sea.
Again we strolled beside the shore,
The girl whom I loved and I;
But the breeze was chill and the heartless sun
Mocked up in his cold blue sky.
I thought she loved me in return ;
But alas ! it was not so—
The girl I loved had tossed her head
As she cruelly said me “ No ! ”
And the Universe was a wasted thing,
And time went dolefully,
And the world was superfluous and dead
That day by a grey, grey sea.
m.l. Geo. Ewell Bisset.
• * * *
The first breath of summer blows on
Sydney ; the drought is still with us ; the plague is
still hanging about the horizon ; the water supply
is lower than ever, and an evil-minded and stench-
ful economy in the matter of flushing drains, &c.,
is forced on the people. Clouds of dust and
germs rage up and down the streets —the worn
wood-blocks of the city and the drought-stricken
macadam further out. A good many things want
doing at this crisis, but they all bump up hard
against these three immutable Australian laws:
(1) It is wrong and utterly sinful to do any public works
of any kind except with loan mone} - .
(2) If loan money can’t be got, the works, however
necessary, must be left undone, even if we have enough
revenue, or could easily get enough revenue, to do them.
(3) When loans become unprocurable we must cease
doing any works and relapse into barbarism.
UP-TO-DATE.

HE: “No, that's played-out. How do you think
‘ Decree Nisi’ would go?"
The royal road to glory, so far as Mel-
bourne people are concerned just now, is to have
known Melba, or to be related to somebody who
knows Melba, or to know somebody who is related
to Melba, or to have some probable chance of
touching the hem of Melba’s robe. Sandow, the
strong man, who has been a craze, fades into com-
parative insignificance before the strong woman,
and the smaller stars hide their diminished rays.
The withdrawal of all competitors from the field
at the approach of the Queen-singer is a remark-
able feature of the visit. Sandow has had many
challenges; nobody with vocal pretensions even
ventures out of doors since Melba came into her
kingdom, and it is better to have carried home
the diva’s washing than to be a little, local, home-
made celebrity. Excruciating bores have cropped
up like mushrooms along the Yarra during the
last few weeks. They are the people who heartily
congratulated Melba and predicted her great
success in her girlish days. These terrors constitute
a numerous class, and do not disguise their great
expectations. If Madame is remiss in exhibiting
her heartfelt gratitude to several thousand people
that she never heard of before, her stock will
slump woefully in many homes.

ney Master Butchers’ Association that two-thirds

« * * »
Archdeacon Gunther, who knows so much
about early Sydney, happened on a mare’s nest in
the Anglican Synod last week. The notion that
the Samaritan nuns made something out of the
site of their old home in Pitt-street. has suggested
to many “squatters ” on Crown lands to try to
establish a deal. The filthy old plot of iniquity
next St. Andrew’s Cathedral became the site of
Sydney Town Hall, and as it adjoined St.
Andrew’s, though the church had no title to it
and merely mouldered next door, a feeling has
arisen that St. A. might have got some money out of
the transaction—been paid for being next door, so
to speak. The Archdeacon tried to revive some
sort of a claim, but it was no use. If the
Church had worked up a claim in political
pressure style at the time some money consolation
might have been given. The Anglican Church
was a kind of suzerain over the little plot, dirty
and desecrated as it was, and many of its highest
pillars mouldered to dust there.

Subscribers to Melbourne Women’s Hos-
pital (KiOO of them) were recently asked to express
an opinion as to the desirableness of continuing
the system by which women patients in the mid-
wifery section are labelled as married or unmarried,
so that kind Christian cats can seek to bring home
to the sisters who are “fallen” a sense of their
thud. Only 100 took the trouble to vote, so very
little light has been thrown on the question, after
all.
* • * *
In Deniliquin (N.S.W.) gaol recently a
prisoner under sentence for forgery, when being
searched before being locked in the cells for the
night, managed to lock the warder in, after which
he leisurely wandered through the gaol, opening
this door and locking that, until he found himself
free. Another warder soon raised the alarm ; the
services of a fleet-footed prisoner were enlisted,
and he ran the fugitive to earth near the river.
Official ructions are pending, and this free-and-
easy gaol is to be inquired into.

* • * *
There appears to be reasonable ground for
the belief that in the poorer parts of Sydney
horseflesh is now disposed of as butchers’ meat.
The number of old and useless horses disposed of
at the Zoological Gardens has been comparatively
small of late, and the inference is that a more pro-
fitable market has been found for them. There-
fore, a larger and darker suspicion than usual
broods, crow-fashion, over the sausage, and there is
a baleful rumor in the pie. There is no reason to
suppose that horse is likely to contain any more
strange diseases than bullock; he leads an open-
air life hauling the omnibus, and seldom lacks for
exercise. Still, the man who asks for a certain
animal wants to have that animal.

charming baby.”
During 1901 South Australia had 9079
births—the smallest number, bar that of 1898, for
25 years. In proportion to population the record
was the worst for 50 years. In 1885 there were
12,040 births in S.A. ; last year, with a muoh
larger population, there were 2967 fewer. All
over the Commonwealth the habit of being born
is dying out—slowly, it may be, but very steadily.
There were 110,186 births in the Commonwealth
during 1891, and only 102,221 in 1900, and 1901
promises to be worse than 1900.

* * * •
An ancient gentleman of 65, who described
himself very frankly as a used-up and thrown-
aside squatter’s dummy, applied early this month
for a pension at Mulwala (N.S.W.). By his own
account, he had dummied for one squatter off and
on for a good part of a lifetime, and had handed
over three selections, receiving a watch and a
saddle by way of consideration. In his old age,
when he probably had become too shaky to tell a
coherent tale to the Land Board when he went to
take up another selection, or tottered too much to
reside steadily on a selection when he got it, he
was (so he said) pensioned off with £25 a year for
life, but after enjoying this affluence for two years
it was stopped. According to the evidence, the
old gentleman’s son had also been in the dummy-
ing business, which seems to suggest that taking
up land is hereditary. The son, it was alleged,
didn’t even get a watch or a saddle when he
handed his selection over. It seems to be a very
poor game.
* • * *
An extraordinary instance of justices’
justice was that at New Angledool (N.S.W.) on
Sept. 8. Billy Leach, a half-caste, was charged
with “living with an aboriginal woman” and
sentenced, apparently without the hearing of any
sworn evidence, to three months gaol. Leach
was a hard-working fellow, and the woman,
“Weetalibah Nelly,” had been his consort for
more than five years ; she also was a half-caste.
The parties were not married, but if dispensing
with the marriage ceremony is going to be a penal
Offence there will be need for a big loan to build
new gaols in N.S.W. The Bulletin knows of
high legal authorities who have dispensed with it
so often that they hardly remember what it is
like.
» * * »
“The art-sales drought," writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “is likely to
be accentuated to the Australian Angelo by the
exportation from here of over 300 paintings by
English and Scottish A.R.A.’s., etc. They go to
Joubert’s Melbourne Exhibition “on sale or
return.” The artist pays only 15s. to 80s. and, if
his picture is bought by a Toorakian or Potts
Pointer, 15 per cent, commission. The English
market is in a shooking state. Frederick Goodall,
R.A., in failing for £11,886, pleaded the decreased
value of art by living artists. So, artists, die that
your pictures may live."

Of the whole population of Victoria one in
268 is under confinement in the asylums as insane.
The percentage of female recoveries in 1882 was
about 61J. In 1901 it had fallen to about 35|.
With regard to male patients, the recoveries in
1897 fell below 27 per cent. The proportion of
lunatics per 10,000 of pof ulation in Victoria is 37,
Q. 30, N.S.W. 29, S.A. 26. In England the rate
is 29 per 1000. The Victorian curative system has
been shown to be a hopeless failure.

* * * •
Stewart Norman Croll, the Albert Park
boy who made love with a revolver, has been dis-
posed of, 10 years being his portion. Croll nearly
beat the life out of the girl of his heart with the
butt-end of the “gun,” after shooting her here
and there, and told the Court that he went “ off
his head ” at a certain temperature, and recol-
lected nothing of what happened after. Chief
Justice Madden evidently regarded the prisoner,
a youngster in his teens, as a vicious ruffian, and
dealt with him as such, although the community
may be grateful that no floggings were thrown in
to assure a proper regret on Croll’s part because
Nature had chosen to make him a specimen of the
abnormal. The Judge failed to recognise in Croll
a pronounced type of a peculiar class of perverts
clearly understood by scientific criminalogists.
The probability is that Croll was utterly irre-
sponsible, and while it is necessary to safeguard
society against men like him, gaol is not the place
to which an enlightened community would send
the victims of psychosis. As for moral homilies,
his Honor might as well go to the Zoo. and lec-
ture the leopard in fluent, _ grammatical, well
rounded sentences on the advisability of changing
his spots.

person who makes haste to conclude the hen that
lays 25 guineas a day.

Hobart City Council is advertising for a
pair of caretakers for the local Town Hall. Must
be married and reasonably young, but “ without
encumbrance.” Any number of similar billets are
ad vertised in Australia. When highly-respectable
Bumbles and others describe this rising young
nation simply as “encumbrance,” and sack the
parents for helping to increase the population, no
wonder the birth-rate is low.

The belated census returns of religions in
Victoria show that in the past decade there is
an Episcopalian decrease from 37.33 per cent, of
the population to 36.51 percent., against a Presby-
terian increase from 14.94 to 16.15, and a Methodist
ditto from 14.15 to 15.21, while Catholics have
remained absolutely stationary (22.24 per cent.),
and Jews have declined from 0.58 to 0.50 percent.
The Sceptics, among whom the statist, with
pleasing cynicism, classes “those who have either
no apparent religious belief or appear to be alto-
gether dissatisfied with the old-established and
popular religions of the world ” —number 4901.
Said statist diagnoses no less than 166 different
religions, among whom are 2 Glassites, 1 Grace-
walking Brother (suggestive of the story of
Colonel Quagg who used to cowhide that denomi-
nation), 3 Relatives of Jesus (females), 3 Latitu-
dinarians, 1 Eclectic, 1 Zoroastrian, 1 Taoist, 2
Shintos, 1 Sanhu, 1 Bi-Deist, 1 God Absolute (this
citizen didn’t actually designate himself a God,
but that is his religion), 1 Historien, 1 Infidel, 3
Scientists, and 1 who Believes in a Knowledge of
All. The statist also accounts for 32 individuals
whom he classifies as jesters or eccentrics, viz.,
1 whose religion is Anything Handy, 6 Calli-
thumpians, 1 Chuckite, 1 Don’t Know, 1 Fanatic,
1 Free Dietarian, 1 Heretic, IGo as You Please,
2 Think for Themselves, 4 Thistles, 1 Various,
1 Windshiftarian, 5 L.S.D., 1 worshipper of
Bacchus and Mammon. Surely the L.S.D.
religion and the worshippers of Bacchus and
Mammon and the Windshiftarian creed are
lamentably understated ! And why were not the
Believers in Property enumerated ? Of the 651
aborigines in the State the C.E. has captured 244,
Presbyterians 126, Methodists 71, Baptists 14,
Lutherans 10, Moravians 100, R.C. 14, and
Salvarmy 3. The rest are heathens and believers
in Native Bear.
* • * *
M.L. Governor Ranfurly is getting little
support for his pet scheme of a Veterans’ Home.
He has been stumping the various centres to
thronged audiences who go away and don’t part
up. The Jingo fever is played out, most people
are heartily sick of the antics of Dick Seddon
abroad, and pockets are buttoned up in expecta-
tion of hard times. So, despite the willingness of
the top-side community to oblige Ranfurly, the
scheme languishes. Otago district even carried a
resolution that it was not good enough for that pro-
vince to contribute to whose site was to
be in Auckland. Fancy establishing a home for
decayed veterans in Otago, where the sleet rushes
at you horizontally half the time—cold, sodden,
boggy, windy, sunless Presbyterian Otago !
* • * *
At the Anglican Synod in Sydney, last
week, Canon Boyce gave a list of 18 applications
for relief made in three days at a city rectory. In
nearly every case the applicant and her children
were on the verge of actual starvation. Discus-
sion ensued on the Canon’s report, as a result of
which it was decided :
That the first work of the committee should be to
provide for the spiritual needs of the poor and populous
parishes of the city and immediate suburbs.
WHEN THE PATRIOTIC TAP IS
TURNED ON.
RETURNED COHENTINGENTER (after fruitless
attempts to get back his position): “Well,
will you promise to give me a place if another
war breaks out? ”
The Sydney police have had some more
greatness thrust upon them. It is not enough
that they have to look after electoral-rolls, tote-
shops, gambling-schools, and larrikin “pushes”;
and, incidentally, to give an eye to burglars and
sandbaggers, so Inspector-General Fosbery, in his
wisdom, has decreed that the unfortunate copman
is to keep his ears open for the sound of the
running water-tap, and bring to book the mis-
creant who watereth his garden with a hose, or
feloniously disporteth himself in a plunge-bath.

“ J.F.D.” : Advocated, some years back,
the letting of church interiors for advertising pur-
poses. Well, it is not far off. Struck a parson’s
residence, in a Sydney suburb, adorned on the
gable end with a large advt. for Somebody’s
Bashed Oats. I canvassed the parson, and he
looked hungry enough to eat a year’s payment for
the advt. at one meal. I delicately hinted at the
sign while conversing with him, but he seemed
too tired to enter on the subject.
A Melbourne telegraph operator tells a
story of how a prominent citizen, in a state of
great joy, rolled into the office on Mafeking night
and wrote out a wire which nobody could
decipher. “So? —(hie). Mus’’ave’nother try. ”
Hurling his belltopper into a corner, and the
remains of his ‘ ‘ brolly ” after it, and kicking a chair
across the room, he knelt on the floor at a table,
grabbed a pen and a telegraph-form, and wrote:
“ Mafeking relieved. Hurroo. Everybody glori-
ously drunk,” in the most beautiful copperplate
possible. Many a drunk who can’t even think
properly standing up can write quite coherently if
he gets his head down on a level with his hand.
• * * •
“F.” : Judge Williams, in the course of
another of his Pleasant Melbourne Sunday
Afternoon addresses, told his audience that
his own creed or belief was subject to alter-
ation every ten years, and that “ very few
honest, thinking men or women could
truly say that their opinions and beliefs
had not altered.” Judge Williams knows
very little of human nature. The
average man who thinks he has changed
his religious belief never had a belief
before he began to think about the
change in it. And the average woman
accepts her belief as an unalterable
thing from which she is deriving com-
fort. The more uncomfortable it is, the
more she tries to persuade herself that
she is deriving comfort from it.
* * *
A telling commentary on the talk
of Judges and lawyers generally about
Gentleman One’s amenability to the
criminal law over and above his amena-
bility to the Full Court was solicitor E.
R. Abigail’s defence of a limb of the
law in a recent Sydney case. Abigail
urgued that inasmuch as Gentleman
One had been wiped off the roll for a
particular offence, that offence should
not afterwards be brought before a court
of law. The argument is bad, no doubt,
but it has the sanction of common practice.
Nothing occurs in a more marked epi-
demic form than vaccination. A town which gets
it in an aggravated way one year, often has no
trace of it again for a decade or so. According to
the official report made to the Assembly (which,
of course, is desperately interested in such things)
it was Bourke’s turn last year ; of 2015 “ cases ”
which the Govt, vaccinators had for the whole of
N.S.W., that poor, little, woeful township
claimed 837, nearly half of them adults.

the blissful wedding. But there was no wed-
ding, and the dark shadow of a writ came between
the sundered affections.
The Cue (Westralia) Quarter Sessions has
fixed the value of a nose at three months’ gaol.
It was, certainly, an Italian nose—a Grecian
might be cheaper. The ornament was lost during
a small dispute over a timber-cutting contract.

YOUNG AUSTRALIA'S 'CUTE
REASONING.
MILLICENT (aged 3½): “ Sometimes I am a bad
girl, and then you won't give me any lollies,
will you, mamma?"
MAMMA : “ No, indeed."
MILLICENT (after a pause): “Mamma, it's not
sometimes now, is it?" (She got them.)
The happy death of the unfortunate heir
of the Duke of Norfolk—an idiotic atrophied
shadow of a human being from his birth —has
called attention again to the general degeneration
of “aristocratic families” in Europe. This is
specially notable among royal families; there’s
hardly a throne which can cast its shadow to a
comfortable length at sunset without striking a
gibbering idiot; and dynasties on every side are
threatened with extinction because there is no
heir and no chance of an heir. The Pope (who is
a cuter man than even the Rev. Dill Macky gives
him credit for) has noticed this, and, in an effort
to remedy the evil, so far as his church is con-
cerned, “ has issued a Note to the Reigning Houses
of Europe stating that no more dispensations will
be granted for marriages between blood relatives.
He advises that Princes of Blood Royal who belong
to the Roman Church should contract marriages out-
side the Royal families in order that the degenera-
tion and mental inferiority caused by the consan-
fuineous marriages of the past may be remedied.”
n short, a little milkmaid is prescribed for the
monarchy’s constitution. At present the toughest-
looking royal family in Europe is that of Sweden
which calls itself Ponte Corvo, but is really Police
Court —or was only four generations ago, when its
ancestor used to defend the drunk and put in an
alibi for the dog-stealer. Next to it, physically,
may probably be reckoned the royal family of
Obrenovitch, which dealt in pigs so recently that
it is still suspected of keeping a side of bacon
under its throne as something to fall back upon in
case of sudden deposition.

“ Bon ” writes :
Poor old “ Dad ” never comes to town but trouble is his
portion. One of him added to the gaiety of Melbourne
during Show week, thusly : Wishing to cross Swanslon-
street at the Town Hall, he carefully paused till two
trams were approaching each other at a right angle to
his course; then, grasping his courage and a decayed
carpet-bag, he made a frantic effort to race across in front
of them. Arrived in the middle of the down track and
brought to bay by the language of the gripman, he pulled
up dead. So did the tram, with the front of the dummy
gently pressing the base of “ Dad’s ” spine. Then de-
scended on him Nemesis in the shape of his wife Betsy,
fire in her eye and a shapeless “brolly” in her hand.
Grasping “Dad’s” arm, she dragged him in front of a
’bus and then stopped to remonstrate. The ’bus-man’s
language was awful as he pulled up, and just then
“ Dad’s ” carpet-bag fell open, and one of the horses
started coughing into it. Roused to unusual energy,
“ Dad ” shouted to Betsy: “Blast it, ol’ ’ooman, don’t
make sich a fool o’ yerself ! ” Then the circus started.
It was short and swift. One welt with the “hrolly” on
the head, another on the loins, and two vigorous thrusts
in the small ribs, and “ Dad ” once more resumed his posi-
tion as a mere humble incident in the family. The crowd
cheered, and the party again stopped the traffic, while
the lady addressed her audience as “a lot o’ grinnin’
goanners.” She waved her umbrella round her head as
she said it, and it struck another tram-car, and the car
knocked it out of her hand, and it flew sideways and
swept away “Dad’s” hat, and a cab went over both of
them, and as “Dad” dropped the carpet-bag and ran
after the ruins she called after him and denounced him
as an “ ijit.”
