“ The Prooshan ” writes :
Don’t agree with “Sprats” (B. 30/8/’O2) that "female
teachers escape the altar oftener than female followers of
any other trade or profession.” Scores of girl teachers
marry for the very reason that they are teachers and not
barmaids—we’ll say—who practically kill the goose that
lays the golden egg if they marry. Personally I have ob-
served that, wherever Miss “ 2B ” controls the “ Infant ”
school, there is always a Mr. “ 2A ” who, if single, is
mightily prone to ask Miss “2B” to accompany him to
the altar and run another infant school on private lines.
There are certainly some very tough spinsters in the pro-
fession, hut then they would be tough, and sp'insters, in
any profession. In fact, they represent the spinster-
profession.
Protest from a Maoriland visitor to
N.S.W. :
Dear Bulletin.— Training it recently from Bathurst to
Sydney we had in the carriage a man obviously in an
advanced stage of consumption. He coughed and spat
on the floor until he became exhausted ; then took the
glass and filled it with water. But the water causing
him to choke, he coughed right into the glass. Presently
he lay back and seemed to go to sleep, and the day being
warm flies swarmed over his feet and the expectorated
matter on the floor, and every time he moved his feet
they crawled all over us. I was fairly driven to break my
journey at Lithgow. Now, is it any wonder phthisis is
rampant ? Here were four of us cooped up with this un-
fortunate, with all the windows shut and the bacillus-
charged sputum spread over the floor.
“ Cobram ” : The velvet softness of perfect
claret is obtained in Australian wine but rarely ;
and the why and wherefore seem hidden from the
best-informed. I remember once lunching at
Yarra Glen, in the De Castella and De Pury
country, and our host, who had made wine for 40
years, sent up to a neighbor for a dozen bottles of
“ze old wine.” In every bottle of that were
yards of velvet. It was worth £lO a dozen in
London. But why not the same sort every year ?
Ah, why not ? “It came” in that year, and no
more is known about it. Yet soil is the same and
climate varies little in'the Yarra Glen from decade
to decade.
AN OUTLAWESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, eyes of keen desire !
Oh, lips with love abloom !
How slyly they conspire
To set my heart on fire,
And drag me to my doom 1
Oh, she-ness of your wiles!
Oh, he-ness of my heart!
The glee-ness of your smiles
Our we-ness reconciles,
And rapture doth impart 1
But do not swear to me
To be entirely mine
Through life’s monotony—
For love is always free
That swims in eyes like thine !
NARRANGHI BOORI.
“COMFORT YE, MY PEOPLE.”
“ The Harbor Trust is not to blame, neither is the
Government, but it is very easy for others —the Mayor,
for instance—to find fault,” Premier See declares, in ex-
planation of the foreshore resumption fiasco. Mr. Hick-
son, on behalf of the Harbor Trust, does not think the
Government, is greatly to blame, because urgently neces-
sary work for which the money was voted has not been
done. Mr. See, on behalf of the Government, is per-
fectly sure that the Harbor Trust is not to blame.
Nobody, therefore, is to blame for a state of things with
which, as the State Premier rightly says, “it is very easy
to find fault.
“ Oh, pray be calm,
Show no alarm,
And everything you see
Will be, by some mysterious charm,
As right as right can be.
This comfort vague
About the Plague
Should, stop remarks unjust,”
Say the Potentate
Of the hapless State,
And the Boss of the Harbor Trust 1
“ At finding fault
Please call a halt,
Do not attempt to quiz ;
It’s very easy, finding fault
(It is, by Jove, it is !)
We’re anxious still
We should fulfil
The duty that is thrust
Upon this State,”
Say the Potentate
And the Boss of the Harbor Trust 1
“ It’s most unwise
To criticise,
And also most unfair,
And friendly silence we’d advise
As suited to the Mayor 1
If we were rash
To spend the cash
He should not make a dust —
Let’s trust to Fate,”
Say the Potentate
And the Boss of the Harbor Trust!
“ Leave a’l to us,
And don’t discuss
Reports that go the rounds.
’Tis most absurd to make a fuss
About Four Million Pounds 1
It is— oh, well,
A bagatelle !
It need not be discussed 1
It well can wait,”
Say the Potentate
And the Boss of the Harbor Trust 1
Here’s comfort, friend,
For those who lend,
As everyone must own ;
What boots it, friend, how we should spend
This paltry little loan ?
Yet some you know
Incline to show
A feeling of disgust
With the Potentate
Of the Cornstalk State,
And the Boss of the Harbor Trust!
W. T. GOODGE.

UOHANAN WHISKY
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, September 23, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
I write to you with the pen which Melba
used just before leaving Sydney on Friday
evening. What she wrote is in my autograph-
book, and (I am about as sentimental as a buzz-
saw) you may have pen and book and a bunch of
boronia from the Melba corsage for £25,000. The
very last gift Melba gifted before leaving Sydney
and “ The Australia ” was the cake of soap with
which she washed her octave compass—major and
minor fingers. She washed them in the key of
E Minor, staccatto, rallentando, diminuendo ; and
the one who was gifted stood fingering Madame’s
Gobelin-blue bed-hangings and muttering some-
thing about “who wanted the morning star,”
knowing that the soap would presently be hers.
As befitted a star of the first magnitude, Melba
was generous with her rays. “ I expected some-
one that would'dazzle me,” said the soap-blessed
one. “Someone who would be as strange as—■
as .” “Pictures ought not to be too pic-
turesque,” broke in Melba. Then she strolled
meditatively past her sister (golden-haired Mrs.
Patterson) into an adjoining room of her suite and
looked reflectively at Burne-Jones’ “ Mirror of
Venus.” “ Yes,” she continues, “ nothing
astonishes people so much as the absolutely
natural when they expect the abnormal.” ’
As one of the sapient interviewers remarked :
“Melba converses most intellectually.” “Itis a
pity,” said someone, “that you can’t stop to inhabit
these beautifully decorated rooms till—at least till
this lorry-load of flowers fades.” “I take all my
flowers with me to-night,” replied Melba. “I have
a note of the names of all the senders.” And just
then someone with a little trolly came and trollied
away the basket of orchids with a Swinburne verse
tacked to the handle, and the skeleton umbrella
disguised with pink roses, carnations and daffodils,
bearing the card of a Personage, and the lyre of
purple violets with strings of white, and the
wreaths and waratahs and baskets of white lilies,
and the more roses—and another wreath with a
Tennysonian motto. And all the time the girl
with the cake of soap was standing by
looking as if she wanted to suck it. “ I cannot
understand,” said Melba, “how one of your people
could speak of me from London as a Tittle body.’”
“ From the extreme corner of the half-crown niche
you would look like a pollywog doll,” was the
reply, “and it was from that dizzy height he saw
you.” Melba is really about sft. Gin., and not
thin at that. I would not recognise her from her
portraits ; neither did the people at the Redfern
station, when she boarded the Southern express.
Her faqe is strong and calm, somewhat Italian as
to features, non-committal as to expression, of an
olive pallor as to coloring ; and the eyes very
dark, longish ; and with little white showing—
eyes that take one’s measure, and size and
sum one, and are bright as a magician’s.
When leaving, Melba looked unutterably weary.
As the papers said that she was remaining in
Sydney for two days there was no crowd. A group,
attracted by the trolly of floral devices, ranged up.
She came through the barriers dressed in black
and fine sables, with her sister and brother-in-law,
her companion and Mr. Musgrove, and entered
her car . and got behind the curtains. The only
exhilarating items were the efforts of her
personal and managerial staff to keep Mr. Dan
O’Connor out of the car—efforts of such diplomacy
as would have done credit to the representatives
of a Great Power, efforts that were entirely
successful—but the O’Connor’s posy was inside all
the same. Report says that Melba is certainly not
mad with joy over her railway journeying.
A kindly thing Melba did directly on
arriving in Brisbane and reading her letters, was
to tell her manager to reserve seats for a man who
had ridden for a doctor for her when she was sick
on a night 20 years ago. That man has a good
memory (he wrote reminding her) and he cast his
horseflesh upon the long bush track to some
purpose.
That was a ghastly shark item, the story that
prefaced a Sydney reporter’s interview with Melba
at Brisbane. Whilst they awaited the Miowera,
the journalists amused themselves fishing off the
light-ship or pilot-steamer, and one of their catches
was a 12ft. shark. Someone ripped it open from
head to tail, and others chopped off its jaws to get
the teeth; but, instead of sinking, as all
expected, when turned adrift again, the
wretched fish, disembowelled as it was, swam
about and around the vessel. None of us hold a
brief for the shark, of course. That particular
fish, or any of his family, would as soon eat the
men who chopped it up so hideously as it would
the lady whom they awaited. But he wouldn’t
have the indecency to grin over his ugly meal and
describe to his fellow sharks with horrible realism
how the writing man looked “just when he saw
my dorsal fin, the curious feel he had when
between my teeth, and the journalistic wriggles
inside of me.” That his restraint would be in-
stinctive and n conscious has nothing to do with
the case.
Have you seen the new veils of ventilated
chiffon ? White chiffon with tiny ventilators like
fairy button-holes of black, and black chiffon aired
with white holes. Also the complexion veils of
fine black mesh, plain, or patterned over white or
faintly-pink chiffon. To the beholder, the wearer
of the complexion veil looks beautiful; she herself
cannot see the beholder; so this must have been
the kind of veil which Omar struck, and of which
he wrote, “ There was the veil through which I
might not see.”
Flannel flowers, first of the season, such big
ones, unusually big the florists say, are with us.
This has been the dullest September, these florists
allege, for many a long year, and those who bought,
as many of them did, the daffodil yield of certain
nurseries or gardens at the beginning of the
flower season, have lost heavily, because there
have been no balls during race-week. No club
balls or big private entertainments where the
mantel-pieces and bannisters are flower-swathed !
One leading flowery firm had but two small de-
s irative contracts—just for dinner-tables.
A bad oyster swallowed in Melbourne has bee
like to make Max Schluter’s music mute for ever
more—at least, so far as the choir terrestrial is
concerned. He has been ill for some weeks, and,
at his farewell concert last week, his friends feared
a collapse, or fiddling that would do himself injus-
tice. But he bowed through triumphantly. The
financial success of the concert was mainly due to
Mrs. Boesen, the Danish Consul’s wife. Schluter
leaves Australia on Monday, to start his American
tour.
A correspondent tells me: “I know a man
who, prior to leaving Sydney for the Coronation,
held a private sale of his lately-deceased
wife’s personal effects. Setting apart one par-
ticular room of the house, he removed the
furniture and decorated the walls with various
articles of attire. To each garment a price ticket
was attached, and the general effect was like a fine
second-hand shop. Then friends were bidden to
the bargain-sale. Complete costumes went for
half-a-sovereign ; smart hats for half-a-crown ;
underskirts and lingerie .”
Sydney Hunt Club got into trouble last week at
Kensington. The hounds led the hunt into a
most deceptive swamp, and about a dozen of the
huntsmen, including two ladies, were bogged and
covered with foul black mud. The horse 3, in some
cases, had to be pulled out with ropes. The
hounds got away by themselves, no one being able
to follow. One member, in trying to save his
horse, was nearly smothered. If it hadn’t been
for the timely aid of about 100 men with ropes the
Hunt Club might have been lost. The most
serious part of the business was that the sivage
salt-herring which the Hunt was pursuing with
great gallantry, escaped completely.
Sydney University goes a-carnivalling next
week in honor of its jubilee. For a whole week,
morning, noon, and night, it will paint Sydney a
delicate ash-color, and it won’t go home till the
end of the week, let alone till morning. There are
to be lectures, receptions, garden parties, literary
addresses, and conversaziones. Prof. MacCallum
leads off at 11.30 (a.m.) on “University Influ-
ence.” Prof. Anderson Stuart will speak on
“ The Majority of the Medical School.” Look at
that text from any angle, and you will discover no
promise of giddiness in it. Not a vestige of fluff.
Sir Josiah Symon’s lecture (unnamed) will coun-
teract the laok of thistledown in Prof. Stuart’s if
it is on “The Humors of Divorce, with
Notes on Breach of Promise.” There will be
a harbor excursion, but the glowing hours
for flying feet will be when the University
Sports Union has its ball in Sydney Town Hall
on October 2nd. Their Ex’s, the, State Governor
and Vice-Admiral and Ladies Rawson and
Beaumont will be present, and no matter where
you live, you’ll find a special train or tram waiting
to run you home, at any hour you please, after the
ball. The hon. secs., A. G. M. Pitt and C. S.
Browne, are to be caught alive at Selborne Cham-
bers for tickets.
At Sydney Palace Theatre, on Sept. 29th, the
S.U.A.D.S. will play “The Critic,” with Mrs.
Lumsden Hare in the leading part. A curtain-
raiser, “The Threshold,” written by Mr. Norman
Gough, will precede it.
Friends of Miss Irene Marks will be glad to
hear that she has almost recovered from the
recent accident, caused by the bolting of her
hansom-cab horse. She was unconscious for
nearly a week. Mrs. Philip Morton, who was
with her at the time, was unhurt, beyond receiving
a severe nervous shock.
My Brisbane correspondent writes:—
“ All social events have paled into insignificance
before the fact of Nellie Melba’s arrival ‘in the
home land.’ Rumor says that one enthusiastic
admirer has bottled the dingy sand whereon her
royal foot first rested. Nellie has risen superbly to
the occasion—taken our Mayoress’s two hands in
hers, unearthed a ‘ dear local friend ’ (Miss O’Con-
nor, a very modest lady dominie); has signed her
Australian message with becoming flourish in the
lucky reporter’s note book ; has let the tear-drop
fall for ‘dear old dad waiting 14 years for a
glimpse of his girl’; and has posed for the local
cameras for reproduction in the ‘dailies,’ said pro-
duction giving a ‘ fair, fat and forty ’ appearance
which is slanderous and ought to be suppressed
by Act of the Continuous Government.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ A great crowd in the Spencer-st. special pen,
Saturday morning, waiting to how-de-do Melba—
Mayor Gillott; Captain Collins, the band-box
Naval Commander, more titivated than his wife;
Melba’s relatives (the honey-pots on whom the
small social flies descended); Tom Patterson’s
baby girl, tricked forth in fluffy blue and white,
and kissed by men and women in view of the im-
portant fact that she is the diva’s niece.
“ The train is in. The crowd heaves, lunges,
and sways. A robust, brown-and-red-complexioned
face—strong, cheerful, excited —is focussed for a
minute in the State car. The crowd sets up a
cheer. The Mayor shoulders his way forward,
and Melba is recognised. Wide smiles break over
her ruddy face. A fringe of brown curls extends
from ear to ear—after a fashion of 20 years ago, it
seems. A flat, dowdy hat of navy blue, a rather
knocked-out dress of blue and tan, a matronly
figure—and you have Melba.
“Waves of excitement surge up in the diva’s
face. She nearly eats her welcomers. She swoops
down upon the baby niece with a fresh access of
joy. ‘My darling !’she breaks out between two
kisses. She is laughingly steered by Mr. Musgrove
to the carriage-steps. Springs in—heaps of energy
there—and starts the flower-pelting. Bunches
of violets are flung in all directions to a waving corn-
field of hands. Then Lord Dick Nevill is sighted ;
the only V.R'. deputy on the spot. Melba is for
flinging him a daffodill, but hesitates, beckons,
laughs, nods, and repeats her ‘ Thanks, many
thanks ! ’ Then, half-distracted with excitement,
she pelts her purse instead of a bouquet at an
eager hand in the crowd. She discovers her
mistake. The smile disappears. She is in a
comical fright. ‘.My purse—just there! ’ she
tells the sentries in blue. The carriage starts.
Melba still cries out for herpurse. It is recovered,
handed up, and the smiles gush again.
“ The family arrangements pending in some
quarters for the Melba guinea concert-treat take
this shape: Pa and ma go in, take their seats,
hear the first part, then move out at the interval,
and give up their seats to two of their offspring.
Several families have decided to go halves over
the treat in this fashion. There is great grief in
all parts over the purchase of tickets. Clubmen,
who paid hall-porters and waiters lialf-a-guinea
to gather the coupons at midnight, and had to
content themselves with middling seats, find now
the pick of the front seats going at 255. Every-
one fell in.
“ All kinds of compliments have been flying
between George Musgrove and Rupert Clarke of
late, and rumor puts the latter down as the
mysterious ‘ gentleman ’ who has proferred the
third fancy price for a seat at the charity concert
that Melba may give in the Exhibition. Rupert
occupies the box nowadays in pally fashion with
Manager Musgrove.
“The cards for the afternoon kow-tow to Melba
by ‘ her former schoolfellows ’ bear the italicised
words: ‘ Not transferable,’ and all are warned
that a rigorous watch will be set at the door,
and on no account will any absent-minded
person be passed in without the bit of precious
pasteboard. At first, word went that the party
was to be regarded as a private one, and the Press
would not be invited. Later, nice little notes
from Miss Fraser retracted the original veto, and
the Incas were invited to meet the other potentate.
“ I have just been reading that among the com-
petitors at some suburban A.N.A. contests was
yet another gifted sixteen-year-old, whose voice
was so similar in quality to that of Miss Amy
Castles that the adjudicator (Herr de Chaneet)
said that if she appeared in the Melb. Town
Hall in Miss Castles’ clothes, those present would
hardly know the difference. Surely it is about
time Herr de Chaneet ‘found’ a voice or two
like somebody else’s—say lima di Murska’s or
Albani’s. Since the original Castles boom, one Herr
or Monsieur or Signor and another have been
assiduously employed in going round to the small
and harmful A.N.A. competitions and assur-
ing open-mouthed schoolgirls that Amy Castles
was never in the same street with them. There
must be a peculiar quality about the Australian
climate, or something, if every girl soprano has
the same kind of voice, and it bodes badly for
our export singing trade, because London sighs
for novelties where art is concerned, whatever
may be its demand in the way of butter or
rabbits. I have heard a few of these budding
Amys, and they struck me as mostly hard-voiced
youngsters with no more real ‘ sing ’ in them than
a distressed locomotive.
“ Isn’t it about time for this country to discover
a typical Australian kind of ‘popular demon-
stration ? ’ Clearly there is no chance of acclima-
tising the ‘ good old British cheer ’ that did duty
for Australian enthusiasm in the days when
natives were few hereabouts and Europeans were
many. Melba must have been struck by the fact
that, whereas thousands of Melbourne folk took
the trouble to stand around in the streets for the
sake of a stare, they didn’t cheer. The impulse
to shout ‘ Hooray! ’ was lacking, because the
Australian notion of a fitting cry of welcome is
something different from ‘ Hooray ! ’ presuming
that Jim and Joe care to make any noise at all.
At the gates of the Harper residence in Toorak,
which the Woman of the Hour has rented for
her temporary Melbourne home, a little crowd
assembled to see her arrive. If they came to
welcome her, they did it in grim silence. Perhaps
they tried to utter the ‘good old British cheer,’
but couldn’t. ‘ Hooray ! ’ stuck in their throats.
The simple foolishness of that ancient exclamation
stands condemned by the sons of the fathers who
imported the habit. Why not adopt ‘ Bush !’ as
the Commonwealth cry of greeting ? ‘ Bush ! ’
would be better than ‘ Rats ! ’ although ‘ Rats ! ’
seems to express Australian sentiment in general.
“ Most women shudder at their first glimpse of
Sandow, standing on his revolving pedestal,
exercising his muscles for show purposes. The
average woman isn’t anxious to watch the physical
wheels of humanity go round. She is little in-
terested in machinery of any kind, and Man,
revealed as a machine, made up of smaller
machines and furnished with palpable air-pump
and bellows, seems rather repellent.
“Mrs. Sandow, who told an interviewer that
‘Melbourne women have pretty faces, but walk
badly, ’ knows now that our folk are impatient of
criticism. One of them voiced the feelings of all
when she said audibly coming out of the Opera
House t’other night, ‘ Too bad that Sandow’s wife '
is not on show ! After what she said, I do want to
see that woman walk.
“Dr. Hay, Moderator of the Presbyterian
Federal Assembly, now sitting here, is a notable
specimen of Scottish grit and energy. Put to
work for his living at ten, he struggled on till he
won his M.A. degree, donned his Geneva gown
and ‘ wagged his head in a poupit.’ Then the
fagged scholar was laid aside with lung disease.
After a year’s helplessness his doctors told him to
try Queensland as his last chance. Friends tried
to dissuade the apparently dying man, with his
delicate wife and young children, from attempt-
ing what seemed a forlorn hope. But the
invalid went and, after more than 30 years in
tropical Queensland, he is still very much alive.
He has been constantly to the fore in the northern
Kirk, has annexed his D.D. degree, seen his
children grow up, and now in his old age still has
bairns to feed in the persons of his orphan grand-
children.
‘ ‘ Mr. Guennett, formerly musical critic of the
Argus, was a big, dark, lean Englishman, who
had worked and battled hard all his days. In his
youth a favorite pupil of Charles Halle, he was a
sound musician in a quiet, non-sensational way.
In Melbourne’s palmy days he toiled from dawn
to dark as teacher, church organist, and promoter
of classic concerts, making much money, and, like
everyone else, losing much of his hard-earned
savings in subsequent lean years. His deafness,
which added so much to the labors of his musical
criticism, was the result of an injury to his head,
which left him insensible for weeks. Guennett
taught Melba the piano in the long ago, and was
very proud of his old pupil’s fame and of the
splendid cigarette-case that the cordial ‘ Nellie
sent him. He died a few weeks before her
return, to which he was looking forward almost as
eagerly as her own people.
“ Two V.R. suites were among the swagger con-
tingents that dragged it to the Findon Harriers’
meeting on Saturday. Sassiety hasn’t been fore-
gathering much of late, and this outing saw many
resurrections. Among the frilly people, the
pleasant, brown-costumed figure of Lady Tenny-
son stood out. Her Ex. moved about, in the
easiest and most natural way, chatting and
laughing with all. The Gov. -General, on the
other hand, was large and slow, heavy and dull as
a German on tour.
“The State V.R.’s stayed till the last race.
Lady Silverlocks sat with the Waxellency dame
awhile in the carpeted V.R. tent—the latter
posing like a duchess, and talking like a haughty
beauty. Miss Lesly Madden was in black and a
heavy Polar cape, and flourished many rings on
her bare hands. Mrs. Pursey Chirnside spoilt a
pretty little black-and-white turn-out by excessive
primness. Mrs. Everard Brown erred on the
other side. She was too jaunty in a coachman’s
rain-proof cloak, and a big, flat, feathered hat,
worn at a sport-like angle. An immense lady
went on parade in a theatrical cloak and trailing
skirt of coral-pink.
“Rupert Clarke had run his ‘Fairy States’ jn
Nellie Stewart’s name, and, as luck would have it,
the gentle steed carried off the first of the
Rupertswood Stakes. Lady Tennyson was
switched on for the presentation of trophies, and
all necks were craned to see who would appear on
behalf of Sweet Nell. Rupert blushingly came
forward and took charge of the beautiful diamond
gewgaw himself.
“In this season of economy, the only brave
soul battling with bills for entertainments seems
to be the mistress of ‘ Rippon Lea.’ Her musical
At Home, last Tuesday, was a very frou-frou
crush. Lady Sargood herself was simply habited
in a homely blue, with fur and lace postscripts.
The gentle daughter of the house cut a quiet, but
rather sweet, figure in spotless cream. Grey-
haired women were more decorated than their
frilly daughters.
“This is the age of perennial youth. One
sparky angel of about 60 wore, at ‘ Rippon Lea,’ a
very lively blue, with whisks of white lace and
chiffon fronting the corsage, a coquettish ciel
blue tulle rosette posted under her shell-like ear,
and a gold toque, with a high jaunty dogcart kind
of perch, on her white waving locks. The borrowed
roses in her cheeks vied with an eye-sparkle that
would not shame a lime-lit soubrette.
“ Nellie Stewart off the boards, these days, cuts
a sombre figure. She dresses in full mourning
and much crape, drives about in a closed carriage,
and presents the saddest little countenance any-
one could imagine the sparkling actress capable
of. Bad luck has followed old Dick Stewart’s
death, and the good-hearted Nellie has been
nursing mother and sister in the day, while per-
forming every evening.
“In a recent unsavory case the question of the
dress that is sent on ‘appro.’ came up. Man and
his makers (i.e., tailors) little know the trafficking
between chiffon firms and their female customers.
A woman (say) sends for a nice 15-guineas theatre-
cloak on appro. It is sent, worn on a special
occasion, and returned in cataracts of tissue-paper
and its original folds next day. A ’phone message
explains: ‘So sorry —not at all my fit.’ And the
firm shakes its fist behind its ledger in impotent
rage. The ‘ smart ’ woman who finds herself short
of cash often resorts to such tricks. A costly
blouse is got home on ‘appro.’ It is worn for the
party of that occasion. Next morning a haughty
’phone or written message requests the firm to
send for the blouse. Rugs, counterpanes,
portieres, all kinds of furniture, as well as articles
of adornment, are got temporarily on this ticket,
and, when they have served the special occasion,
returned with regrets that ‘ they did not suit.’
“A Melb. woman who dabbles a bit in Ex-
change deals for female clients possesses a cattery
out St. Kilda way. There is money in the hobby,
and the cat-grower encourages the habit of large
feline families.
Y ours affectionately,
AKENEHI.
“ Vic.” : The worst of the drought is that
small holders will resolve to sell out on the first
opportunity. The capitalist will mop up their
property and build big estates. Then his friend,
the parson, will pull the string of the shower-
bath.
T’other day, a N.S.W. squatter rushed
10,000 drought-stricken sheep over into Vic.,
pastured them for a week, and then sent them
down to the Melbourne wool companies’ sheds to
be shorn. The wool off, they were turned into
the yards, and that night a cold snap came along.
Next morning, over 1500 skin-and-bone carcases
were found piled up in a corner of the yard—six
deep. So far, nobody has been able to fix the
responsibility.
A graceful form and well moulded bust
are essential to perfect beauty. Lacti , the
wonderful “Skin Food” is nature’s remedy for
giving back those essentials when lost through
sickness or action of time, and for supplying them
when constitutionally deficient. It fills all hollows,
develops and gives a charming roundness to neck

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Obit. : Mr. E. M. Fisher, one of Hobart’s
ancient ship owners, and one of the few who made
money since the departure of the whale from Tas.
waters.
“Charlie'’ Hall, M.H.A. for the V.D.L.
Co.’s territory, Tas., who moved the “secesh”
motion in the Assembly, stood for the last Federal
election —and lost his deposit.
Gordon Stronach, editor of the Sydney
Echo some years ago, has been appointed secretary
to the Royal Commission which has the over-
hauling of the Westralian public service.
Obit. : At Geelong (Vic.), Jas. G. Carr,
aged 83 years. He arrived in Hobart in ’3l and
Port Phillip in ’45. For several years he sat in
Vic. Leg. Council for South Grant and Geelong
West.
Obit. : Fat O’Brien, of Cowwarr (Vic.),
aged 74. He was one of the original prospectors
of the old Walhalla claims, Gippsland, where the
great Long Tunnel has its being. He died a
wealthy pastoralist.
Mr. John Bede Polding Dailey has been
appointed Crown Prosecutor at Goulburn
(N.S.W.) at coming assizes. The first time in 50
years that the name of Dailey has appeared for
the Crown in a criminal case.
Canon Henry Lowther Clarke is to be the
new Bishop of Melbourne. Yet another Clarke !
Melbourne owns 3 Lady Clarkes, 2 Sir Clarkes,
besides numerous offshoots of the great families,
and now the newly-appointed Anglican Bishop is
a Clarke.
Mr. Lee was forced out of the Opposition
leadership more by worry than by any definite
physical disease. Worry is a thing which no
doctor can cure, and Mr. Lee, after a long
struggle, has had to confess himself unfitted by it
for arduous political work.
Monsignor O’Connor stands first in the
list of three candidates proposed to act as coadju-
tor to and ultimately succeed R.C. Bishop Tor-
reggiani, of Armidale. If, as is likely, the Pope
appoints him, the last of the non-Irish R.C.
Bishops will in time depart from N.S.W.
New Queensland Commissioner for Rail-
ways Thallon stands sft. Bin. ; is 55 years of age ;
measures about 45in. round the chest, with a pro-
jecting lower region; speaks broad Scotch, as
all railway experts should ; is fond of shooting,
bowls, chess, and likes to see a good horse-race.
He is a hard worker, but that goes without saying.
Every stout railway boss with a Scotch accent is.
Jimmy Ah Lin, of Cairns, has 800 acres
under sugar-cane, pays wages and expenses
amounting to £ll,OOO per annum, and is the most
slavishly-worshipped, yellow plutocrat in N.
Queensland. Jimmy was one of those Chinese
Cairnsites who once sent in a petition to Bob
Philp wherein they described themselves as the
“Backbone of Cairns !”
SACRILEGE
HIS GREAT PATRON : “ Hullo! I say, Willoughby, ‘heavy
loan burden ,’ eh! What does this mean? What
are you running down loans for? Do you think

Arthur Russell, a well-known Melbourne
dentist, who graduated in the legerdemain busi-
ness of yore, was the first Australian thereabouts
to practise with the grip dumb-bells. He brought
a pair from London, when they were the latest
novelty, and is now qualified to pull a tusk from
an elephant.
Premier Jenkins Iscariot, of S.A., was 51
on September 8. Coming to Adelaide a com-
pletely unknown American book : canvasser in 1878
he was in Parliament within nine years, and has
stopped there ever since. He was made a Minister
about eleven years ago, and since then has hardly
ever been out of office.
That unfortunate epileptic spinster who
died lately in Victoria, after spending the best
part of 17 years confined in a shed, was the
daughter of a clergyman, and the man whose shed
she enjoyed has two brothers in the Church.
There seems to be some sort of a moral about this,
though it is hard to dig out.
“D.H.” : Magistrates at Tamata, British
Vew Guinea (1) Green killed, (2) Shanahan
died of fever, (3) Armitt died of general break-
down of the system, (4) A. L. Walker died at
iSamarai, June 10, 1902, of blackwater fever.
They are all dead but “ Crow,” and he fled ; and
whether he’s alive or not is more than I can say.
Captain Staughton, the member for Bourke
West in Vic. State Parliament, who recently
inherited that supposed “pocket constituency”
from his father, is at present on his way from
England with the Coronation Contingent. He
left behind a signed nomination paper, and his
friends will test his chance of election in his
absence.
Old Jimmy Munro, of the many dead
banks, ex-Premier, ex-Agent-General, ex-boomer,
made a half-hearted that Victoria
sliould take him back at the present elec-
tion. And, sad to say, a time seems to be
coming when the country might do worse than
take back the dilapidated wreck and wraith of
Jimmy. It is a sorrowful thing, but old backwash
James is a better democrat than most of the crowd
that is coming to the top iust now.
One result of the suddenness of the Irvine
Ministry’s resignation will be the loss of Deegan
to Victorian politics for the present, if not for all
time. In his blissful ignorance of what the future
had in store, Deegan tripped to Maoriland three
weeks ago, and can’t get back in time to stand
again for Melbourne East. The Vic. Assembly
has lost in Deegan a fine assortment of quotations
from the Immortal Bard.
The Forbes (N.S. W.) Crown land-agent (a
correspondent alleges) was transferred, a month or
two ago, to Orange, and died after an illness of
about five days. The Dept., when accounting for
his salary, reckoned the amount only up-to the
last day he had signed at the office, docking him
for the five days he was dying, and thus ‘ ‘ chiselled ”
his unfortunate widow out of a few pounds. It
seems too mean to be true.
Seddon's speech about the utter wrongness
of the proposal to invite the Boer generals to the
Coronation (it was delivered at a banquet of the
Tallow Chandlers’ Company where grease seems
only natural) evoked a casual sort of reply in the
Pall Mall Gazette. The substance of the reply
was that Seddon was himself the King’s guest,
and that a guest, if he is a gentleman, doesn’t
lecture his host about the selection of his other
guests.
Melbourne gets a family man for Bishop
at last. Rev. H. Lowther Clarke, who steps into
Goe’s shoes, has a quiver full, there being nine or
ten sons and daughters. The dailies have already
discovered all the ordinary virtues in the bishop-
elect, beside which he is devoted to cricket and
other British institutions. Neither Moorhouse
nor Goe had a family, but they were never poor
curates, whereas Clarke was moderately poor for
some years.
T. H. Guenett, Melb. musician, and long
the Argus musical eritio, just dead, claimed the
proud distinction of being the unluckiest man that
ever bestrode a bicycle. When the bike boom
was on, Guenett was never out of slings and
bandages. One day he rode up alongside a well-
known pressman in Swanston-street, and said :
“By jove, do you know, I’ve ridden into five
people within the last quarter of an hour.” Just
then, bang came a knickerbockered cyclist into
Guenett, and a woful smash and tangle resulted.
Guenett drew his great length out of the ruins,
and, holding his nose to stop the blood, said:
“Well, this is a pleasant change. I’ve been run
into! ”
Charles Winnecke, who died in his middle
age at Adelaide t’other day, led the Horn scientific
explorers to Centralia in ’94. The party’s best-
known find was the diprotodon remains, which,
later on, added a letter or two to the titles of
Prof. E. C. Stirling, South Australia’s premier
scientist. Winnecke’s own work, apart from
surveying, covered a very wide field ; and, if he
was not a leader in any branch of science, he did
very useful work in many—especially in botany
and ornithology. He was, on the whole, more
popular among the natives than among the whites,
for he had very decided opinions about the rela-
tions of the two races on the outer fringe of
civilisation.
Ex-Premier Cockburn (S.A.)
has evidently made up his mind to
stay in London, for he is steadily
adding to the directorships which
he is accepting. He will ere long
become a veritable “guinea pig”—
a sad fate for the democratic idealist
of the old days.
Bluff and kind Captain John
Mackay, discoverer and founder of
Sugaropolis, is now port-master of
Brisbane, and chairman of the
Marine Board. In the early seven-
ties he startled the cotton planters
of Fiji by delivering their mails with
a fast clipper brig in a time seldom
exceeded by the steamers of to-day;
indeed once, with Bishop Selwyn
and the writer on board, he arrived
at Levuka some hours under five
days from Auckland, beating
H.M.9. Cormorant and an Ameri-
can vessel by three days. Capt.
Mackay never refused a passage
to a dead-beat from Auckland or
Sydney. In the early eighties, the
MTlwraith Government having
awarded him 1000 acres sugar-land
for his services as a pioneer, he gave
up the sea ; but Griffith repudiated
the grant.
Tom Pearce, the hero of the
Loch Ard wreck near Port Camp-
bell (Vic.), in ’7B, is now shore
superintendent at Jamaica for the
Royal Mail Steamship Co. His
son, bearing the same name, recently reached
Melb. as an apprentice on the ship Loch Venna-
char. Capt. Bennett, of that vessel, made un-
successful application for a position on the Loch
Ard before she started on her fatal voyage. The
old tragedy seems to stick to the family in one
aspect and another.
“ N.” : Commissioner
Howard, formerly head of the
Salvation Army in Australia,
is here again, sent by General
Booth to inspect the accounts.
Col. Barker, who launched the
cause in Melbourne, sent home
such brilliant reports that the
General feared he would get
swelled head. So Ballington
Booth was sent out to super-
sede him, and Howard came
to bear-lead Ballington.
Barker and . Ballington
couldn’t hit it at all. Barker
turned rusty, and entrenched
himself in possession of the
property. He was loyal to
Booth, senior, but not to
Booth, junior. Ballington was
recalled. The General made
a kind of compromise.
Howard was made head, but
Barker was to do as he liked
in the Social Branch. Howard
and Barker got on well. The
General wheedled Barker
away. Howard may possibly
become General of the whole
Army. He has done useful
work everywhere. A remark -
ably suave, un-Army like
gentleman.
Adelaide people have
discovered a Crimean veteran,
James Arthur, of Glenelg, who
is alleged to have won the
Victoria Cross before Sebasto-
pol in June, 1855. He is in
poor circumstances, like most
of his kind, but his wife is still
able to keep the family pot
boiling by taking in washing.
He sold his Cross when he was
discharged from the army,
long ago, and in a recent issue
of London Times its re-sale,
“in a state of good preserva-
tion,” for £47, was notified.
There are three “silent”
men in Vic. politics—Stanley,
Dyer, and Spiers. The first,
a bluff Irishman from Hor-
sham, is always to be found
in the Assembly, if any funis
going on, but contents himself
with a rich, infectious chuckle.
Dyer hedges his unwilling-
ness to speak behind a pre-
tence that he doesn’t want to
waste time. Spiers was once
put up to second something. He said: “Mr.
Speaker, I am—that is, I have, er—er —good
Lord,” or something of that sort, and he has ever
since been too scared even to interject.

Capt. H. .G. Neave, of the Royal Warwick-
shires, murdered in Madras lately by a trooper,
hailed from Christchurch (M.L.), where he was
educated. He graduated at Sandhurst in ’92, and
obtained his captaincy in ’99. A brother of
deceased was killed in the battle of Paardeberg,
S’Africa.
Rev. C. S. Dennis, an American evangelist,
is looked up in gaol for assault on a youth, who is
expected to die from his injuries. The preacher
had just concluded a prayer, in which he beseeched
his hearers to forgive their enemies and lead up-
right Christian lives, when some lads threw several
handfuls of pebbles into the room. Dennis sprang
from the pulpit, and rushed into the street.
Wrenching a burning gasoline lamp from its
fastenings in front of the door, he hurled it at the
crowd of retreating boys. The lamp struck one
of them, exploded, and enveloped the youngster
in flames.
Labor-member Kidston, who has been
leading Queensland Opposition during the illness
of Browne, is a Scot with a big bump of combat-
iveness and considerable opinion of himself. He
is a keen critic, and a ready, although somewhat
ponderous, debater, and if he has his price it
hasn’t been discovered yet. If Kidston was just
a little more human, and a little less Presbyterian
in his habit of mind, he would make a fair leader
in his present company. The Ministry dislikes
him greatly, which is something to Kidston’s
credit.
The clearing away of the police quarters
in Phillip-st., Sydney, revives the old story about
Vincent Wallace and his imperishable “Mari-
tana.” There are probably a good many places
in and around Sydney in which Wallace resided
during his time in Australia, amongst them being
the house on the corner of Phillip-st., but the claim
of every abode as being the seat of the musical
score of “ Maritana ” is much too strong. Wallace,
as likely as not, put his germs of musical thought
to paper in his native Ireland, and touched his
notes up here, there and everywhere.
PARIS NESBIT, K.C., OF ADELAIDE.
Late Melb. attorney Albert Read left
England in ’s2—bolted, as a matter of fact, with
a ward in Chancery. The Chancellor, in those
days, took his High Court of Chancery even more
seriously than he does now, and he who married
the Court’s nice girl-ward without the Court’s per-
mission “got slops” if the Court could capture
him. He was imprisoned for an indefinite period,
and talked to death by the Court at intervals,
and brought up before the Court, and sent down
again, and asked if he fully realised the enormity
of his offence, and otherwise knocked endways.
They are making a row in Maoriland
because it has been discovered that Premier
Seddon’s brother is an inspector of noxious weeds.
That brother is unfortunate. Had he been a
brother in the days of the Continuous Ministry
he would have been an Under-Secretary at the
very least. There is no reason why Seddon’s
brother shouldn’t be an inspector of noxious weeds
if he knows a weed and pursues it swiftly. The
Continuous Ministry, in the days of itsown power,
packed the public offices with relatives and
hangers-on, and used to boast openly that no
matter what Govt, came in it owned the Civil
Service, and its brother there would block innova-
tions by a process of passive stonewalling till it
came back to office. Seddon and M‘Kenzie
spent nearly 12 months sacking the old Tory
Ministry’s brother out of the largest wooden
building in the world before it could get any real
work done.
Vie. Treasurer Shiels says the railway
expenses are too great, and that economy must be
the order of the day. Yet he is one of the most
expensive customers or deadheads the Railway
Department has. When he travels from Mel-
bourne to Casterton, the chief town of his elec-
torate, or vice versa , his relations, horses, goods
and chattels, appurtenances, household gods,
retinue, and smelling-bottle are numerous and
extensive enough to almost form a small train. A
special order goes forth that Mr. Shiels’ horses
are on the train, and that special care must be
taken to prevent damage, &c. On the journey in
either direction the horse-trucks are turned two
or three times in order that the pampered beasts*
heads may be kept the same way. The time lost
in this extra shunting (which is not done for any-
body else) has to be made up by the driver. It
cost very little more to carry Turner round the
country all the five years of his Premiership than
it does to hump his long, faint, affected successor
once to Casterton and back.

BUSINESS INSTINCT.
SMALL Boy (to bolting cyclist): “Hi, mister, if yer don’t come back
can I ’ave the cap ?”
“ Bobrid ” : In reply to “ W.L.” (8.,
IS/9/*O2), concerning bunny’s habits: I merely
stated what I actually saw, not what I thought.
Which makes a difference when dealing with
rabbits, or kings, or other current matters. On
Erin vale station (N.S.W.) trappers showed me
scores of gravid does, but no “ kittens ” were ever
seen; this was from March to July. Soon after
the trappers left the “youngsters” began to ap-
pear : no change in pasture, which was as
dry as a Presbyterian sermon. All of which
looks as if trapping had some effect on
bunny’s family affairs. That the rabbit has
an “aversion to a trap” is well-known amongst
professional trappers, so much so that great care
has to be exercised in setting a trap to avoid
arousing suspicion. Let a novice try his hand at
rabbit-trapping, and he’ll find bunny knows more
about the game than he does. Bunnry will
avoid a burrow where a dead rabbit is—this I have
proved. Evidently the Bathurst rabbits and
rabbit “ experts ” are a bit behind the times !
From Nyngan to Condobolin, the recent
rains have done very little good, except to put
water into the tanks. The country is still as bare
as a doorstep, and as hard as iron ; the roots are
apparently dead. The poor cockie on his 1280-
acre block is in a pitiable way, with his sheep
dead, and no crops for years. This year, he has
no possible hope of a crop, as the wheat is only
just beginning to shoot, whereas it should be at
least a foot high. As he cannot do anything for
himself, he works for the squatter, skinning
and plucking wool off dead sheep, or else scrub-
cutting. Most of these “selections” are now
reverting back to the stations, as the cockies are
unable to make a living on them, and the squatter
gets them for a song. One 10,000 acres, which
was resumed some few years ago, has all gone
back to the station, bar 2560, and the same thing
is to be found in the whole of the Nyngan and
Condobolin districts. Every man is trying to get
away at the best advantage. These cockies have
nothing but a few fowls, perhaps a pig, and
sundry frames of horses (not a cow to be seen).
How they keep their wrecks alive is a mystery.
I saw one man, who owned five equine skeletons,
give each one a pannikin of wheat for a night
feed. One cockie was knocking out a pound or
two poisoning crows. He had a unique way of
doing it. After skinning a sheep he placed a ball,
made of fat and phosphorous, in the empty eye-
socket, which the crow eagerly devours. He had
over 1000 heads, killed in this manner; twopence
is allowed for each head. Birds are very easily
caught, and, on Melrose run, scores of magpies
and minahs are to be seen carrying large medallions
round their necks, made out of the lids of jam
tins and pieces of leather —they seem quite proud
of them, too. A soul-weary rabbit dwells about
here also, and, with the grasshopper, devours
everything that tries to make an appearance
through the soil. Go on the land, young man !

A ten-year-old horse, bred and reared in
the Bamawm (Vic.) district, was sold and
trucked to Kyneton, 140 miles down the line.
Within four days it was back at the slip-rails of
its old paddock.
Dear Bulletin.—
Some of your correspon.
dents doubt that swag-
gies play the pedlar on
their routes, and ad-
vance reasons for the
why and wherefore of
their thinking. The
time is just coming on
when all Vic. towns
within two days’ tramp
of the Murray will be
infested with the ped-
dling swaggies. Camp-
ing at the river-bend
nearest the town, he
dumps “ Matilda, ” and
sets off on his quest for
the raw material, in the
shape of calves’ hoofs,
soogee bagging, pieces
of string, necks of broken
bottles, turkeys’feathers,
&c., &c. A few days
(sometimes hours) after,
the craftsman of the
road goes forth with his
speciality. Perhaps it
is a pair of beautifully-
finished calf -hoofs,
mounted in velvet as
pin-cushions; a bunch of
flowers, or a plant (in an
improvised cardboard
pot) wonderfully made
up of dyed feathers, and
a credit to the faker ;
a waterbag made of
cadged canvas, with glass
outlet; a ship majesti-
cally rigged out, en-
sconced in a brandy-
bottle (how it got there
is the mystery); puzzles
of various kinds made
by cutting cast-off play-
ing - cards .in various
shapes to be put together
as squares, triangles,
&c., &c. ; butter-coolers
and safes made of butter-
cloth, &c.; fans, fantasti-
cally carved and twisted
out of soft pine-wood ;
bullocks’ horns taste-
fully polished and
mounted as hat-racks;
chair - cushions made out of soogee bagging
stuffed with straw; fly - catchers wonderfully
fashioned and festooned out of gaily - colored
tissue papers; paper-knives and scarf-rings
worked and carved out of knuckle and shin-bones,
and so on. Last year struck a swaggie who would
take a cigar-box lid, and on it paint the landscape
before him, dispose of it for a drink, and paint
another, till tired Nature laid him low—but he
was an artist, not a swagman.—Nymsie.

“ Tonbur ” : A station in Goondiwindi
(Q.) district has 30 Chinamen ring-barking.
Chows are employed at all kinds of work about
there. The people of Texas take after our friends
in Cairns, and lease land to the heathen for
tobacco-growing. A nice mongrel population is
growing up in those parts. Three sisters are
married to a Hindu, a Chow, and an aborigine
respective] . Scores of men are camped on the
Macintyre River, existing on cod ; no use travel-
ling now—while the work is given to the alien.
A GREATER THAN SANDOW.
THE YOUNG 'UN (excitedly): “This’ere Sandow
can lift a man of list, and sling him onto a
horse with one hand.”
THE OLD 'UN (calmly): “ Well, I ain’t never
seen a horse with one hand, but I’ve often
lifted a bullock weighing more’n that and
slung it over the border without making any
fuss about it.”
“ Indra ” on artesian bores :
Re “ F.M.” “ speaking to the point” (B. 13/9/’O2), also
all these other fellows who talk about irrigating with
bore water. What I have seen of it is just this :It will
grow anything A 1 for the first year ; next year it’s
moderate ; next year it moderates some more, and so on,
till it won’t grow a durned thing. There’s too much
mineral in the water, and it seems to take all the sub-
stance out of the ground ; and if you flood, it cakes the
surface like cement. As to the hardening properties—l
know of a man who built a mud house, obtaining mud
from a hole filled by bore. He built up the walls, leaving
the windows and door to be cut out when dry. Well, he
hadn’t got any dynamite and fuse, and it took him longer
to knock out a door and three windows than it did to
build the house and put a roof on it, not to mention wear
and tear of picks, which, when they didn’t break, would
turn round and look at him. Some people swear by
Ohinamen for gardening. One of ’em said, re artesian
water : “ One yea’ welly goo’, allee same, glow high ; too
yea’, not much goo’; tlee yea’, no plully goo’ at all.”
Artesian water must be used moderately and tactfully,
or your magnificent crop the first year will be the only
one.

“ Wyoming ” : Mr. Clias. Capel, of Gorian
Station, N.S.W., was astonished, on recently
post mortem-ing some of his wethers which had
died unaccountably, to find that their stomachs had
been transformed into miniature grass paddocks,
growing trefoil luxuriantly, with the roots em-
bedded in every crevice of the walls of the
stomach. The only explanation forthcoming is
that, for some months, the sheep had been existing
principally upon such grass-seeds and roots as
they could pick up.
“X.X.X.X.”: Some
queer things happen in our
country. Here is one: In
Gormanston (Tas.) last winter
lived a man with a wooden leg.
He tried to bring home his load
of timber one night, but had
overloaded with something
drawn from the wood. He got
into one of the principal
thoroughfares of that miserably
bleak town on the mountain top,
when his timber leg became
fast in the clay. Man walked
round the leg till he got tired.
Then he went to sleep standing
up. Next morning an early
riser saw a man standing in the
street. Thought him rather
quiet. Went up and spoke to
him. Dead ! Verdict—expo-
sure.

“ Bush Boy ” : To “ T.J.G.” (8., 9/7/’O2)
about rearing pheasants. I have tried and failed,
but I know of a man at Wood’s Point (Vic.) who
reared four of these birds by fencing-in a |-acre
at the head of a gully. He had them very tame.
They would imitate almost any sound. He was
going to “travel” with them, when someone
poisoned them.
“ A.P.R.” : The “roasting”
which a bushie receives in the city
is trivial compared to that bestowed
on the city man out-back. After
a week’s sojourn on a station the
new-chum is ready to disbelieve any-
thing, and life becomes a weary
burden that lie might shed any time
without feeling sorry. A Technical
College wool-sorter, who had never
been among sheep, went up North
some years ago, and got along quite
nicely until he asked the shed boss
how many lambs a sheep usually
had at a litter. That query will
follow him to his dying day. Horse-
gear always puzzles a “chum”; he
will ride along with a surcingle
strapped around his horse’s chest,
and then wonder what “those other
idiots ” are grinning at. He is con-
tinually overhearing alarming con-
versations, and wouldn’t
to find that his own shadow had
invented a sinister practical joke to
explode upon him at the first oppor-
tunity.
* * *
The Hindu hawker is as
numerous as ever, though he doesn’t
look it. He has found that when
a procession of six men, each with
the same yellowish-brownish face
in front and the same heap of trash
behind, calls at the same farm-house
inside two or three days, only one
does business, as a 'rule—the other
five merely draw attention to the
increasing accursedness of the
spindle-shanked brown curse. So
in many districts the brown curse has come amicably
together and divided up the land into beats, and
each brown sticks to his own beat. As a result,
lie does the same business with less walking and
less waste of boots and feet, and only one greasy
head salaams at the door where six used to salaam,
and the alarm about the size of the nigger invasion
is wearing off.
* • » *
Gladstone (Q.) is awaking at last. In the
good old days sucli vessels as visited the port
always arrived to time, and the people knew
exactly when to expect them. Just once, a
steamer dropped in “ permiskus-like,” and finding
nothing alive on the wharf but a tired dog,
whistled again. Presently there appeared an
ancient with a sleepy and injured expression of
countenance. Captain (loq.): “Where the devil
are all the people ? Races on—or what ? ” The
Ancient; “ Races ! The mayor or someone giv a
arf ’oliday for summat or other and all of us turned
into bed. Shake ’em up with another whistle.”

“H. Mac. : Remarkable that cockatoos and
galahs can distinctly sound “f” without the upper
mandible touching the lower ; whereas a crow, a
lyrebird, or a magpie cannot, to my knowledge,
sound the “f” at all'. T.he “f” sound is very
common among Australian birds, more particu-
larly among the genus Sitella.

No man is a reliable authority on his own
wife or his own dog. At Rutherglen (Vic.),
t’other day a large Newfoundland, which its owner
insisted was an entirely benevolent and well-
meaning animal, attacked a little girl, and worried
her so severely that her life is despaired of.

PROGRESS.
SHIRE PRESIDENT (to distinguished visitor as the horse
negotiates another stump): “Yes, gentlemen, 50 years
ago there was nothing to be seen on this spot, as far as
the eye could reach, but gum-trees.”
One pathetic effect of the drought is that
when approaching a belt of timber from a plain
half the trees seem to be blossoming, when, as a
matter of fact, they are white with death. On
the Castlereagh the kookaburras lie dead in heaps
(perhaps for lack of anything to laugh at). Most
of the scrub wallabies are so poor they can be run
down on foot. The grey landscape is dotted
thickly with yellow sheep skins, hanging on logs,
fences —everywhere. The recent rain has hardly
made a shoot.
NO MONARCHS NEED APPLY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ King Micky (Illawarra) applied recently to the
Premier, through the member for the district, for the
old age pension. The Premier replied, however, to the
effect that the present state of the law prevented his
Majesty’s request being complied with.”
It is not seemly that this nation grand,
This-sceptred Southern Queen,
Should let its monarchs wander through the land
Without a bean.
What if the powers they wielded long ago
Are dead and bygone things?
For out* own sake we should not play it low
On these poor kings.
We did not know the Great Diprotodon ;
The Pouched Lion too]
Into the dust, unseen by us, has gone ;
The Kangaroo
Remains, with some small remnant of the race
Whose flag will soon be furled,
That made this continent the weirdest place
In all the world.
Deal gently with them. There will come a day
When men—beyond a doubt —
In speaking of that remnant small will say—
We wiped it out.
What would it cost this mean State Government,
That at the fallen jeers,
To keep King Mick in comfort and content
In his last years i
The wages of an office-boy to sweep
The shop of carrot-merchant See ;
King Mick in flour, and baccy too, would keep,
And rum and tea.
But unto John, it seems—to end this pome—
No monarchs need apply
Since he was not invited to go ’Ome
In last July.
DRSEVE ROE.

A PROUD DISTINCTION.
NED: “Hello! So Sis ez give yer the chuck, eh?”
DENNY: “Well, yer can't blame 'er fer slingin' the likes o’ me.
It turns out ez her father used to serve Melba with bacon.”
“ Yatu ” Recently came across a Kanaka
albino—a 17-year girl, native of Aoba, or Leper’s
Isle, New Hebrides. Her parents are of
the true Melanesian brown, but she i i
white as any European. Her hair (or
rather wool), eyelashes and eyebrows arc
•snow-white; and were it not for her
broad, flat nose, thick lips, and large,
straight mouth, she could not be distin-
guished from a European albino. Her
skin does not sunburn, freckle or tan—
it simply reddens, and whitens down
again during the night. Her eyes are
unfitted for seeing well in the sunlight,
but are clear blue at night, as I saw them
by candle-light. She is elaborately tat-
tooed in blue on back, breast, arms and
legs.

♦ * * *
That horse (mentioned in recent Bulletin)
that ate the wool off a dead sheep must surrender
his laurels to a Western cow, which (as reported
by local paper) follows the opossum-shooters
round jind feeds upon the skinned opossums—
waxing, fat and sleek on the diet. The Western
Cow and the Western horse will, in a few more
drought years, be classified as carnivorous.
“A.R.”: During a re-
cent grasshopper plague I
noticed the ground was liter-
ally spotted with groups of
these insects, and, on exami-
nation, invariably observed
the following : —ln the oentre
of each group sat a large grass-
hopper. On each side of his
“neck” rested the heads of
two of his comrades; their
bodies trailing off from his at
right angles. Periodically the
latter uplifted their hind-legs
in ‘a careful, mechanical sort
of way, and then replaced
them. This went on indefi-
nitely, and the rest of the
group (running from 3 to 20,
and apparently mere specta-
tors) looked on, in a calm,
judicial fashion. I never
noticed any of them slipping
away for a drink, though the
ground was fairly blistering
with heat. Is this a religious
ceremony, or something con-
nected with the Masonic
grasshoppers, or what ?
• * *
If you want to prosper
in any town out West, don’t
pay your way. Paying your
way is fatal. The western
town lives on a system of
barter, swap, and exchange.
Nobody gives you a job unless
you owe him something and
he wants to work it off; it
takes all the jobs he can give
to collect his own debts. If
you aren’t in debt to anybody
nobody will employ you ; if
you are in debt to everybody
you get all the work they
have to give, unless somebody
is deeper in debt than you
are. Owe nothing outside
your own district if you can
help it; inside your own dis-
trict owe all you can to as
many people as you can, and do it as long as you
can.

“Cronje”: Be timber dying in Central
Queensland (B. 2(i/7/’O2), on the watershed of
Lachlan and Bogan (counties of Cunninghame and
Kennedy), the following timbers are dying :
Bimble box, apple box, grey box, mountain or
rocky pine, broom, wait-a-while, come-back,
lawyer bush, copper wattle, currawong, common
pine and ironbark. Jackasses have died every-
where ; in fact they are almost extinct.
* » * »
The vitality of the seeds of plants indi-
genous to Australian drought-land is phenomenal.
The Sturt pea is an instance. Certainly for 12 or
14 years none growing wild have been allowed to
seed in Broken Hill, and yet every spring, after
the slimmest apology for a shower, plants will
appear. Not many things could stand baking in
Centralian dust for, perhaps, 20 years, and still
live ; btlt these seeds do.
* * * 9
Wild goats are plentiful in the ranges out-
side of Broken Hill and Silverton. They are the
progeny of animals that esoaped from control
years ago. f
HOSPITALITY.
COCKY: “Don't wanter
buy a dog! W ell, if
yer hold on a jiff I'll empty the pupa outer the
billy an' we'll 'ave some tea.”
Camel-carrying out back is a brutal busi-
ness. Cows often drop their calves on the track
(the breeders are worked continuously); and the
simple plan adopted in that case is to tie the new-
comer to the staggering mother’s load. On the
average there are more wounds per square yard on
a working camel’s body than on anything else
under the sun ; and altogether a train coming in
after a heavy trip is one of the most dismal and
repellent sights in a land where cruel sights aren’t
uncommon.
THE SPUD-DIGGING DAYS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In days that are gone we spent part of each year
Digging spuds till the season was done,
And few were the grafters and “ murphies ” were dear,
And grand was the wage to be won ;
And gre.it was the fame of the spud-digger then—
No shed-ringer ever as proud—
Whose total was top in the tally book when
The boss settled up with the crowd.
16 was strip to the shirt and the diggers would go
Till the sweat was a-drip from each brow,
And bags were a-standing in cluster and row—
lint there’s nothing in spud-digging now.
To Warmainbool mostly or wild Bungaree
We —sure as the seasons—returned.
The rollicking spud diggers, sturdy and free,
And fat were the cheques that we earned.
'Twas “ Tucker yerselves and five shillin’s a ton.”
And other conditions were few,
When the season was late and a lot to he done,
With grain-sowing waiting them, too.
So we camped in bark humpies, and tents, and in logs,
And we worked while the light would allow,
In rain and in frosts, and in lingering fogs—
But there’s nothing in spud-digging now.
And then, surreptitiously, some of us used
To slip out a-courting at night,
And others would go to a pub. and get boozed,
And, maybe, get nurt in a light.
But we know them no more, they have vanished for aye,
And we bade them good-bye with regret,
For smoogers and crawlers now graft by the day,
Digging murphies for what they can get.
And out in the paddocks you mostly can liar
The heathen “ whaffor,” of the Chow,
And the fact is becoming increasingly clear
That there’s nothing in spud-digging now.
V.

J.F. D. : Solution of gaol-labor problem seems
to be : make every prisoner work, and work hard at what
he is best suited for ; credit him with the full value of
his labor ; debit him with cost of maintenance and sur-
veillance ; devote the balance to his wife and family (if
he has such), and to giving him a start when he gets out,
or, in some cases, to recouping his victims. There’s no
earthly reason why poems, boots, furniture, leading
articles, bricks, pictures, statues, toys, dog-collars, &c.,
&c., shouldn’t be made in gaol on strictly commercial
principles. The State should not under-sell, but
“ trade fair.” Pick - and - shovel men in custody
might be put to dig irrigation canals out West

APHORISTIC.
How we clamor and howl for “ Justice ! ” We should
howl a lot worse if we got it.
Duty’s only skin-deep. With most of us it has a hard
job to get even that far.
We temper the wind to the shorn sham.
Genius has been defined as the faculty for taking
infinite pains. Stuff !—Genius is the faculty for catching
a big fish on a bent pin.
The pessimist is the worst of fools. He suffers in anti-
cipation all the evils he may possibly never experience.
There is a remarkable family likeness, between the
devils we beget and the gods we deserve.
A man’s opinion of his comrades is seen in the story he
tells them ; the measure of himself is seen in the story he
likes.
Why try to forgive the person who has wronged you
when you can’t even forgive the person you’ve wronged
yourself ?
Brotherhood—A sublime aspiration, provided you first
pick your brothers.
The superstition of our century—That by becoming
scientifically intimate with the laws of Nature, we can
compensate ourselves for a daily violation of the laws of
Justice.
Q. P.L.
“ Kiator ” re a food-matter :—
In Melb. to-day I saw a fashionable lady and her son,
about 14 years old, making their two meals off one dinner.
The restaurateur was rather staggered, but gave in. This
kind of thing is common in Paris. One can never eat the
whole of a meal. An experienced traveller will give you
the tip of getting one meal for two. Also, it is common
with foreigners at London restaurants, and makes pro-
vincials stare, but not the Londoners. In San Francisco,
when ordering two meals, if you know your way about,
you ask for steak or chops (or any other dish) “’for one,
with two plates.” If you ask for steak “ for two,” they
give you as much as would serve two huge navvies. The
“ two plates ” system is not regarded as mean—it is the
local custom, and suits the restaurateur very well indeed.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
The new “Vulcanised” tyre issued by the
Dunlop co., has caught c\ c ists’ favor at a bound. The
new cover is vulcanised under intense hydraulic pressure,
rendering parting of rubber from the fabric casing an
impossibility. As this has always been the cause of fully
50 per cent, of the troubles associated with pneumatic
tyres, those cyclists who have not yet seen the new covers
will readily understand what a boon the Dunlop
“ Vulcanised” tyre will prove. As the new cover costs
no more than the hand made one, and it will outlast
almost two sets of ordinary tyres. Cyclists will quickly
appreciate the advantages the Dunlop co’s new tyre
offers. The present output of these covers from the
Dunlop Mills, Melbourne, is over 2,400 a week.

immediately upon the reassembling of Par-

“Ashby v. Borer, 1899, S. 4182.”
WHEREAS by an ORDER of the HIGH COURT of
VV JUSTICE, CHANCERY DIVISION, dated the 22nd
day of July, 1892, and made in the Matter of (the Trusts
of the Settlement, dated the Ist day of June, 1853, made
on the Marriage of ROBERT SHAPLAND and HARRIETT
lIAYTER, both Deceased, and in an action wherein JANE
ASIIBY, the wife of THOMAS ASHBY, suing in respect
of her separate Estate, is Plaintiff, and WILLIAM BORER
and WILLIAM REEVES Defendants. It was Ordered
that the following Enquiry be taken and made, namely:—
Whether CHARLES HAYTER, Brother of HARRIETT
SHAPLAND (formerly HAYTER), CHARLES FREDERIC
HAYTER, Son of GEORGE HAYTER (another Brother
of the said HARRIETT HAYTER), NELSON HAYTER
(another Son of the said GEORGE HAYTER), and
GEORGE TOLHURST (Son of CHARITY TOLHURST),
a Sister of the said HARRIETT SHAPLAND, formerly
HAYTER, are respectively living, or dead, and if dead
when they respectively died, and as to such of them as
have died since the 26th March, 1899, who are their
respective Legal Personal Representatives, and if the said
CHARLES HAYTER died prior to the said 26th day of
March, 1899, whether he left any and what issue, and
whether such issue are living or dead, and if dead w'hen
they respectively died, and who are the Legal Personal
Representatives of such of them as have died since the
said 26th day of March, 1899.
NOTICE is Hereby Given that the said CHARLES
HAYTER, if living, his Legal Personal Representatives if
he died since the 26th day of March, 1899, and his children
or issue (if any) if he died prior to the said 26th day of
March, 1899, and also the said CHARLES FREDERIC
HAYTER, NELSON HAYTER, and GEORGE TOL-
HURST (if living), or their Legal Personal Representa-
tives if they died since the 26th day of March, 1899, are,
by their Solicitors, on or before the' 2nd day of December,
1902, to come in and prove their Claims at the Chambers of
Mr. Justice Byrne and Mr. Justice Buckley, at the Royal
Courts of Justice, Strand, London, or in default thereof
they will be peremptorily excluded from the benefit of
the said Order. Tuesday, the 9th day of December, 1902,
at 11 o’clock in the forenoon, at the said Chambers, is
appointed for hearing and adjudicating upon the Claims.
Dated this 11th day of August, 1902!
RICH D. WHITE, Master.
RACK HAM & CO.,
46 Lincoln’s-inn-tields, London, England,
Solicitors for the Plaintiff.

EDWARDS, DUNLOP & Co., Ltd., Sydney and Brisbane
Victorian Selectors’ Rents.
Although Victoria appears to be in a bad finan-
cial state, it is largely because the supporters of
the Kyabram propc «iH who are farmers in irri-
gation districts, have repudiated the principal
sums they borrowed from the Govt., and only
occasionally even pay the interest; likewise
because those of them who are selectors don’t pay
up the nominal rents they are charged for being
allowed to become owners of Government lands,
and also, under the absurd Vie. law, escape
land-tax. These rents and arrears would
pay off the accumulated deficit. And it is
nothing short of monstrous that the people,
who are nagging at the Govt, to ruin the
Factories Act, to break their contracts with the
public servants, to turn off hundreds of casual
employees of the State, and to turn a deaf ear to
the troubles of the unemployed, should in hundreds
and thousands of cases be earning interest in
the banks on money which belongs to the Govern-
ment. There are many cases of hardship among
selectors, but there are myriads of them who can
well afford to pay their just debts in this time of
the State’s needs, for Victoria, in the main, has
escaped the drought curse, and is doing well.
Selectors have many privileges which the rest of
the people have paid dearly to secure for them.
For their sakes the costly irrigation works have
been made, the most crushing burdens in unpro-
ductive railways have been incurred, and expen-
sive and liberally-scattered State school, post-office
and police accommodation has been provided.
They have been given a majority voice in the
Assembly, whereas their numbers only entitle
them to a minority voice. They are all electors
of the Legislative Council, too —a right denied to
most of the workers. They are allowed to sweat
their employees without limit, being exempt from
any Minimum Wage law-; no Health Act applies
to them ; they get large grants every year to help
keep their roads in order; they get seed wheat
lent them in times of distress, and all sorts
of facilities to build tanks, erect rabbit-
proof fencing, and to engage in novel indus-
tries. The Agricultural Department scours Europe
and America to find the best experts to teach
them how to make money. Most of these privi-
leges are not grudged to the selectors, but it is not
unfair to demand that, before they put new bur-
dens on, and deny rights to, other classes in the
community, they should at least pay their very
light debts to the State. No harshness is urged
to those really distressed, but there are a
great number of selectors who could easily
wipe off their obligations to the State.
As for those who, instead of paying their
arrears, are getting interest on the amount in the
U ’ ' until a threat of forfeiture comes, it is the
plain duty of the Government to bring the whole
machinery of the Lands Department into drastic
operation against such thieves. Premier Irvine
says that he cannot neglect the general interests
of the State for a small class, even though that
class consists of starving men, women and children.
Let him make good Lis statement in case of this
class of land-owners, for whose benefit the whole
interests of the State have been shamefully sacri-
ficed in the past, and whose neglect to pay their
honest debts to a generous creditor is the cause of
much of the present difficulty as regards the
State’s finances.
The Dead March in Irrigation.
There never was a funeral so slow as the State of
N.S.W. in the work of irrigation. It moves
a step in a quarter of a century. It is only one
step so far, and then a dead halt for a generation
at least. One step with the artesian water was
Pera bore —12 years since it was made. Another
with an irrigation colony at Hay—10 years.old,
and still sole in the land. One step in the way of
cutting a new channel for the Murray water—
plans completed in 1887 ; no water running yet,
and no other channel begun. Yet, with 10,000,000
acres of rich artesian country surveyed, a dozen
streams, with periodic supplies, are running to
waste east and west of the dividing range. An irri-
gation system, moving to quick time, would change
all that. But here people only mark time. The
Devil’s toe of this drought even fails to kick them
into motion. And yet they wonder that Victoria
still leads Australia. Victoria has got a patch of
mallee and dry country in her north-west, as bad
and as good as any of the N.S. W. West, and in
area about one-tenth of the Western Division.
What is she doing with it ? She stuck Mil-
dura there, 12 years ago, and now the rail-
way is going out fast. But not alone. To
right and left of that line are two water service
schemes—the left, from the Wimuiera waters, im-
pounded at Lake Lonsdale ; the right, from the
Murray, at Tooleybuc. The first scheme is
under weigh, and will be completed before
the railway; and the second has only been
delayed by a bit of log-rolling in favor of
a crazed £2,000,000 Goulburn Valley idea. Lake
Lonsdale scheme will be finished for £200,000.
Tooleybuc is estimated to cost £127,000, and the
two completed will make the oi-dinary mallee
settler’s life tolerable and pave the way for irriga-
tion development which will follow the railway
opening at Mildura. All this in the vvorst corner
of Australia’s smallest. State, three parts of it
covered with mallee, infested with dingoes, foxes
and rabbits and with a. {rainfall less than Bourke’s.
Truly one of the worst things “ Home ” ever did
for Australia was the fixing of the Victorian
boundary at the Murray and not the Murrum-
bidgee. There would have been a new face on
the continent by now had the enterprise of the
“cabbage-gardeners” been permitted that field
for its operations.
The Happy Teacher.
From March number of Vic. Education Gazette
(1902):
Owing to the dearth of junior male teachers it has been
decided to alter the regulations so as to provide for the
preparation after future competitive exams, of two lists
of meritorious candidates, namel3 r —one of males and the
other of females. . . . Head teachers are requested to
assist the Department in obtaining a supply of male can-
didates. As most teachers are aware, a pupil teacher on
appointment receives £30 a year, and is entitled to a yearly
increment of £10 up to £60 a year on passing the requisite
examinations. The employment is thus more remunera-
tive than most to which a lad, from 16 years to 18 years
of age and of average ability, can be put.
That notice was apparently inspired by depart-
mental despair at getting Miss Jones to teach a
school in the backblocks, and sleep with the three
youngest of the selector’s family, and live on mut-
ton and sodden home-made bread. Also Miss
Jones, intellectually above the average bush-
hoodlum, felt instinctively that the chance of
mating suitably thereabouts was 300 to 1 against
her, and being allowed to decline, declined. The
male, on whom a male department wastes no
sympathy, wasn’t available. They wanted to
kick him out to Kookaburra, and there was
nothing to kick. So they inserted this misleading
bit of encouragement to male candidates of average
ability. Note the average. The notice is mislead-
ing, because (1) candidates are always nearer 18
years than 15 before they get into the service ; (2)
the future—a necessary grind of dull, staid respect-
ability on from £100 to £150 a year—is hidden.
This is all the candidate of average ability can ever
expect. And now, from the Argus, of September
4th, 1902, we learn that £700 a year will be
received by the Education Department from these
sources:
(1) Teachers will be charged from Is. 6d. to 5s. for their
parchment certificates.
(2) Candidates for employment must pay 2s. 6d., as
entrance fee, before they can attend competitive examina-
tions.
Now, just suppose a merchant advertised for a
clerk, and demanded 2s. 6d. entrance fee from
every candidate !!
