“ Buzz ” writes
Re the blacks’ horror of being sent to Fraser’s Island
(Q.). It is not any especial dislike to Fraser, but to
restraint of any sort at all. The aboriginal is a tameless
animal, as tameless as his own native regent bird. lie
loves to rove over a big area ; and prefers to be half-
starved doing it rather than confined to a few acres with
plenty. This gathering of the aborigines into mission or
protection stations is one of the greatest cruelties the
whites have ever inflicted upon them. The blaekfellow
loves his own district, and clings to it with all his heart
and soul. His intelligence is only large enough to take
in the smallest of facts, and his own camping ground,
where he was born, is one of them—he will wander from
it, hut if he is sick or unhappy, it is there he wants to
be. This Fraser’s Island is a scandal the Queensland
Government will one day have to face and inquire into.
The year before last a half-caste gin was taken from near
Yaamba, Rockhampton, against her will. She was earn-
ing her own living as housekeeper on a station at the
time ; could read and did write up to her friends at
Yaamba, asking them to intercede and get her back. As
she put it: “ If I am to work, why was I brought away
from a place where I was getting good wages to a place
where I get no money and have plenty more hard work.”
It looks as if some one at Fraser wanted a good house-
keeper. A half-caste stockman wanted to marry her and
wrote to the authorities at Fraser to that effect. He
hardly wrote to the right quarter. The “ Sacred Ibis ” is
not so greatly beloved by the blacks as most people sup-
pose.
The heartiest eater of toads in Australia
is a Melbourne personage of importance, who
is boss cookie of a public institution, and yet
a man who bears himself humbly
and lowly in the company of dis-
tinguished people. Likewise, the
presence of a title in the same
street makes his backbone as
pliable as a whip-lash. Other
night, Sir Whatsthis and
“ his lady” and various con-
spicuous persons were at
the theatre. The obsequious
moderately-great man was
also there, keeping an adoring
eye upon the grand pfirty.
When the show was over the
former made a dasli for the
stairs, and a minute later was
opening the carriage-door for
Sir Whatsthis and his people,
and bowing again and again
with the ardor of a trained
lackey as magnificence climbed
into the vehicle.
The £1300 raised at the Coro-
nation Carnival in Melb. Exhibi-
tion for the purpose of assisting
the unemployed has been handed
over to a committee of Important
Public Personages, and they have
resolved to devote the money to
assisting men to gain farming ex-
perience on a labor colony, “from
which they can be placed on good
land by the Government.” It is
not a cheering outlook. The farm-
ing experience mostly gained on
Vic. labor colonies is the experience
that drives farmers to stick straws
in their whiskers, and take to the
trees howling. Few of the labor colonies promise
to be good for anything till fertilised with the
bones of generations of heart-broken settlers.
Colliery Explosions.
“ Jimmy S.” on the coal-miner’s risks :
The safety of mining conditions is jeopardised by the
strenuous resistance of both miners and proprietors
whenever a Govt, inspector proposes the introduction of
safety lamps into a gassy mine. To anyone acquainted
with the ordinary safety lamp, the miner’s objection
appears verj T reasonable. The lamp has to be placed well
back from the swing of the pick. The flame is little
larger than a thumb-nail, and is protected by a glass Jin.
thick and 3in. long. When this glass is smoked and
coated with mine moisture the strain on the eyes can
well be imagined. Perhaps, acting on the principle of
adaptation to conditions, this may result in the evolution
of a new type of miner born without eyes, like fish in the
Jenolan Caves.
A slight jar, a sudden raising or lowering, puts a lamp
out. The miner has then to take his mate’s lamp, and go
to the lamp cabin to have his own lamp unlocked and re-
lit. Work is thus suspended until his return. With a
safety lamp, considerable time is lost cleaning the coal.
If a miner fills dirty coal, he must pay the penalty—lose
a day’s work. Where safety lamps are used, the miner
cannot fire his own shots. The shot-firers have stated
times for making their rounds, and, if the miner is not
quite ready, he must wait until the next round. Shots
are fired by a wire heated in the firer’s special lamp, or,
where there is a slight indication of gas, by a battery.
Lastly, a bad roof cannot readily be detected with a
safety, and a careful miner takes up much time looking
for bad spots. The miner of to-day, who appoints a sala-
ried district check inspector, and has also a local check
inspector working at the mine, cannot be compared with
the drunken, reckless “ underground savage ” of former
years, who returned to the coal-face five minutes after a
miss-shot; who entered the pub. coming from work on a
pay Friday night and started work on Tuesday morning
with the same old coating of coal-dust on his face.
Summed up, tire miners’ objection to the “firefly” is
better more danger than less tucker for all the draw-
backs of the safety they ask for a slight increase in the
hewing rate. ....
There are , three collieries in the Newcastle district
worked with safety lamps, and the introduction of safeties
at one of them marks an era in Newcastle’s coal-mining
history—it was not preceded by an explosion.
A seam may be giving off only a small percentage of
gas, but the safety of that mine must not be judged by
the general working conditions. It is a possible flaw in
these conditions that must be guarded against. For
instance : A wheeler is hurrying with the last set and
knocks a few tacks off the canvas stopping, used to divert
some of the air-current to where men are working. He
fails to notice and report same. Let this pag’e represent
a canvas stopping diverting so many thousands of feet of
air per minute. Turn down a corner and we get a correct
idea of the damaged stopping. As the current strikes this
corner with direct velocity far more than a proportionate
amount of air fails to be diverted.
Suppose gas is being given off from a freshly-exposed
coal-surface and there is not sufficient air to drive it out.
It rises to a broken roof. The examing deputy makes a
hurried examination—there can’t possibly be gas only
60yds. from the shaft bottom—and reports “all safe.”
Examiners’ districts are often too extensive and lie must
push on. The stopping is repaired later, and a good
current follows. But special means must be adopted to
drive an accumulation of gas out. From the gas in the
broken roof to the floor is probably 14ft., and the men
have been known to pass underneath with naked lights
without danger. But the trouble would come when the
timberer climbed on a heap of stones with a naked light
on his head.
Of all the dangers incidental to life below the surface
none is more to be feared than a heavy fall displacing
gas in old workings. When this occurs the gas must find
an outlet somewhere, and brick stoppings are straws in
the march of the tempest rushing on till it gains fresh
vigor from a naked lamp. That simply shows the neo; s-
sity of having all old workings effectively timbered,
inspected, and included in the ventilation system of the
new workings. If, under the present Act, an inspector
cannot compel the use of safety lamps, then the Govt.
Inspectors Enabling Act, last year before the British Par-
liament, should be seriously considered by the Legisla-
ture. Surely we are not waiting for a few more
holocausts to strengthen the inspectors’ hands.

“E.D.M.” : “ Why is Australian meat so
much cheaper in England than it is in the Com-
■ monwealth ?” Why ? Because Australia has to
send £16,000,000a year to pay interest onpublicand
private foreign debt. Mutton-chops and rump-
steak are just as good as gold or wool for that
purpose. Something has to be exported to meet
our political grandfathers’ debts in dear old
Hingland.

Thrilling descriptive item from Ballarat
Star : —
Some amusement was caused at the Western railwav
station last night by a sorry looking individual presenting
himself and asking if they had a truck of pigs for him.
On being answered in the negative, he demurred rather
loudly, but had to go away without his porkers.
And the statued city, where the fact of a man
asking if his truck of pigs has come is considered
worth recording in the papers, wanted to be the
Federal capital !

Max Hirsch has donned the war paint and
feathers for the third time, to again contest Man-
d trang (Vic.) electorate, against old-established
sitting member O’Neill (of Anti - Cigarette Bill
fame). Max’s desire to enter politics has become
more than an unremunerative hobby; it is now a
mania 1
Premier See says that he proposes neither
to abolish nor alter the N.S.W. Agent-General’s
department in London. If the other States follow
suit this will be the only country of 4,000,000
people which maintains seven ambassadors in
London. The two Kings of Barataria were a
small joke by comparison. /
The Vic. railway-men are organising solidly.
The latest move is the formation of the Victorian
Railways Clerical Association which already
numbers 600 members, and the stationmasters are
coming under the Association’s wing. Seemingly
the chance of another climb-down being necessary
iu time of trouble is being minimised !
During the present Vic. elections the
Irvine Govt, will have the support of the con-
servative Argus, the “Liberal” Age, the Vic.
Employers’ Federation, the Stock Exchange, the
Chamber of Commerce and the Citizens’ “Re-
form” League. You may .know a Government by
the company it keeps.

at as tangible evidence of the triumph of Kyabram.
Every ’pootra got on his mound and crowed.
Now the same M‘Donald is saddled with the curse

ment that caused the dissolution that will cost the
stricken Cabbage-Garden close on £lO,OOO.
Sydney D.T. publishes this amongst its
country news:
Windsor, Tuesday.—Times were never known to be so
bad in the district as now. Industries that have invari-
ably thriven have either been shut down or are working
short-handed. This is a decided facer for the policy of
I‘rotection, the drought and the failure of crops not
having any effect on the industries in question.
Windsor is a one-horse, one-street N.S.W. town,
which lives on it 3 memories and the occasional
sale of a case of fruit. And it is represented as
stricken down by the ruin of its “industries”
through the creature Protection. It possesses a
butter factory, four tanneries (the drought has
reduced the supply of hides), and a gaol. Whioh
of these “industries” has the tariff ruined?
HORRIBLE POSSIBILITIES.
DOCTOR (to Mick, who has hurst into the surgery with a blanched face): “ What's the matter, Mick? ”
MICK : “ Some of the bhoys bet me five bob I couldn’t smaller a bin’s egg whole, an' Oi done it.”
DOCTOR: “ Well, is it hurting you?”
MICK : “Divil a bit; but it’s frightened Oi am, bad luck to it.”
DOCTOR: “If you got it down all right there shouldn’t be any danger.”
MICK : “ Whilloo! Oi'm frightened to smash it fer fear the bits uv shell ’ll stick in me gizzard;
an’ Oi’m frightened to let it stay fur fear it’ll hatch out a rooshter as’ll kick the
innards out o’ me.”
The motion of Broughton, M.L.A., for a
select committee to inquire into the housing of
the working classes in and around Sydney amounts
to mere pretentious humbug. Plenty is known
already about the housing of the working classes.
What is wanted is a remedy, not a long-dragged-
out inquiry into things that have been discovered
long ago. One remedy, if Broughton has such a
thing about his clothes, is worth a thousand
inquiries.
The alleged anti-Federal reaction, about
which so much has been said of late by papers
whose pauper aid in the shape of free postage is
going to be stopped, has been put to the proof in
one State at all events. Hall, of Waratah,
brought on his separation proposals in the
Tasmanian Assembly, and about two members
voted with him—apparently more to humor the
patient than anything else.
Six months ago Victorian Lands Minister
M’Kenzie, as bigoted a Conservative as ever
floated a big loen and cut down the wages of a
fellow-creature, declared that he would gladly
denounce the whole scheme of factory legislation
from every platform in the State. M'Kenzie is
now one of the Irvine Government which says, for
a wage, that its sincere friendship for the Fac-
tories Act is something too beautiful for words.
One possible good result of the Shiels-
Irvine upheaval is that it may compel the Labor
party to assume the responsibilities of the Parlia-
mentary Opposition, as in Queensland. At
present the Vic. Labor members practically
follow Peacock, but if that gentleman doesn’t
move a little faster they may be compelled to get
in front of Peacock.
Premier See’s excuse for the State’s empty
purse: “We spent £400,000 in sending troops to
S’Africa; £122,000 on the Federal inauguration
ceremonies ; £200,000 in stamping out the plague ;
£21,000 celebrating the arrival of the Duke and
Duchess of York.” Leave out the plague, which
should have been met with a local tax, and does
not the rest constitute the excuse of the razzle-
dazzler who awakes without the price of a beer ?
As usual, when a strike breaks down half-
way, it is the friendly employer who comes down
with the deadest thud. A.W.U. men are now
charging him 225. 6d., while the squatter who
fought the union is getting his work done for 20s.
And when the friendly squatter sees that he could
have saved half-a-crown if he had stood out a bit,
he is very likely to make up his mind tojdo it next
time.
One Melbourne employer, at least,
allowed no grass to grow under his feet before
taking advantage of the lapsing of the Factories
Act. He, boss of a fashionable city restaurant,
immediately rounded up his “hands,” and, bel-
lowing “It’s my turn now,” told them that in-
stead of the 60 hours a week allowed under the
defunct Act he would work them as long and as
hard as he choose, “ and there’ll be no half-holiday
nonsense either.” Two members of the Caterers’
Employees Union who ventured to protest were
promptly sacked “for interfering with my way of
doing business,” and sweating began as of yore.
The silly “secessionists” of N.S.W. are
allowed by the sympathetic D.T. to air every
nonsensical whim as a reason for separation.
Just now it is the Dawes Point question. Last
week it was the constitutional outrage involved in
the detaining of some importer’s vanman a few
hours at the Customs House. The dispute as to
whether the N.S.W. inhabitant owns Dawes
Point in his capacity as a citizen of N.S.W. or
owns it in his capacity as a citizen of the Common-
wealth doesn’t really matter a straw to anybody.
It is only another grindstone on which to grind
the axe of the importer, and the parochial crank,
and the Tory, and other debris.
Premier Barton comes back pledged to
recommend that Australia’s naval tribute be in-
creased to £200,000. Chamberlain tried to squeeze
his guest to the extent of £400,000, but failed,
Also Seddon proposed that Maoriland should pay
one-sixth of a naval subsidy of £240,000, and in
return should have one-third of the fleet per-
manently stationed in its waters, which, as a
cheeky attempt to swindle a near neighbor and
relative, was worthy of Richard.

item :
QUEENSLAND’S DESIRE FOR SEVERANCE.

Then the paragraph which followed set forth that

ad received one telegram congratulating him on

That beautiful Private Enterprise, the
Van Diemen’s Land Co., has lost an action
whereby it endeavored to assert its right to block
all access to 60 miles of the Tasmanian coast. The
V.D.L. Co.’s huge steal —mostly roadless, uncul-
tivated, shut off from settlement, a howling waste
of ignorance, mud and retrogression—blocks the
progress of N. W. Tasmania, and lies like a ton of
mud on the chest of civilisation ; and the co., in
its desire to lie even more heavily than it does, is
going to carry its present claim to the Privy
Council.

The poor elector makes a desperate attempt to get out of David Same's clutches.
Melbourne Argus (15/9/’02):
It is economy—economy to be achieved by reform and 1
retrenchment —economy in the Parliamentary establish-
ment and in all departments—economy that will prevent
further deficits, wipe out the floating deficit, strengthen
Victoria's credit in London.
It never strikes an Australian daily paper to sug-
gest knocking off the drink for a spell for any
other reason than as a preparation for another big
spree in the future.
Dog-in-the-mangerish is too complimentary
a term to apply to the action of the N.S.W.
Government in connection with the Murray
waters. What is the sin of Victoria ? How can
she hurt N.S.W. when all her canals are made ?
Premier See’s engineers could tell him that
N.S.W. proposes to put in her main tap a good
many inilas east of Victoria’s farthest up-river
“trespass.” When that tap and adjuncts
are ready (they are hardly begun) and N.S.W.
wants to irrigate, Victoria could only take
from the Murray what N.S.W. allowed to pass.
The Vic. irrigation works are far west—they only
touch the Murray when N.S.W. is about done
with it, and it is on the point of leaving
N.S.W. territory. The trouble is because Vic-
toria acts while N.S.W. “ reports,” protests and
whines.
The N.S.W. Land Courts are now busy
considering re-appraisements of the cookie’s C.P.’s.
Values have been lowered to five shillings per
acre upwards—generally a reduction to three-
fifths of first price, and thousands of selectors
with, as they thought, many years’ payments
ahead, are free. It is all very fine and large ; but
this sweeping away of hundreds of thousands of
future revenue, while the State sits disconsolate
on Cohen’s doorstep, is so like N.S.W.
Nansen moved, the other day, in the
Westralian Assembly, that a local mail would do
well enough for State Governor (he certainly does
well enough in Canada), but in the face of
“general opposition” he withdrew his motion.
Nansen has since thrown up the Opposition
leadership, after a very brief reign. He advanced
some progressive ideas (the above was one of them,
and the abolition of the useless and nefarious
Upper House was another) but he had a
tendency to back down in the face of “general
opposition.”
There was a pathetic scene in one of the
corridors at Melb. Treasury buildings the other
morning. Old man James Munro, the forlorn
ex-candidate for Dandenong, or somewhere, en-
countered the committee of the “ Citizens’ Reform
League,” and began to shriek over his wrongs.
The League had deserted him, or ignored him, in
his hour of expectancy, and Mu.nro felt like
banging it with his umbrella. Then Davies,
brother of Matthew, came gently into the un-
pleasantness, and led the poor, old, faded boomster
away to a cool place.
Vic. Opposition leader Peacock is just
now a lost-looking circumstance. Since he let
go his Bill for the election of a Convention to draft
a new State Constitution Peacock has drifted
from bad to worse, and has lost the run of one
thing after another. Now, he can’t effectively
attack Irvine’s miserably thin pretences of demo-
cratic sentiment. The obvious answer to any
attack is that Irvine may carry out democratic
reforms from interested motives without believing
in them ; while the country has tried Peacock and
found that his belief in democratic principles is of
little use, owing to the limpness of the believer.
The Jenkins Iscariot party, feeling sure
that the alleged Opposition will keep it in office,
grows aggressive. Government whip O’Loughlin,
the mildest-mannered man in S.A., who wouldn’t
harm a fly if the fly’s vote was of any importance,
called Labor-leader Price an “arrant ass” lately,
and also accused him of telling tarradiddles, and
the Premier cheered approval of his minion’s
aggressiveness. Still, the Labor vote will be
wanted again some day, and then Jenkins and
O’Loughlin will crawl once more.

Extract from the financial colomn of
Sydney D.T.:
The growth of Canadian banking has been remarkable
in the past 10 years, especially when we bear in mind that
those banking institutions are more restricted in their
business than is the case with the Australasian banks.
This is from the same financial column which p. 9-
dicts desolation and ruin to Australian banking if
the Canadian system is adopted here.
11l Maoriland, the natives have challenged
the titles of some white landholders, and the case
involves 1,500,000 acres of land—some of it held
by the Government. So a Bill has been intro-
duced providing that the natives shall not b&
allowed to appeal to the courts at all in the
matter, except with the permission of the
Governor-in-Council —which is just the Govern-
ment ; and when the Government happens itself to
be the defendant, it is really proposed that plain-
tiff must not sue defendant except by defendant’s
permission. If Paul Kruger had struck the idea
of enacting that a British subject must not sue a
Boer without the Boer’s permission, Maoriland
would have said that the thing was “unprece-
dented.” This also looks rather “unprecedented,”
but it is so much easier to recognise the other
fellow’s unprecedentedness than to recognise our
own unprecedentedness, even if our own unprece-
dentedness is the more unprecedented of the two.
The big flaw in the ingenious Trvine-Shiels
manifesto is its omission of any reference to the.
establishment of a permanent Vic. Factories Act-
All that is promised' is a temporary measure*
which those bitter opponents of Factory legisla-
tion, Irvine, McKenzie, and Cameron, and artful
dodgers Shiels and Taverner, no doubt feel sure,
will never get through the House of 48 Rich.
Landlords. No better proof could be desired of
Irvine’s intention to sell the workers, and play
into the hands of Flinders Lane. As it is, the.
Parliamentary labor representatives despair of get-
ting the Factories Act back in its old shape for some
years, and look upon the Wages Boards as already
lost. Every wage-earner whose vote is not cast,
this time for the retention of the Factories Act
above all other considerations will deserve a,
reduction of screw, increase of hours, and all the
evils that the Irvine-Robert-Reid-McKenzie-
Davies-Cameron-Sachse-Tommy Bent crowd has.
always desired to bring upon him.
Yic. Minister of Education (“ education ”
is usually the booby of the Ministry) Reid is,
starting to put his flabby finger into the already-
disorganised pie of the Education department..
His plaintive wail is of the abnormal cost of-
teachmg the young idea how to shoot —this in,
face of the notorious fact that the education vote
has been starved for years. Schools all over the-
State have been overcrowded for so long as the-
youngsters thereat can remember; schools that
were promised years ago in new districts are still
awaiting the finding of the necessary money ;
schools that were closed during the post-boom
depression have been pulled down and never re-
erected. And now the commercially-minded'
Reid proposes to lessen the expense by puttings
some 630 schools, whose average attendance is-
under 20, on a half-time basis; while others, with
an average attendance of under 10, are to be
satisfied with the occasional visit of a “ travelling
teacher.”
Tommy Bent is a cheerful soul. He de-
clares that Victoria has no national debt; it has
merely borrowed money and spent it on railways,
which could be sold, if need be, to some out-
sider (in which case the value of the lines-
would still be debt). According to this, the-
man who has bought a shirt and neglected to
pay for it isn’t in debt for a shirt, because he
still possesses the garment, in a frayed and worn
condition; and Britain has no national debt,
because Britain acquired its debt in acquiring
Canada, and Cape Colony, and Gibraltar, and
India, and Ireland, and in building its big iron-
clad fleet; and if it sold its fleet and the above
possessions, it would have no debt. Therefore,
according to Bent’s logic, it hasn’t any debt.
N.S.W. has apparently drunk its three-
headed Railway Commission to the dregs, and
now that the term for which the Commissioners
were appointed is ending, it is said that the three-
man system has rendered the whole railway ser-
vice unsound. The original Victorian commis-
sion, under Speight, was a one-man concern, so
was the original N.S.W. trinity under Chief-Com-
missioner Eddy, whose decisions his subordinate
Commissioners merely endorsed when required.
And when Charles Oliver, a former Under-Sec.
for Lands, with no railway experience, stepped
into Eddy’s shoes, they didn’t fit him. He could
not pronounco ex-cathedra on questions of railway
management, and was virtually only the president
of a board, liable to be outvoted by the other mem-
bers, who understood the business in hand perhaps
better than he. Hence the failure, and it seems
pretty clear that N.S.W. will have to appoint a
sole commissioner, general manager, or something,
of that sort.

VITADATIO
35 Gas tlereagh-street,
Sydney, 4th March, 1901.
To Mr. PALMER.
Dear Sir, —Some months ago I was troubled with very
acute pains in my legs which I treated as gout, and
took different patent remedies from which I got no
relief; on the contrary gradually getting worse and
suffering intense pain, until at last I could not stand on
my feet and had to take to my bed, my friends noticed
the change which had come over me and advised me to
go to the hospital, and with their entreaties and knowing
how bad I was I decided to go, as I would receive the
best medical attention and skilful nursing. After
remaining there for some time I found I was getting
worse, and my complaint seemed to puzzle the medical
men attending me ; so I decided to leave the institution,
and with the aid of sticks reached home and took to my
bed again. About two weeks later a gentleman asked
me to try VITADATIO, and said that it had done
“ wonders for him ”; he very kindly sent me a bottle to
try. I found it act at once, and it did me so much good
that I continued to take it, the result being that I can do
without my sticks and am a changed man completely.
I have put fully a stone on in w.eight since I started to
take the medicine, and I cannot say too much in praise
of VITADATIO and what it has done for me. I am
deeply indebted to the gentleman who advised me to
take VITADATIO and the great benefit it has been to
ni“
You are at liberty to make what use you like of this
letter. Yours respectfully',
FRED. W. MOODY.
For further particulars ,
S. A. PALMER,

While Marconi has been perfecting his
system of upper-air wireless telegraphy, one
Armstrong, an Englishman, and Orbing, a Swede,
have been experimenting with earth-currents. A
year ago Armstrong had on a hill 2\ miles from
his house in Bucks an electric lamp, which,
though unconnected with the house by wires, he
could, from the house, light and extinguish at will
by merely pressing a button. Since then, apply-
ing the principle to the telephone, he has had
successful communication up to three miles, and
now states that he can supply a regular service to
subscribers at £1 a year.
A letter from the Far West—the bogus
Bulletin representative again:
A cove arrived at a bush pub., 400 miles from Sydney,
i the other night and said he was representing Tub
Bulletin, also that his bike had broken down a few miles
back and he had given it to a carrier to bring on, and also
he would stay the night. He was a nice little man, well
groomed, slight moustache, dark, and hands that were
the admiration of the girl and the envy of Mrs. Bung.
He ate enough for two, and afterwards retired to the par-
lor, where he spread himself on three chairs and read and
slept till midnight. Then the working staff of the hotel,
poking round to see if he’d gone to bed, so’s they could
turn out the slush lamp, found the room empty. They
presumed he’d gone to bed, so did ditto. He —your repre-
sentative —ain’t been seen or heard of since; the bed wasn’t
touched ; no carrier passed with a bike ; and the cove just
cut ’is stick in the dead of night without paying his
blanky bill. The population was much mystified and
contemplated dragging the only adjacent waterhole, but,
on second thoughts, decided not, lest they should stir up
the contents to such a,n extent that they wouldn’t settle
again. Again, if they found your representative people
might get to know of it, and raise a row about drinking
that water. So if your office will oblige by forwarding 2s.
fid. for meal and wear and tear of oxygen in the best par-
lor we’ll say no more about it.
.Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to perusellerr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
33 of this issue *

Disorders of
the Stomach
Regulated by
Chamberlain’s
Stomach and
Liver Tablets
Price 1/6. Just what you need.
Disorders of the stomach are, as a rule,
caused by a torpidity of the liver and bowels
and irregularity in living. When the diges-
tion is naturally weak, as is often the case
with people of middle age and older, too much
care cannot be used to keep the bowels
regular, for when they become constipated
the trouble is greatly aggravated. In fact,
constipation is the prime cause of nearly
all disorders of the stomach. Keeping the
bowels regular, however, will not alone cure
these disorders. Only easily digested food
should be taken, and that moderately and
well masticated, And at regular hours.
Fully six hours should elapse between meals,
so as to give ample time for digestion and
allow the stomach a period of rest before
more food is taken. The liver and whole
digestive apparatus needs toning up, for which
purpose take two Chamberlain’s Stomach
and Liver Tablets to begin with and then
one tablet each day for a few days until the
disorder is corrected, They should be taken
immediately after supper. In case one tablet
a day does not keep the bowels free, take two;
or if one each day causes the bowels to act
too freely, take only one every other day.

The Wonder of the Age
ALL CHEMISTS
THROUGHOUT
AUSTRALASIA.
“Nymsie” writes about a class whose
death has been unduly postponed :
Would it be of any use warning the country people, and
“ wayback ” folks in particular, against the hawks who
pose as duly qualified opticians, make a great pretence of
taking the correct optical measurements, and supply
glasses to young and old at prices commensurate with the
humbug—and to-morrow have flown to repeat their
swindle further on. Three days ago a stylishly dressed
man, with a taking gift of the gab, exploited this village
(say Hayseedville). In two days he disposed of 70 pairs
of glasses, the majority in rolled, gold frames at £1 per
pair. Not five pairs suit the sights of the simple Simons
an hour after being used, but the saddest cases are those
where children have been fitted with glasses that will ruin
their sight if worn. The pill-pedlar commands my sym-
pathy, but the quack eye-doctor and bogus optician
deserves the cat, vigorously applied. Another matter
that needs attention in these days of opthalmia is the
habit, growing daily, of purchasing cheap specs, for the
family, from Dad to the “ Kiddy,” from the Hindu
hawkers. No wonder eye diseases are becoming an epi-
demic in the bush, and the questionable economy of
letting one pair of colored globulars go round the family
when sandy-blight, etc., attacks a wayback household
tells its own tale eventually. Blaze it on every tree —
“ Sight is priceless 1 ”
Trains.
“ Galvo ” : The Sydney folk who growl at their
electric trams (in reality one of the best-conducted
systems in the world from the passenger point of
view) should take a ride in an Auckland horse-
car and learn what bliss is. They are lugged
along by three fat,, lumbering mokes, whose chief
ambition is to lie down and rest; whilst the cars
are ancients arks from America —probably dis-
carded New York horse-cars. Each car might
easily have come all the way from Mt. Ararat,
and not have had a coat of paint since Noah
decided to let things slide. They spend part of
their time on the rails and part on the macada-
mised road, according to the driver’s fancy. No
one is in a hurry in Auckland, so occasionally a
cart is left across the tram-rails while its owner
does some shopping. That doesn’t worry the
car-driver. Not much ! He whips up his
mettlesome steeds, and pulls the car off on
to the metalsome road and round the
obstacle. Sometimes he’s able to get the
car on the rails again at once; most times
he bumps it along for a quarter-of-a-mile
with first one wheel on, then another, before he
succeeds. The passenger inside clings to his seat
like a ’possum to a gumtree, and gazes heaven-
wards at a stained-glass inscription on the roof
light, which says: “This car is fitted with
patent springs, contributing to quiet and ease.”
He wonders between jolts what it would be like
if it wasn’t fitted with patent springs. If you
want to pay a bill, or run up a bill, the car will
wait outside a shop while you do so, and when you
get in again the conductor will bang the door and
say “Thank you” as politely as if he meant it.
They won’t wait outside a pub., though, as in
Westralia, while you get a drink, because Maori-
land is nominally a temperance country, and
drinking mustn’t be encouraged. There is no
particular object gained by riding in an Auck-
land tram, except a good liver-shaking, for the
cars don’t travel above three miles an hour at a
fair average, and the lines are as devious as a
legal argument, so that by taking short cuts one
gets home much earlier on foot. Considering
the variety of sensations to be obtained from a
single ride, the fare of 3d. for a mile trip seems
dirt cheap. The humorous part of it all is that
the Auckland people glory in their horse-tram |
system, and the change to electric traction now
in progress was bitterly opposed—not because it
meant handing over what should be a handsome j
revenue-producing municipal asset to J. Bull, but
because it was an innovation. They hate innova-
tions in trams as much as they do in methods of
sewerage disposal, which is saying a good deal. j
The completion of the Mombasa-Uganda
railway in British East Africa is an item of
some slight interest to this country. The coast
districts of British East Africa are not fertile,
nor good for European settlement, and the exist-
ence of the tsetse fly, which is utterly destruc-
tive to all animals of burden, made it necessary
that the products of the prolific interior should
be carried to the coast by porters —an expensive
process, even when the carriers are paid only
with beads, copper wire, and turkey-red twill.
But the railway has changed all that, and goods
can now be brought from Uganda and Unyoro
to Port Mombasa as cheaply as they can from
Dubbo to Sydney. Uganda itself, which pro-
duces coffee, indigo, rubber, gums, and other
tropical exports, does not concern Australia
particularly. It is the high, rolling table-land
of Unyoro, which lies to the north, and north-
west of Uganda, that will eventually prove
this country’s trouble, for although almost under
the equator, its altitude gives it a temperate,
bracing climate and a plentiful rainfall, almost
equal to that of Maoriland. Its soil is suitable
for wheat-growing and for pastoral purs'-its, and
even before the advent of the railway, large quan-
tities of hides used to find their way to Mombasa
on the heads of porters. Iron is everywhere, also
copper and some gold. Added to this, there is
a plentiful supply of cheap colored labor, which is
rather more reliable than colored labor generally
is. The country is British territory, and garri-
soned by Soudanese troops under British officers,
and there is no fly in the country, and
no fever, and the soil is first-class, and
the access to the railway now quite easy.
In all probability, a large white population will
settle there, and, with closer proximity by several
thousand miles to the British market, their more
reliable rainfall and better soil, will promptly pro-
ceed to undersell in the European markets what
the Sydney Telegraph and the Dry Dog party are
pleased to call “our natural and staple products.”
The sooner Australia recognises that her
hold on the markets of Europe must diminish
yearly as the African continent is opened up, the
sooner will steps be taken to provide employment
for our people by the creation of other industries.

is not a sport that everyone cares to indulge in. Gentlemen, and ladies,
too, often very much prefer a country ride or a little touring. But, in
choosing a mount for theconntry ride or occasional tour, all the pleasure

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“Ihe Final Test Match,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent, “ has never been equalled
for extraordinary vicissitude and astounding ending. It
was a truly magnificent victory, and the most bigoted
Australmaniac, having one pin’s point of sportsmanship
in him, could not have grudged the victors their grittily-
won honors. It opened on a chilly August day to an
enormous crowd. They sat on the turf to the ropes for
twenty feet beyond the seats, they filled the five rows of
garden seats—no backless planks at the Oval—and stood
six and seven deep behind on the level asphalt.- A day of
changing phases. Australia none for 47, then 4 for 82.
Noble to rescue with Gregory— not the Sid of old—to 126.
Next Armstrong collaborated to 174, and, later, exit
Noble, a fine 52. Trumble—Hopkins, slow, dull, but
very useful play till latter pavilioned at 256. Kelly
started clumsily, then opened out and saw 324 go up and
total ended. i
“ Second round, England 5 for 65, then Austral
bain-ackers and, it is hugely suspected, Austral players,
fatally cheapened their opponents, and that ‘ reduced
valuation ’ only disappeared, drib by drab, in the tension
throes of that last ghastly hour. England should have
followed on. She didn’t. Now we follow her two
matches behind in the Test totals. The least said of
Australia’s flitter-flutter the better. Cheap estimates
still ruled, and 50 runs were flung away—some day
anterior to the sea giving up its dead we shall know why.
That the tour has not been a great financial success we
know, and at one period, it is said, the Melb. C. C. were
called on for aid. Well, you needn’t kill a centipede with
a steam hammer, but a tack hammer sometimes lets him
escape.
“Third Round —and a ‘count out.’ A worn,
patchy wicket that had yielded 628 runs was asked to
breed 263 more, in opposition to one of the finest
‘ cohesive ’ teams ever got together. Lunch-time : 5 for
87—4 to Saunders—and 176 to win. Then Jessop took a
hand, or rather two hands, four eyes, and six feet, and,
ye gods, Josses and Buddhas, how he did hit! He col-
lected runs as 1 kids ’ collect cherry-stones, not for style
or shape, but just mere quantity. His bat was one huge
flat scythe that reaped its crop every slogging swing, and
the crowd went sheer, raving, yelling, frantic. He
slogged Saunders off his food—he never got another
wicket, and was kept on too long—and even may have
disturbed Trumble, whose trundling, unchanged (34 overs,
108 runs, 4 wkts.), was superb to the last run. Hirst, a
nugget of pluck who couldn’t spell ‘ fear,’ finished the
good work. He lent Lilley and Rhodes his rigid back-
bone and vim, and both batted as if naught of conse-
quence was in suspense. Lockwood, with the ‘ Surrey
rot ’ in his bones, played like a cow in bail with its legs
tied to the stumps. He should have got two ducks., not
two runs. But the Yorkshire pros, finished like iron-
barks, and when Rhodes, after the tie, hit the winning
single, few onlookers knew whether the run was ever
completed, for pandemonic cheers reigned, the fieldsmen
rushed off, and the thousands fled in two mighty con-
verging armies across the ground to the pavilion gates.
“To sum up : Darling, in that last innings, was
not at his best as a general, and the chances missed are
nightmarish to think of now. Kelly will never, never be
a Blaekham. The hero of the vanquished team was
Trumble, easily; Noble next, Armstrong, Hopkins,
Saunders, and not least, though last, Trumper—oh, that
silly, suicidal run-out! ”
* • • *
London Times, writing about the difference
between Test Match conditions in Australia and England,
likewise the difference in representative character between
the teams sent from Australia to England and vice versa,
suggests that the alternating visits should happen not
oftenerthan every third year, for the reason that “ though
on patriotic grounds our leading amateurs may be per-
suaded to go to Australia once in six years, they will not
go oftener.” The Times hangs on to the genteel Tory
doctrine that several “ gentlemen ” are essential to a
propah All England Eleven, whereas Australian records
show that the best English teams seen in this country
during the past 20 years were composed entirely of pros.,
working under the speculative management of Shaw and
Shrewsbury—the same old Shrewsbury who topped the list
of batting averages this season. Amateur and prof essional
elements never seem to mingle effectively in a touring
eleven ; nor is it clear that any All England combination
can be made stronger than the best possible team of Players
only. The men we send ’Ome are for the time being pro-
fessionals—a profit-sharing band travelling as equals.
If the visits of English Elevens were arranged by the
authorities at this end with a view to cricket rather than
a desire to make a pot of money for the Melbourne
Cricket Club, and a treat for a few of the local girls who
chase the “gentlemen” around the sassiety circle, then
the teams would be all professionals playing in grim
earnest for the dollars.
* » * *
0. B. Fry, writing in the Strand, names Frank
Iredale above all other Australian batsmen for “ polish
of style.” Complimentary all round, he gives Trumper a
blazing record. “ A batsman of batsmen,” “ a great
lesson in cricket to watch how he uses his feet,” “ able
to play the same free-and-easy kind of game on all kinds
of wickets,” “ never had a superior in attractiveness of
style wedded to thorough efficiency.”
• * * *
“Adelaide” writes: Cabled statement of
Darling and Trumble that they will not visit England
again reads like a threat, but probably is merely a
prophecy. Trumble i, a wonder, but in 1905 he will be
38, and a bowler who kept up to international form at
that age would be a marvel indeed. It seems likely that
a fresh generation of. players will soon be called for, as it
was about 1894-5. Jones and Howell have pretty cer-
tainly made their last trip, perhaps Kelly, too. Saunders
and Hopkins look all right, but when a man does not gain
international honors till he is 27 his tenure is apt to be
precarious. Gregory and Carter-both doubtful, still I
would rather have Charlie Gregory’s chance of going to
England in 1905 than any one of those mentioned ; and am
also open to bet that (at the outside) not more than half
of the present team will go. - On the other hand, Trumper
and Noble, Hill and Duff, ought all to be at their absolute
best then ; and, with luck, Armstrong may by that time
be another Trumble. But there is a big chance coming
for some young bowlers and stumpers.
* * * *
Jessop’s upheaval of 104 in the fifth test, which
England won by a wicket, was marvellous. Before he
came in everything was cramped and labored—England
was down, with Australia standing over her, and Trumble
personified the giant dealing with puny martyrs. Jessop
went for victory—neck or nothing. Good length balls
were sent fluttering round to make a disturbance among
the serried ranks of spectators on the leg side. Saunders,
who came with his arm, went with his legs. Jessop liked
Saunders. He didn’t give the ball time to pitch and
break back in the way that had caused Saunders to be
dubbed the “Boomerang”; he never moved out of his
crease, but, stretching forward in that unique crouching
manner which has no imitators, got to the ball before it
could do its worst, and slogged fearfully, while all the
field wondered. Jessop’s 200 against Yorkshire at Lords
last year was more punishing, but in the cirs. this 104
topped his fame. Thrice he drove Trumble out of the
circle, the biggest stroke pitching clean over the pavilion.
And this despite that the wicket all through favored
bowlers. When the little man does let himself go he is
really great.
Australians finished up “down under’’oppos-
ing Players of England, and match resulted in a draw.
Players notched 356. Notable feature was Trumble’s
failure with the ball; his figures at the end were 1 for
129, while Saunders got 4 for 50, and Noble 3 for 85.
Next effort saw three British wickets down for 147. Of
these, Noble’s bag was 1 for 40, Armstrong’s 1 for 12, and
Hopkins’ 1 for 22. Visitors gathered 414 in their only
innings. Towards this Trumper brought 96, Clem Hill
81, and Trumble 68. Trumper’s compilation carried him
within 32 of an aggregate of 2600 ; while “Sid.” Gregory
wound up a tour, throughout which he has been singu-
larly and conspicuously unlucky, with a score of 22 (run
out) —just 1 short of his thousand. Noble wanted two
wickets to complete a hundred. Team left for S’Africa
Saturday last.
* * * *
Speaking of no-balling bowlers, a Melbourne
paper states that a certain Australian cricketer, who
thought he ought to have been chosen with the last team,
wrote to umpire Phillips, now in England, asking him to
no-ball Saunders when that player appeared on the
English cricket field. Phillips carefully pocketed the
letter, and showed it to the members of the Australian
Eleven ; now there is trouble in sight when the team
comes home—and a sorrowing man is daily practising
the Sandow system and boxing with a view to justifying
himself later on.

“ A.”: Points from Australian XI. statistics.
Paddington (N.S.W.) supplied first and second in the
batting. The three Vic. trundlers were first, second, and
third in the bowling. Trumper batted 53 times, was
always out, never failed to score. Saunders, 34 times at
the wicket, never made double figures once, and amassed
84 runs altogether. Gregory missed his 1000 in all
matches by one run. Noble was two short of 100 wickets.

Records held by 1902 team, and members of it.

turies, 24 ; best aggregate (Trumper, 2670) ; highest aver-
age (48, Trumper).

Cabled that gross receipts through Australians’
matches in England are £4270 less than those of last tour.
A big drop, but, at this, the fund won’t pan out too
badly, for the ’99 takings were much ahead of anything
known before, and, though the public heard that each
member of the combination secured £BOO, those who
ought to know insist that the dividend was considerably
more. M.O.C. people announce a profit, over recent tour
of Maclaren’s team in Australia, of £2794, which cannot
be called great, and this spite of Sydney record crowds.
» * * •
T’other day at Mallala (S.A.) a cricket team,
with six wickets down for 30 runs, was threatened with
ann'hilation, but two batsmen made a vigorous stand and
nearly 240 runs were smacked on for the seventh wicket
—a player named Dunstan hitting up 206 not out. The
S.A. inter-State selection committee should keep an eye
on such juniors, for among them are the champions of the
days to be. The Wheat State sadly wants another Hill or
Darling.
* * * *
N.S.W. Cricket Assn, should seriously consider
the advisability of bringing some of the best of the
country cricketers to Sydney with a view to their inclusion
in representative cricket. Last season there was plenty of
talent unearthed by Maclaren’s team in the country dis-
tricts, and it is time that N.S.W. was represented by
other than an eleven of metropolitan players in Sheffield
Shield matches. Certainly one or two are occasionally
given a chance against Queensland, Tasmania, or Maori-
land, which are considered weak cricketing States;
and if they are good enough for these fixtures they
should be good enough for inclusion against S.A. and
Vic. There has for many years been too much of the
orthodox cut-and-dried eleven representing N.S.W., and
it is time some of the young ’uns were given a chance.
• • * *
Affleck’s proposed Bill against playing on Sun-
day bore fruit at North Shore (Sydney) last week when a
policeman pounced down on five or six youths ■enjoying
their afternoon game of cricket, and arrested their
jaokets. He seemed to have a dim idea that it was too
soon to imprison the offenders themselves, so descended
on the heap of garments aforesaid and took them into
custody.
• a a «
“ Charley ” M‘Mahon, of Wentworth, judging
at the Adelaide September Show, told an interviewer that
the Darling River country is about played out as a re-
cruiting-ground for racers ; the breeders have sold their
best stallions for the Indian market and breed from any-
thing they have left. He advocates a tax on stallions, to
keep the brutes “ not worth a tenner” from perpetuating
rubbish.
* * * *
The first steeplechase started at Broken Hill,
15 years ago, isn’t finWhed yet. The favorite broke his
neck, ;the second ditto fell and broke up his rider, and
the {rest of them couldn’t find their way round and lost
the track. During the recent winter B.H. racing meetings
the reoord was one horse killed on each of the three days.
• * • «
Racing is a great game for all but the man (the
punter) who provides the wherewithal to keep it going.
V.A.T.O. report, to be presented Monday week, will tell
that stakes handed over during past 12 months totalled
£26,730 —£1110 in excess of the previous year’s amount.
Assets now show £11,052 better than liabilities. Cup and
Grand National meetings brought a profit of over £3OOO.
» * • *
Magda’s appropriation of the Spring Handicap
(lm.) at Port Adelaide Club’s meet on Saturday places her
• among the possibles in one or other of the coming Cups.
Her load at Caulfield will be 6.9 and at Flemington 7st.
On Saturday she humped Bst. to the front in 1.43 4-5.
Among other races that previously fell to same nag was
Adelaide R.C. Mayor’s Mile. Magda’s breeding too (by
Bill of Portland from La Tosca, by Robinson Crusoe) is
capital endorsement. At moment of penning this par.
her price for each Cup is 100 to 1.
* * * *
The second edition of Miller’s Sporting
Pamphlet is issued. The supplement gives the perform-
ances of Cup horses from July to September 15th and the
acceptances for both Cups up to the latter date. A good
few entrants seem to have dropped out since the first
edition of the pamphlet was issued. And still there’s
more to follow—nearly 100 more to follow.
» * • *
Only fitting that Mo. should have a good time
at City Tatt.’s Randwick meet, Saturday. Weronga,
twice heavily supported by owner Fielder during recent
A.J.C. Spring four days, gathered Flying Hep., going
out, not much wanted, at tens and twelves. In this race
running of red-hot favorite (5 to 2) Purser, whose big-
falling off since his sensational second to Sir Foote in the
Doncaster Hep. last Autumn, has sorely puzzled punters,
was questioned by stewards (more wide-awake than
usual), and owner Kelso and jock Want were severely
cautioned. Weronga also closed on Club Hep. ; this time
weightily backed. Biggest knock public experienced
during a very bad day was through principal event (City
Tatts. Hep.), which fell to rankest outsider of the field,
Still Water—a most pronounced in-and-outer, who lined
up at 12’s, while his stable chum, The Maine, was in
much demand at B’s. Still Water is a 4yr. old by Far
Niente (imp.) from Rivula, and fully sustains the reputa-
tion of all the same get, in that it is impossible to rely
upon him. He’ll show something tip-top to-day and sink
very flat to-morrow. Disappointments of the race were'
Westralian crack Warrior 11. and Lucknow, which dis-
puted favoritism. Warrior and Still Water were toge-;
ther in front till the home turn, where the former;
chucked it palpably. Trainer Lamond gave Warrior 11.
as something choice for the Metrop, but the nag made
no show at all, though on the track he travels and stays
good enough for anything. Still Water has Caulfield
(6.13) and Melbourne Cups’ (7st.) engagements and a fair
chance in one or other if he only runs honest.
• • * *
Man who drew Queen of Sheba in Tatt.’s
Metrop. sweep lives in Adelaide suburb Bowden, and
found getting employment an impossibility for some
time. Such were the straits he was reduced to that his
wife had to take on scrubbing and anything else in the
same line that offered.
• » * *
One time noted Queensland turfite and breeder,
Mr. Andrew Gordon, is dead. Vespasian and Darebin,
Sydney Cup winner 19 years ago, were once Mr. Gordon’s.
Famous brood mares Stockdove (imp.), dam of The Aus-
tralian Peer, and First Lady (imp.), dam of Grand Flaneur,
were brought to Queensland by him, and he bred, among
other good ’uns, The Australian Peer and Prime Minister.
* • » *
“J.R.”: There is something queer about that
“military ride” from Brussels to Ostend. Either the
men couldn’t ride, or they were miserably mounted, or
the cable has got the places mixed up. The distance
between the two places is 75 miles by road, and the men
took over 15 hours to do it —under five miles an hour !
» * * *
Boulder City R.C. (W.A.), after four days
racing, paid off £2500 debt on new grandstand, gave
£3550 in stakes, and came out with a balance of nearly
£2OOO to credit. Over £33,000 passed through the tote,
and yet nearly £3OO was collected in fees from the
bookies.
» • * *
Fonto, the horse that ran second for three Cups
—Kalgoorlie, Boulder, and Goolgardie—at recent gold-
fields round, was also second in the City llcp. at Boulder
and Kanowna. He is a six-year-old son by Tremando,
and at Geraldton the sons of his then owner used to trot
him out of a dirty, muddy stable, and pit him against any
hack that happened along for £1 wagers, running him up
and down the metalled street.
* * » *
Sandow’s length is 69J inches. The inevitable
compositor set up a 3 for a 9 in last week’s Bulletin
interview with the Muscle Maker, and several hundred
correspondents have pointed a scornful pen at the obvious
misprint. Snorters at a distance will please accept the
intimation that Sandow is half a foot taller than sft. 3£in.
* * * *
The M.L. amateur athletes, G. W. Smith and
Simpson, just returned from England, were told by the
easy-going Bull runners that the “ colonials ” and the
Americans took their racing altogether too seriously. The
Maorilanders were asked to tour the States, but couldn’t
do so. American pedestrian Orton proposes to organise a
lilywhite team to tour Australasia next year.
* * • *
’Murkan heavyweight bruiser Jim Scanlan had
a narrow squeak with half-bred Maori Ruenalf at New-
castle t’other night. Ruenalf never did anything to
warrant the impression that he had a hope against the
man who, without much apparent effort, squelched negro
Ike Stewart decisively, but form was knocked endways,
for Ruenalf made at least an even thing of it and fought
cleverly enough to score often. In the fourth round
things fairly buzzed, and Ruenalf seemed to have the
fight, but Scanlan pulled himself together very well.
They were often locked, and each evidently beside him-
self, punched in holds. Referee Ford cautioned that if
same thing occurred again he would disqualify the
offender. Ere the fifth was well under way, Ruenalf
thumped a hard one home while holding and Scanlan got
the money. Decision maddened the Maori, who went for
his man, and a rough-up, lasting several seconds, occurred
till friends sundered them. Another match ’tween same
two would draw a big house, and, perhaps, result
differently. , , »
Attenuated houses at both Sydney boxing clubs
for weeks past herald, it is to be hoped, the passing of
the unclean scrapping novice, whose smellful pre-
sence has been tolerated too long. Crowds have
thinned out to almost nothing lately, till the
wonder is how promoters give the money advertised
and pay exes. Competitors, too, are falling off in num-
bers, until it is so hard to get them that rival halls run
competitions on different nights in the week, with such
elastic weight limits as will allow the same lot to figure at
each place—a stone or so is a mere trifle. Which reminds
that the accepted definition of a novice should prove a
curiosity, for the same old names and faces of years ago
are still" being trotted out. This form of pugilistic inflic-
tion threatens to completely throttle the more legitimate
game. ,
Ancient bruiser, Bill Jennings, from way back
in the early nineties, faced Australian artilleryman, Jack
Thompson, at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday night, and
opened the eyes of the crowd by the condition he had
worked himself into. All that aggressive abdominal
development he was wont to hump round for years, had
disappeared, and Jennings looked something like the
scrapper he used to be. But the stiffness of action and
lack of force begotten of increasing age showed painfully
in contrast to Thompson’s unusual vigor, power, and
mobility. Still, Jennings showed a great fight, and,
though a gory spectacle early, he kept going, en-
during heavy punishment in the hope of finding Thomp-
son’s weak spot. Once or twice it seemed as if Jennings
had a chance, and in the fourth round Thompson got one
in the wind that made him look round as if inclined to
wilt, but he came again, and served out thump cruelly.
Thompson drove home heavy rib-benders that told every
time, and occasionally the left pounded the other side.
It was marvellous how the old battler stood it, but he did
until his seconds, moved by pity, elevated the rag in
token of defeat. Jennings’ grit was as pronounced as
when he fought that never-to-be-forgotten butchering
match with Herb M‘Kell (since dead) in Melbourne years
ago : but grit doesn’t always win.
» * * *
Forty thousand turned up and yielded a £9OO
gate for final match of Victorian foothall premiership,
last Saturday. Gollingwood and Essendon furnished a
good game for first half, thereafter the “ Woods ” went
away and got the pennant by 60 points to 27. That £9OO
gate was responsible for a slanderous report. The cranky
rules of the League provide for two rounds of matches
between the eight competing clubs. Then the eight play
off to a final, and the winner of this may be challenged
by the highest scorer in the two earlier rounds. Colling-
wood was this challenger, but, having got into the semi-
final, it is accused of having deliberately gone down to
Fitzroy with a view to the extra match and the lovely
“ gate ” aforesaid. Several Gollingwood hotels sold out
that night, and things were very merry down that way ;
but writer has his doubts about the popularity of football
next year. (He lost a sov. over the game, and is naturally
pessimistic.) *
N.S.W. tennis championships ended Saturday.
Holder of singles and joint holder of doubles, Dr. Kearney,
was.a lot short of “ trim,” but still-showed boldly against
apparently invincible Englishman, Dr. Eaves, who
repeated his performance of 11 years ago when he wrested
the singles from Webb. Rice and Gaden won doubles
supremacy, playing finely throughout. Miss Payten
again asserted her right to the title of lady champion by
finishing successfully in all three available premierships—
Miss M. Dransfield being associated with her in the
doubles, and Mr. Rice in the challenge pairs. Best match
of the week was ’tween Dr. Eaves and Brookes (N.S.W.).
Former forced wonderfully, every stroke was intended to
win right out, but Brooke’s defence wanted a lot of
getting over. So clever is Eaves that, while in the centre
of the court himself all the time, he had his opponent
running to the verge of exhaustion.
» » * *
At Bolton (Eng.), Thursday last week, Aus-
tralian sprint swimmer, Fred. Lane, wiped out prior best
(1.37 3-5) for 150 yards by lsec. Previous figures were
created by Englishman Derbyshire in July, ’Ol, when he
cut 4-ssec. off the then existing best, which also belonged
to him, and was accomplished the year before.
* * * *
Published statement that Yankee bikist, F.
M’Farland (6.4), is the tallest at the game in the world is
wrong. Sydney amateur D. Ellis (6.5) owns that distinc-
tion.
Cyclists in N.S.W. should welcome Pearson’s
“ Cyclists and Travellers’ District Road Map ” —and not
alone they, but all tourists. The book gives maps, on
linen, of the whole State and of the various tourist
districts with the length and character of the various
roads clearly marked. There is, further, a letter-press
description of 18 tours, designed chiefly for the cyclist,
but also available to the horseman and the tramp. The
book is published by John Sands, Sydney.

A Melbourne case, heard in camera a while ago,
threw a lot of cerulean light (for the lawyers) on the
darkly-suspected subject of bicycle riding-schools and the
instruction that ladies gain thereat. The darlings do get
instructed, to be sure.
_
Trenwith’s jibe, characterising the bogus
Reform League as the League of the three B’s,
just meets the case. Share-brokers, pawn-
brokers and stone-brokers.
Bill Lyne told the Sydney woman-
suffragists “ Queen Victoria was a woman, and
she sat on the British Crown for 60 years ! ” And
Crowns are this shape :—AAA !
Sachse, M.L.C. —of all people ! —has
announced the watchwords of the Irvine Govern-
ment party for the forthcoming Victorian
elections. “Honesty, Activity, and Firmness”
is the inscription on the back of the Irvine pro-
gramme, says Sachse. Doubtless Shiels will
represent the Firmness, and fat T. Bent the
Activity, and they will divide the Honesty among
them.
Theodore Fink, the great somersault artist
of Victorian politics, voted for Irvine in the recent
crisis. You never know which side Theodore will
land. It is reckoned that he has belonged to both
parties three times in the last two years.
Ever since the downfall of the Patterson
Govt., which, it was asserted, was largely con-
tributed to by an aggrieved civil service, the
organised service vote has been feared in Mel-
bourne politics. Hence the desperate efforts of
the Age and Argus to destroy the cohesion of the
public servants by proclaiming that they have
joined in an “unnatural and unholy” alliance
with the Labor party against the lovely Bent.
The Age-Argus alliance is also unnatural and
unholy—but that is a different story.
J. S. White, who misrepresented Albert
Park in the Vic. Parliament, recanted on his
knees, and with tears and abject supplication, the
vote he gave against the Irvine Government. “ He
thought civil servants should be paid what they
were worth. Personally he thought his vote
against the Government was right but under the
circumstances he felt that, in the best interests of
the State, he should accord Irvine his undeviating
support.” These touching “ circumstances ” were
that he thereby became the nominee of the
Government and the sham Reform League. And
the “strong, reforming Government” takes the
support of shifty dodgers like this, who openly
abjure their alleged principles for place and pay.
It is cabled that the British Tory Govern-
ment proposes to screw an indemnity of a hundred
million pounds out of the Transvaal and the
Orange Free State. In proportion to population
(including niggers), that is eight times the in-
demnity Germany exacted from France, and that
was the biggest indemnity in history till now. In
proportion to the white population, it is about 30
times the French indemnity. It is about times
the record of Spain’s exactions from Cuba ; and it
will leave the two late Republics with a debt that,
in proportion to population, will beat the world’s
record, and in proportion to the white population
will beat the world’s record about six times over.
This stupendous indemnity wasn’t mentioned,
apparently, among the terms of surrender.
Victorian political history contains few
things so mendacious as Knight Gillott’s shame-
less desertion of his party. Gillott, as Mayor of
Melbourne, was elected to Parliament to support
the Turner-Peacock party. He was Attorney-
General in the Turner-Peacock Ministry, and
went into Opposition with Peacock when Irvine
and his Tory crowd lied their way on to the
Treasury benches. He voted against the Irvine
Government on the motion that brought about
the dissolution, and afterwards attended a caucus
meeting of his party to arrange for the campaign.
Then the next thing heard from this honorable
knight was that he had signed the Reform
League’s ticket, and had promised to desert his
party and sit behind Irvine. Although not
excusable, it is understandable when impecunious
political hangers-on desert their party in order to
cling to the £3OO a year; but Gillott is a wealthy
man, and there is no need for him to sell his party
and principles for bread.
A Pathetic Partins'.
A writer recently described the light-hearted
manner in which modern people say good-bye at
wharf or station. He tells how folks set out on a
long pilgrimage with the possibility of never
seeing their friends again, and they say farewell
as if it were quite a jolly affair after all. There
are still partings at which anguish keeps the
tongue silent and the cheek wet. But there are
partings which should be merry, partings which
we should celebrate like a wedding. It is a
pleasure to chronicle such partings, and con-
sequently we make public the parting of Mr.
Michael Dwyer, Fernmount, N.S.W., who said
a long good-bye to acute indigestion. He speaks
as follows—“For many years 1 have been a
victim to severe indigestion, dyspepsia, and loss
of energy. I tried many medicines. Some would
make we feel well for a day, and then I would be
as bad as ever. There was a constant mist before
my eyes, and like a ball of fire in my breast. I
gave up trying remedies, and made up my mind
to have nothing to do with them; but I was
induced to try one dose of Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills, and I can inform you I was agreeably
surprised in finding that they other
medicines taken so often by me. I immediately
procured a bottle, and since taking them I can
speak from experience, and now recommend them
as a certain cure for indigestion. They do not
cause griping or pain, and seem to build up the
whole system, at the same time clearing it of all
bad substances. I am cured, and my sight clear
as of old.” Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a
perfect blood purifier, and a positive cure for
biliousness, indigestion, constipation, headaches,
sallow complexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles,
pimples, boils and blotches, and for female
ailments. Sold by chemists and storekeepers, Is
lid per bottle, or six bottles 6s 6d. Sole pro-
prietors, The W. H. Comstock Co., Limited.
(Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Packed in amber bottles and the full name blown
thereon.

Satisfactorily settled by the magistrates in
Co. Kildare, Ireland (of all places) that for a
soldier to kiss a girl at a window, with her consent,
is not “riotous and indecent behaviour.” The
only remarkable point is that the town clerk
thought it was. There were people passing, said
this Puritan survival, and he “thought it was in-
decent, happening in the public view.”
Some more Drayton Grange points :—
Col. Lyster to the Drayton Grange Commission : “ The
more he read Captain Shields’ evidence, the more con-
vinced he was that that officer was inexperienced.”
Well, Lyster had just before told the Commission that
his “ field-return,” as he called it, showed there were only
1918 men and 41 officers on the ship : “ If there had been
any more his officers would have reported it; they called
the roll.” That is to say, a sergeant of each company
called the roll of his company, and someone said “ Here !”
to each name. Then the sergeant reported “ all present ”
to the captain, who passed the report on. Which simply
proves Lyster to have added another to his innumerable
silly' statements. As to the number actually on board
the plague-ship, the chief steward said : “ They first pro-
vided for 143 tables, and increased that by nine. Such an ;
estimate would make a total of 2142.” If the “ex-
perienced " Colonel Lyster’s full total of 1918 men is taken
from that, it leaves 224 stowaways at least. But there i
were more than 14 men in some of the messes. One man ji
said there Were 10 extra in his mess, and another ;■
had three extra in iiis. At that rate, allowing j
only two extra on an average to each mess,
there would be 2448 men on board—27J per cent. |
more than the “ experienced ” Lyster knew of. I

The A.W.U. and the Arbitration Court.
Mb. Abthuh Rae writes
Your article on “Industrial Arbitration in Working”
(B. 13/9/’O2), as well as previous allusions to the case of
the Australian Workers’ Union in the Arbitration Court,
although couched in the kindliest terms, displays a want
of knowledge of some of the facts.
Permit me, therefore, to state them. The President
of the A.W.U. lodged a protest with the Registrar
of the Arbitration Court against the registration of
the Machine Shearers’ Union, when the registration
of that body was first announced in the daily
press. The A.W.U. general secretary, accompanied by
a solicitor, subsequently waited upon the Registrar to
ascertain how he purposed dealing with applications for
cancellation of registration, the Act being silent as to the
method and no rules of procedure having been framed for
the conduct of the Court. The Registrar replied that he
could only take action upon lodgment of an affidavit
setting forth reasons for the application. In answer to a
direct question from the Union solicitor, the Registrar
further stated that the Court would have full power to
consider all matters pertaining to the case whether raised
before him (the Registrar) or not.
An affidavit was then lodged with the Registrar in
which the M.S.U. was charged with being a bogus Union,
and several reasons detailed to back up that charge. In
addition the evident intention of the Act to permit only
one Union to exist for each industry was duly set forth.
The bogus character of the M.S.U. was further elabo-
rated when the case was subsequently argued before the
Registrar by both sides. As a result of that argument
the Registrar recommended the court to cancel the M.S. U.
certificate of registration on the ground that the Act
never contemplated the existence of two unions in the
one industry occupying the same field. The Registrar,
deeming this reason all-sufficient, delivered no opinion
upon the question of the bona tides of the defendant
union.
The conduct of the Judge, when the case reached the
Court, displayed but very little knowledge of the Act. He
first held that the Court had no authority to deal with
any phase of the question other than the one on which
the Registrar had based his recommendation, contending
at the same time that Spence’s protest, which had not
raised the question of bona fides, was the basis of the
application, and not M‘Donell’s affidavit, which had de-
finitely raised that issue. He therefore refused to allow
discussion as to the genuineness or otherwise of the defen-
dant M.S.U., nor would he consent to an adjournment for
the purpose of amending the protest. This was mani-
festly bad law and worse equity, for the protest had
never been sworn to nor even witnessed, had never been
considered by the Registrar in the preliminary proceed-
ings, and for aught the Court knew might have been put
in by P. U. Abbott instead of A. W. U. Spence.
The Registrar only took action upon the sworn affidavit
of the A. W.U. secretary, which did impute mala fides to
the bogus union, and there was neither justice nor
technical legality in confining the case to narrower limits
than those contained in that document. That the mind
of the Judge was in a state of flux was clearly shown
when at the close of the plaintiff union’s opening state-
ment he announced he had changed his mind so far as to
allow any matter brought before the Registrar to be
argued before the Court, but that he must still confine
the issues to those raised in the original and informal
protest.
in the reply of the plaintiff Union we therefore
witnessed the extraordinary spectacle of a Judge calmly
listening to arguments proving that the defendant Union
was spending more on legal fees for one day than its total
income for a whole year, y'et refusing to make any use of
the evidence he admitted. The whole Act is built upon
the principle that there shall be perfect bona fides both
on the part of employers and employees. Yet when that
basic principle is impugned and the evidence in support
of the charge is st> strong, a Judge totally unhampered
by rules of procedure, and clothed with greater powers
than any other judge in the Commonwealth to deal with
all matters in equity and good conscience, refuses to move
except upon another application. And The Bulletin
applauds his resolve. What would a Higinbotham have
done in such a case ? If against the policy of the Act to
have two Unions, he would have wiped the mushroom
Union out of existence and told the other one that the
Court would refuse preference of employment to its
members until the objectionable rules were eliminated.
The members of the M.S.U. would have not only suffered
no injustice by such a decision but would have gained a
distinct advantage by sharing the advantages of unionism
without contributing to its up-keep.
The only possible losers by the cancellation of the
M.S.U. registration would have been the squatters who
are using that body as a buffer between law and justice.
Of course, The Bulletin was not aware of the
proceedings before the Registrar in Arbitration
until now informed by Mr. Rae. But the new facts
do not affect its conclusions. From Mr. Rae’s letter
it may be seen that there is a good deal of blame
to be attached to others than A.W.U. officials
for the contretemps in regard to the shearers’
trouble, but those officials don’t secure a verdict
of acquittal at all. Before the Registrar, the
A.W.U. should have (and probably any counsel
would have told them this) set forth definitely
the issues on which it claimed that
the M.S.U. should be refused registration,
and seeing that that Court was one of
primary jurisdiction only, should have pressed for
decision on all the issues raised, or a reference of
all the issues to the higher court. When the
matter came to the higher court the A.W.U.
seems (from Mr. Rae’s letter) to have been repre-
sented by two persons with different pleas—Mr.
Spence with one, Mr. M‘Donnell with a different
one. The very best of cases would be spoiled by
such conflicting advocacy. To say that Justice
Cohen’s law was bad is just a say-so. If it were
so, why not have appealed to the Full Court?
Justice Higinbotham must have done in the
ease just what Justice Cohen did —confined
the issue to the point raised by the plea of
the A.W.U. Finally, as already stated, the
A.W.U., anyhow, went into court doomed to
failure because of the nature of its rules; and,
construing the Arbitration Act in a spirit of
equity, it would, honestly, have been extremely
difficult for a court of justice to endorse it as the
sole labor union in the pastoral industry. It is
because The Bulletin knows the good work of
the A.W.U. in the past, and believes that it is the
best hope of the bush worker in the future, that it
has pointed out to the A.W.U. officials the mis-
takes they made. It will be of more value to
remedy these mistakes than to seek to defend
them.

Governor Denison, who inaugurated Re-
sponsible Government in N.S.W., had ideas about
making iron rails locally for the colony’s infant
railway system, but put the matter aside as being
too large a contract. On the other hand, he fore-
saw that to pay for imported rails to rail the
country as he wanted would necessitate slow going,
and Imperiously contemplated.building railways °f
hardwood. He was a competent engineer, and a
sight of the time was to see him with samples of
hardwoods at Government House, calculating
what pressure they would bear on inland railways.

Scott’s Emulsion is a scientifically blended

A distinguished physician who made com-
