“P.X.” to “Waddy” on the Miners’
Accident Relief Fund (B., 6/9/’02): —
It may be unfair to collect for the fund as much from
the few-shillings-a-week youngster as from the full-paid
miner. But it’s only 4Jd. a week in either case, and to
have sacrificed the simplicity of the scheme would have
been dangerous and (in view of the smallness of the con-
tributions) not worth the risk. Also if the boy (he must
be at least 15 to be legally employed on a mine) were to
pay in less he could only expect to draw less ; and the
present allowances (12s. a week duringdisablement, and 8s.
a week pension, payable to a dependent mother or depen-
dent sisters as well as to a widow) are surely the irre-
ducible minimum. The Act is so remarkably good a one
that, if it has little weaknesses, they should only be
looked at with one eye, while the other one (and prefer-
ably the stronger) is glued tight on the unspeakable
benefits conferred.
Persons “in the know ” recognised from
the jump that the game of thelrvine-Shiels crowd
was to get a dissolution at the earliest possible
moment, and no previous Vic. Govt, has contrived
to get in so many inflammatory speeches as those
of Premier Irvine himself and Tom Bent on the
railway crisis, and of wily Treasurer Shiels at odd
intervals when submitting his various reduction
of screws Bills. When one of his own supporters
very late one night moved the adjournment, so as
to come up fresh to a speech the next day, the
usually cold and “polished” Irvine wrathfully
proclaimed that he would take it as a censure on
the Govt, if the House packed up its traps for the
night. And the moody Equity man wa3 beginning
to look quite worried because Parliament wouldn’t
give him a show to parade his spirit and go back
to his country constituents, who are naturally
[ delighted to find that their land is not to be taxed
and the public servants are, and, also, that the
Govt, has contrived to upset the Factories Act,
under an appearance of the utmost solicitude for
that unfortunate piece of legislation.
“ Burglar Bill’s ” letter:—
I was unlucky enough to be confined in a country gaol
during the last three weeks of a murderer’s life. From
the time the Executive confirmed the sentence the con-
demned man started to sing regularly at 9 p.m. and kept
it up until tired out. “ Thinking of Home and Mother,”
“Annie Rooney,” “Half-mast High” and so forth kept
ringing through the silent wing while the cell-occu-
pants lay and listened. Three nights before the man was
slaughtered the rope was tried, with a bafr of sand as a
“dummy,” and as the bolt of the drop was stiff (not
having been worked for years) “ Nosey Bob,” the hang-
man, had to use a sledge-hammer and much force. He
finished his “rehearsal ” at 12 midnight, the condemned
man being in his cell about 5ft. away from the drop. (He
had refused to move to another wing for the time, but
there were 87 other prisoners in the wing to be edified by
the preparations.) I myself “ lived ” only three cells from
the “condemned cell,” and the tramping to and fro past
my cell-door shook my nerves for many a week after.
The night before the execution the rope was dangling
from the beam on the second landing when the 87
unfortunates were marching in to their cells at “ lock-
up ” time. I occupied the nearest cell to the drop on the
middle-landing, and through a small crack where a plate
(closing the old “ feeding hole ”) had sprung, could just
see the ghastly thing.
I watched the priests go in at 7 a.m. (no prisoners let
out till after execution). Governor and Nosey Bob
inspected the drop at 7.30, numerous inquisitive warders
between 7.30 and 8.30, and then stillness, awful, horrible,
choking. Nine o’clock struck, and I Watched the pro-
cession to the door of the cell—saw the doomed wretch
led out, and pulled myself away from that crack by a
mighty effort just as the “ bolt ” was about to be drawn.
The “thug” of the fall, however, could be heard all
through the wing.
Note the “ mighty effort ” with which the shocked
Bill tore himself away from the spectacle.

A strange story comes down from Cape
York Peninsula re a party of police who, having
overtaken sundry blacks guilty of certain trifling
depredations, settled the score in the good old
way by exterminating the aborigines in one act.
There is serious trouble a-brew over the business*
and sensational disclosures may be shortly antici-
pated.
Fizzin' fur the D.'s.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yer come outer stir,
Full up of it all,
Fur the chanst is too fur
Of yer makin a haul;
But yer waller in welf,
An’ live at yer ease,
When yer biz.
Is ter fizz —
Ter fizz fur the D.’s.
Yer shook on the scheme
Ter be hones’ an’ straight,
An’ yer ponder an’ dream
On a vision that’s great;
Fur they’s whips er shine women,
An’ liquor an’ spons,
When yer lurk
Is ter work
Fur the demons an’ Johns.
They’s heaps er good tips
Fur ter pick up at Pen,
An’, roundin’ the kips,
Yer kin pump ’em, an’ then
Yer off ter the demons
Ter sell what yer got,
When yer through
Wif a few
Ter squeal on an’ pot.
Yer kin pile it on strong
When yer know yer believed
(Your troubles on wrong
“ Information received ”).
Jes’ pot ’em whenever
Yer see they’s a show,
When yer game
Is ter name
The fellers yer know.
They’s twenty or more
As had done yer a turn.
Or has come up an’ swore—
But they’s money ter ear 1—
Yer worm out their secrets
An’ put ’em away,
When you pick
On the trick
As is likely ter pay,
Yer turned a new leaf
An’ yer hones’ an’ square,
No more yer a thief,
An’ yer treadin’ on air ;
Yer proud an’ .yer happy,
Yer mind is at ease
When yer biz
Is ter fizz,
Ter fizz fur the D’s.

Mr Wm. Stanley, said to be the oldest pianist in the
Australias, died last week, aged 82 years. He was born
at Windsor, and came to Australia in 1837. He took part
in the opening of the old Victoria Theatre in Pitt-street, on
St. Patrick’s Day, 1838.
The yarn used to be current in Melbourne that Mrs.
Tallowfat brought her precious daughters to the Presby-
terian Ladies' College, and said to the head-master . t
“Oh, I hope you won’t turn them into Melbas!” To
which he replied “My dear madam, there is not the
slightest danger of that, I assure you.” All she wanted
was enough music for husband-snavelling.
“ N.” : Melba’s chums, of the Presbyterian Ladies’ Col-
lege, are effusive, and, indeed, such an astounding num-
ber turn up as to make one think of the multifarious
remnants of the true cross or the 16,000 survivors of the
charge of the 600. At a visit to the college, not very long
ago, on a festive occasion, I remember that, although
30 photos, of more or less distinguished former pupils
were on the walls, Melba’s face was not among them.
Further, the Principal made a speech, recounting the col-
lege triumphs, and never made even a distant allusion to
Melba. What a change !
Melba comes home like an antipodean Magda in many
respects. Papa Mitchell was just as averse to Miss Nellie
becoming a public singer as was the dull old middle-class
military German in Sudermann’s drama.. But Melba s
father has more sense, and has changed his mind so far
as to be one of Nellie’s most enthusiastic admirers.
Sudermann’s shocked old German had no sense—only
chest.
Sydney Tivoli programme, the other week, described
Miss Dorothy Vane as “ nee Mrs. Norman Menzies.”
The Melba boom in Nellie’s natal place results in the
selling of little brassbound miniatures of the greatest
soprano by street-hawkers, prices varying from 6d. to 2s.
6d., and the demand is big. The little street-bred people
have thrown aside racecards to sell Melba’s photo—which
in Australia is glory in excelsis.
Failure of Robert Newman, the London concert
manager, will, possibly, be of some inconvenience to one
or two Australian singers. Florence Schmidt and Amy
Oastles were among those under his wing.
Abbott’s Opera House, Auckland (M.L.), desires to
announce that it is now open to accept tenants. The
theatre seats 1671 persons when they all get there at
once. The stage is 40ft. deep and 67ft. wide, &c., &c.
Everything on the premises is lovely, and the cable
address is “ Matakana,” Auckland.
The Sunday-school crowd has been ingeniously worked
for Rickards’ Sandow show in the eastern States (in the
west, where there is no Sunday-school crowd, he shared
the limelight with the lovely undraped Lotty). At
Ballarat Her Majesty’s an agile, bright and harmless
song-and-dance turn was temporarily withdrawn so that
the men of prayer might see the man of muscle without
being shocked by the spectacle of a girl in tights. A
shocked Sunday-school is the best advertisement —only it
is the girl in tights who gets it.
Sandow told a Melbourne interviewer a yarn with some
warm blood in it : “I wrestled in Italy for many years as
an amateur. It was in Berlin that I encountered one of
the strongest and most unprincipled wrestlers that the
world ever saw. He possessed phenomenal finger
strength. On one occasion he was known to drive his
fingers through the wall of a man’s stomach, and almost
pluck out his intestines. You see these marks ? ” San-
dow bared his arm and disclosed two round white marks
as of old wounds that had healed, but left their brand
behind. “ I wrestled him,” said Sandow. The light of
battle shone in his eye. “ That’s where he gripped me,
drove his fingers into my arm, and tried to grasp my
sinews. I rushed him, squeezed him round the body,
broke three of his ribs, and nearly broke his back. I
bear my mark ; he has never been right since.”
The other week George Musgrove stated that an Anglo-
Australian Theatrical Trust would shortly come about.
Now it would appear that J.O.W. is likely to join
the Trust as permanent Australian representative.
The prospect of a new and all-powerful “ Firm,” having
its headquarters in London, is not an unmitigated joy.
Such a Firm, or Trust, could bring smashing resources to
bear against rivals and flourish on its immunity from
dangerous competition. We want competition. The
liveliest days of Australian show business were from Oct.
1886 to Dec. ’92, when Brough and Boucicault ran their
own theatres in Melbourne and Sydney. It was during
this time of competition that Musgrove seceded from the
old Firm and started management on his own. The Nellie
Stewart Opera co., with “Paul Jones,” “Boccaccio,” etc.,
Mrs. Bernard Beere’s co., and the third Gaiety co. were
notable results of Musgrove’s break away.
“ J.P." : The difference between Ouyler Hastings and
H. Plimmer in the character of Sherlock Holmes is mainly
the difference between an actor who knows the original
Gillette’s performance by heart and an actor who lacks
that advantage. Ouyler Hastings was Gillette’s under-
study, and when he came to rehearsal with the company
that had been playing up to Plimmer, he showed the
stage-manager some points. The drama, as Melbourne
sees it, is said to be much more catchy than it appeared
in Perth a few weeks ago. The new man gives to the
name-part an interestingly unnatural individuality,
which seems to explain his conduct. He plays an impos-
sible person in a peculiar way, whereas Plimmer betrayed
the unrealness of the character by playing it naturally.
His own method was not sufficiently odd, or sombre.
The late Scot Inglis might have given a striking ‘ ‘ crea-
tion ” from Gillette’s stage directions without seeing
Gillette at work. Nat Goodwin would probably shine as
a new variety of Sherlock Holmes. A fine part for any
clever actor with a clean-cut face and style, incisive
Speech, and the art of cold repose. Apostle Worthington
Btrikes one as an ideal Sherlock —wasted.
Alterations in names and addresses in the
city and suburbs, for insertion in “Sands’ Direc-
tory,” 1903, should be sent to the compiler not
later than the first week in October.
When King Richard of Maoriland was
leaving for the Coronation he was voted £l5OO to
keep up his paunch and dignity. It now
transpires that during his absence his draft for a
further £3OOO turned up in a casual, lordly way,
and was paid. Fifteen politicians of the Seddon
party tried to interview Acting-Premier Ward
about this off-hand method of dealing with the
public funds, but Ward declined to see them.
Whether the money was for the M.L. contingents,
or for Seddon’s new Windsor uniform and the
purchase of whisky with which to shout for the
Boer generals, appears to be a mystery. Probably
it was for the Contingents, but it was an illegal
spending all the same.
The Prince of Braganza’s successful de-
fence to an obscene charge came pretty rough
on the cult of royalty, anyhow. His Nibs,
who is a grandson of a one-time King of Portugal,
told tli6 London Criminal Court that he came to
London for the purpose of attending the Corona-
tion ; arrived at 7 in the morning, lunched,
napped, dined, went to the Empire Music Hall on
his own, with considerable champagne inside him,
and met a girl. At 11.30 p.m. he had to keep an
appointment with his brother at the Hotel Cecil,
where he took more champagne before returning
to the Empire to meet the girl again. She was to
have been waiting for him outside the theatre, but
she didn’t turn up. A bookmaker’s clerk and two
youths accosted the noble prince and offered to
conduct him to a bagnio. He followed them to a
house, gave the youths a sov. apiece for their
kindness to a stranger in a strange land, went into
a room and threw himself down on the bed, and fell
into a drunken slumber, from which he was
awakened by a policeman and the landlord.
That’s the prince’s story of how he started to play
his part as a distinguished visitor to the Corona-
tion. If many of the illustrious crowd enjoyed a
similarly intellectual time, and probably they did,
the atmosphere of the historic ceremony at West-
minster Abbey must have been spirituous. No
wonder the poor old Archbishop of Canterbury
gave way in the legs.

Cabled that “200 Boer prisoners at St.
Helena are signing allegiance to Great Britain
daily.” Great Scot ! and haven’t we been told for
at least three years that the Boer can’t write ?
Senator Keating, lecturing at Melbourne
Trades Hall on “ Municipal Advancement,”
proudly quoted his beloved Launceston as an in-
stance of progress. And, for its size, Launceston
is not a bad instance of municipal socialism, and
goes to show that a small live community may
stay awake even in a State of sleep.

Despite the frothy mouthings of wildly
excited “loyalists’'-at the Pro-Boer oratory of
Barclay, member for Dunedin city (M.L.), a while
back, and the determination of Liberals and Labor
people to knock' him off his political perch, the
Dunedin Political Workers’ organisation has now
placed him on its ticket for next election. Public
ardor for the “ good cause ” in B’Africa has visibly
cooled in the interim, and Seddon is fast becoming
a lonesome and raging anomaly in this matter.

A good ad. was given to a member of the
demi-monde t’other day by a Ballarat pressman
who has the Sandow craze badly. The demi-
monde was using horrid “lang\|ridgo” amongst the
statues and fell into the arms of a policeman, and
the transit of Venus to the lock-up followed, to the
accompaniment of great eloquence on the part of
Venus, who objected to be transited. A pressman
gave way to the habit of the moment and so
pictorially described the “remarkably fine de-
velopment ” of the lady who was dragged away
that the court was crowded, and a huge bench of
Jaypees wept over her lovely form while inflicting
the usual fine. Since Sandow came, even the
person who applies for a billet in the ribbon
department states that he has three large ridges of
muscle on his lower chest.
The first politician to tell Maoriland Far-
mers’ Union to go to Sheol is Alfred Levavasour
Durell Eraser, member for Napier. Hitherto no
one ever suspected that shifty and unreliable, but
beauteous, young man of superfluous courage, but
he seems to have suddenly developed more back-
bone than even Lands Minister Duncan, who
condemns exposure of the farmers’ sweating
atrocities practised on their own children. The
Union has prepared a list of questions which it
will present to every candidate at the coming
general election, and the men who swallow most
of the Union’s platform are to receive the block
vote. Fraser received the list, and then jour-
neyed down to Hastings and met the farmers face
to face, and told them he had put their paper in
the dust-heap, that he had no intention of
answering the questions either verbally or in
writing, and if the farmers didn’t like it they
could strangle themselves.

“ Galvo ” : Announced (B. 9/8/’O2) that
“ Edison declares that he has solved the automo-
bile problem with a new storage battery.” Pro-
bably Edison has not declared anything of the
sort, and, anyhow, present writer is very sick of
Edisonian declarations which are really ; only
made by American journals when copy is scarce.
According to them Edison “declared,” when
England and U.S. seemed likely to have trouble
over Venezuela, that he could sweep the seas of the
British “as clean as your hand ” with his electric
chain-shot and what-not. The plain fact of the
matter is that Edison is an extremely smart busi-
ness man with the gift of assimilating the ideas
of others, and with a thorough appreciation of the
value Of advertisement. He is not a scientist —
nor even an electrician —in the same sense that
are Kelvin, Silvanus Thompson, Rayleigh,
Crookes, Rontgen, Ferranti, and half a hundred
others that the public seldom hears of, and his
genius is mostly newspaper-created. Followingare
a few of the matters for which Edison is popu-
larly held responsible: Electricity (I was actually
once asked seriously if Edison hadn’t “ invented”
electricity !), phonograph, telephone, dynamo, in-
candescent lamp, electric trams, chewing-gum,
telegraphs, magnetism, gravitation, light, qine-
matograph, heat, linotype, motion, photography,
jam-tarts, perambulators, and liars, He is prinoi,
pally responsible for the liars,
Exit Rignold.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Oh, yes ! Williamson’s post prandial remark
about the five Juliets is correct, as far as it goes.
Only there were seven instead of five. It was at
Booth’s Theatre, New York, in ’75, where, by the
way, I first produced ‘ Henry Y.’ The manage-
merit proposed a mixed bill towards the end ot tne
season as a draw, and I was put down for the
balcony scene with Adelaide Neilson. lhen as
Fanny Davenport, Ada Dyas, and Sarah Jewet,
and others were available amongst the Juliets, and
Coglan and Harry Montague among the Romeos,
the idea was suggested
of performing the whole
play with different stars
for different scenes. The
male element objected,
however, to sectionise as
Romeo, so I took ’em all
on.”
“And your ideal
Juliet ?”
“ Neilson, of course.
Her age, beauty, tem-
perament, and art com-
bined to make her the
poet’s ideal of last, or
possibly any, century. I
originally played the part
with her at the Queen’s,
Long Acre, in ’72. God-
win, who afterwards
made a name with Irving,
designed my dress, which
the press said was a
revelation. I think I
was the first to play the
part as a lusty lover, and
not as a love-lorn loon.”
“No, I’ve no favorite
parts. Henry V. has, of
course, brought me the
best returns from the
box-office, which possibly
influences one, but. I
enjoy playing Bill
Burkly, Macari, or Mor-
timer Mumpleford just
as well. ‘Julius Cffisar ’
and ‘Midsummer Night’s
Dream ’ were the most
successful Australian
productions., after
‘ Henry V.,’ artistically
and financially.”
“ Do you mean to say
they brought in more
money than your big
melodrama productions?”
“Asa class, yes. Be-
cause I’ve never lost
money over a Shake-
spearian play, and I’ve
had some damnable melo-
dramatic failures.”
“ You still think there’s money in the ‘legit.,’
then ? ”
“ Undoubtedly—if a man will take the time
and trouble to do it adequately. The popular
managerial idea is—give the public what they
want. I can’t say I quite agree. Perhaps we’re
living a bit faster, but I can’t see the amazing
degeneracy in dramatic taste. The musical
comedy that is now running at three Sydney
theatres seems to me no better and no worse than
the stuff Baker and Farren did, when I first struck
Australia. London West End managers can, of
course, appeal to particular tastes, but you can’t
expect a community that is, as yet, making its
aesthetic blood and muscle, to go the whole hog
for the higher drama. Your Guy Boothby sells
100 per cent, better than George Meredith, but
the latter will live longer. This is a consolation
for the dramatist, if it is denied the artistic actor.
“Do I think I would have done better if I’d
hung my banner out 25 years in London or New
York instead of Sydney? Well, I might have
struck a tide that would have flowed to bigger
things, and I might have struck an ebb one, with
a backwash. Of course, if I had had more capital
I could have done more, but I’ve never had a
backer all my life. If you hit ’em really hard, and
get a following in London, you’re an institution,
and the enormous floating population visits you
just the same as the Tower and Madame
Tussaud. I produced ‘Henry V.’ under
Harris’ management, on Sept. 1, 1879,
at Drury Lane. I remember the date exactly
because Irving inaugurated his big production
of ‘The Merchant of Venice’ at the Lyceum on
the same night. The press asked me to give way,
but I couldn’t see it, as I was the new-comer.
Well —everyone seems to know Irving’s financial
position if they don’t know mine. He comes out
to Australia, I go to England. .
“The usual farewell benefit? Yes, the subject
was broached, but I’m well enough off. If it
had been possible I should have liked to have
asked friends and supporters to a farewell per-
formance. Some day, if ‘dead to the world,’l
may want one—then I suppose I won’t get one.
Vale / ” . ,
And George Rex took up his presentation bag
and walked.
A.D.
Sample of Australian journalism :—
This monstrous baboon beast has been jibbering and
jabbering at me for the last six or seven months. While
I lay in hospital at Brisbane, hanging between life and
death for several weeks, with a gaping hole in my head
as big as this bestial brute’s mouth, he hurled his putrid
paragraphs at me, and took a peculiar delight in digging
up and hurling at me as I lay prone and prostrate the
shattered fragments of my domestic hearth and broken
household gods—blasted hopes. Curse and confound this
scurvy, scabrous, scrofulous scoundrel!
What a chance for an Australian artist to paint
an allegorical picture of a baboon hurling the
domestic hearth at the gentleman quoted above.
“ Jungle ” writes :
The Vic. Govt., apparently, takes the bun for "kidding
a man on.” A good worker told me recently that, after
tramping 90 miles “ on spec,” he was engaged to work on
the Mildura line. He bought a new shovel (price 55.) and
provisions (price 10s.), but only got one day in ! The
only explanation he elicited was that “ others must have
a chance,” and that he “would probably be put on
again.” He left—and so did a lot more. The usual
batch of unemployed, it appears, had arrived from the
city on free passes.

Within the last few weeks four workmen
have been killed in Victoria by accidentally
touching Uncovered electric wires.
The luck of children again. At Timor
(Vic.), on Sept. 6, a youngster, aged 5, fell down
the unprotected shaft of the old Billygoat mine,
and 20 minutes later was recovered, quite unin-
jured, at a depth of 35ft., where he was found
clinging to a slab with his head just above water.
Same evening a railway employee, a steady man,
with a wife and eight children dependent on him,
tripped over a small mullock heap at Elaine
(Vic.), and, falling into a shaft only 6ft. deep,
broke his neck.

Mr. W. G. Hearne.—Dear Sir,—l am writing to tell you
about the wonderful cure your medicine has effected in
my case. About three years ago I began to cough. At
first the cough was not severe, but it gradually got worse,
and I became very weak and troubled with night sweats,
pain in my chest, and great quantities of phlegm. On
several occasions there was blood in the expectorated
matter. I had been treated by a doctor, who pronounced
my case to be Consumption, and various other treatments
had been tried, but without benefit. It was at this stage
that I heard of your Bronchitis Cure, and sent to you for
a course of the medicine. When it arrived I was too ill to
leave my bed, but I commenced taking it at once, and
gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.

since I commenced to take it. I used to be in bed a fort-
night at a time always, and after that for months I was
as weak as I could possibly be, and was always taking
cod liver oil, so you will see at once it is quite worth
while sending for it such a long distance.
“ Something more I must tell you. Charlotte has been
very ill since I wrote you. Her cough was so bad she
never had a night’s rest, and was spitting up blood
very much. The doctor told her husband that there was
nothing more he could do for her. So on the Sunday I
sent her half a bottle of the Bronchitis Cure, and told
her to try it, and if she did not use it, not to waste it,
but send' it back again. She had such confidence in her
doctor that I thought she would not try it. On the

from the lungs better. She sent for another half-bottle,
and on the following Sunday sent over to say that she
was quite cured and did not require any more medicine.
So you see what great good it has done, and she wishes
to have some of my next supply.”

The passing of the Postal Rates Bill will
effectually squash the long and unnecessary
0.H.M.5.. wires sent by every tinpot official too
lazy to write letters; also certain State Ministers
who transact private mining business by “free”
wire will have to pay up.
“Jungle”: Came across a pair of new-
ohum sft. wood-jammers just outside a Wimmera
(Vic.) township. They were getting Is. 9d. a ton
(trees to be cut off at the ground), and averaged
two tons a day between them. That meant
10s. 6d. a week each, less breakages, and one was
paying 12s. for board at a pub, while the other, a
married man, was paying 9s. rent, and Bd. a lb.
for meat. Both were heavy smokers, but their
axes cost nothing. They were “ borrowed ” from
adjacent wood-heaps. It took those experts eight
weeks to discover that they weren’t making a
shilling a day !
“Burglar Bill” re the administration of
justice “inside”:—
The visiting “ beak ” is, in gaol, the fourth person of
the Trinity. Three whistles from the gate herald his ap-
proach. Prisoners (lined up outside the justice room five
paces apart) are faced the other way while the Great Man
and the gaol-governor walk into the court, because a
glimpse of his face beforehand might lessen the terror
which his countenance should strike into the heart
of the criminal. The prisoner, in the court, holds his hat
m his left hand, stands to “attention,” and waits. The
offence may have been merely the giving of a “ chew,” an
ounce of sugar or a pinch of tea to a fellow prisoner, but
the charge says “ trafficking.” Putting one’s own num-
ber on a blanket in the store-room which one might think
a shade better than one’s own is “ wilfully damaging pub-
lic P ro ,l lert y-” have known a man get seven days
‘ dark” for putting his initials on a whitewashed wall.)
A prisoner inadvertently omitting to salute an official is
charged with “insolence”; while a prisoner’s “delegate”
to the cook-house who considers an article unfit for food,
and has the courage to say so, is charged “ with making
a false statement with a view to creating a riot in gaol.” A
prisoner is put into “lockup cells” when the supposed
offence is committed, and waits there perhaps for four
or five days before the “ beak ” arrives.

HUDDERSFIELD, ENGLAND,
This Tangled World.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They lived at Drummoyne. He was in a city
bank; and she was his wife—the wife of
a few months. She had been brought up in
the bush, and was used to riding and out-door
exercise. Above all things she liked plenty of
room—room to romp. This she had not in the
rocky yard attached to their pretty cottage; and
she would sit through the day and mope. She
couldn’t cook decently herself, and they kept a
woman who managed that after a fashion. Had
she been anything of a housewife it would have
been well for her, as her time —a lot of it, at least
—would have been taken up by the household.
Tom had met her during a bicycling excursion,
and had liked her for her freshness and her milk-
and-roses complexion. She didn’t make up, as
she was young, and looked it. She had liked
him, perhaps because he was so big ; perhaps
Just now she was wondering why she married and
gave up the life she loved, and the brothers and
sisters, and the horses—especially the horses—
and everything. What wouldn’t s'lio give now for
a flutter on Dandy, or a trip to the spring with
o}d Nugget for a cask of water? She felt like a
caged bird, and pitied the canary. She couldn’t
even go to town without crossing the water. It
was water everywhere ; and she hated water and
boats. Somebody was always trying to catch a
boat, or something. It was all very sickening.
In the country nobody ever tried hard to catch
anything ; everything there seemed to wait.
What had marriage done for her, anyhow ? It
meant a man coming to see her every evening, and
leaving her in the mornings to pass long days
with a sour-visaged, unsympathetic cook as sole
companion. Tom rarely took her out, and
she began to wonder if he really loved her. He
had never—since mar.ry»ge—told her he did, and
she couldn’t be sure.
Like many country girls she was, in some
things, very simple. Mr. Brown —a friend of
Tom’s—who used to step in o’ days, “just to
cheer her up a bit,” had told her but, she
thought it was very mean of Mr. Brown. If Tom
only knew, what would he do? He’d surely kick
Mr. Brown. Yes, Brown was what Tom would
call a cad; but he hadn’t found him out. It
takes a woman to know a man’s friends.
Brown was a nightly visitor at the cottage. He
and Tom used to play chess, whilst she sat reading
and thinking—mostly thinking. Chess, to her,
seemed a silly game. She used to play euchre at
home, and liked it. Mr. Brown was very thought-
ful. He seemed to anticipate her slightest wish,
and was ever ready to oblige. Was the light too
strong ? Would she prefer being nearer the fire ?
It was always Mr. Brown who asked. Tom was
not thoughtful; and he was not polite—not very
polite, at least. He was only good—yes, he was
very good, and big.
One evening Tom was to be detained at the
bank. It was, he said, the half-yearly balance.
She said she would go to town; and, perhaps,
wait for him. She reached the city early, and
lunched with Mr. Brown, who was a man of
leisure, and always about. Nobody could help
meeting Mr. Brown. At lunch, he spoke of going
to Campbelltown, and she wished she could do the
trip. She dearly loved the country.
An idea came to Brown, and he worked on it.
Why, he asked himself, should she not do the
trip ? Why not ? Tom was a wooden man who
couldn’t appreciate the woman, whilst he had
never met one he liked better. The pity was he
hadn’t met her before, but that mustn’t make any
difference now. Tom might make a fuss about it
for a while, but he’d get over it all right. He was
not the sort of man to fret, and if he did make a
noise and he —Brown—found himself called upon
to figure as co-respondent, he had the money and
could pay the price. Anyhow, it was worth
chancing.
The evening train was leaving Redfern station.
There were many passengers and a great scramble.
A cab dashed up and a lady and gentleman
alighted and entered a first-class carriage. After
a while a tattered newsboy stepped up and sold a
penny newspaper. The gentleman handed him a
threepenny bit and waited for his change, which
the boy was a long time in finding. At last he
handed up a couple of coppers, which the gentle-
man pocketed.
The lady sat in a corner thinking. She was
thinking of that change. Would Tom have
watched and waited for it and have taken it? No!
She was sure he wouldn’t, as he was never mean—
anything but mean. And the boy was such a
delicate-looking little fellow, too. Then she de-
cided. Brown had buried his face in the paper,
and she quietly opened the door and slipped out
on to the platform, almost into a gentleman’s
arms. It was Tom. Next moment the engine
whistled, and the train moved off with Mr.
Brown.
She explained that she was seeing their friend
(Miss MTherson) off. Kitty, she said, had gone
to Picton. It was her first great lie to Tom ; and
he knew it, for he himself had been to Coino with
Kitty, and had just put her on a tram.
Three days later, at breakfast, Tom said,
“Why, Nell, I met Brown in town yesterday,
and he told me he went to Campbelltown tbe
night you saw Kitty off. ”
Nell bore up bravely, but it was a facer.
That evening, when Tom returned from town,
he found her far from well. The doctor called it
shock to the nerves, and prescribed perfect rest
with, later on, a change to the country. So she
went home for a month, and had a great time
among the stock.
Just now she is back with Tom, and they have
the Browns for neighbors. Brown married Kitty;
and Tom, as an old friend of the M'Phersons,
gave the bride away.
YARRUM.
Melbourne lampless bike-riders have been
having a bad time of late at the hands of the
police. A suburban householder, the other night,
heard sundry mysterious noises, and, upon inves-
tigating, discovered three burglars packing up
some valuables prior to flitting. He clambered
down a balcony-post, got his bike from the rear
and set out for the police-station. Hardly had he
turned the corner of the street, however, when a
policeman called upon him to stop. This the
bike-man did, and began hastily to explain, but
was met with a stony smile and the remark : “ It’s
no use yez thryin’ to come thot yarrn over me ; so
just come along wid me to the station an’ be
charged wid havin’ no loight on yer boike.” So a
partially-dressed householder was marched to the
station, where his emphatic assurances at length
induced the sergeant to send a couple of men back
with him to investigate. They reckon they have
found a clue, but the burglars nad taken practic-
ally everything else.
An Unfinished Tragedy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jack Bkoderibb drew his breath heavily, as a
man in physical pain might, and passed one hand
across his bewildered eyes; the other held a slip
of notepaper he had, a short while before, picked
up from the garden path, and which he had since
been regarding with mingled incredulity and
despair. His very eyeballs ached with the intensity
of his gaze as he stood vainly striving to read any
other than their legitimate meaning into the
words written there.
His wife a wanton ?
Impossible !!
Why, it was inconceivable. Yet—yet—there,
staring him in the face, was the unmistakable
proof in her own handwriting.
Were not the fanciful flourishes of those
capital letters and the affectation of the Greek
“e’s ” but too surely familiar to him ? So familiar,
indeed, that the cold horror of certainty,
which had been slowly coiling itself about his
heart as he read and re-read the little note he had
at first fancied must be addressed to himself,
crushed all hope and joy and kindliness for ever
out of it, and forced, at last, a groan of acknow-
ledgment from his dry lips.
He himself wrote a round, labored, schoolboy
sort of hand that, in laughing defiance of her
playful ridicule, he had often called “not a bad
sort of a fist.” For she prided herself on writing
in what she was pleased to term a very “genteel”
style, having been educated at a fashionable
boarding-school.
He remembered all these trivialities with curi-
ous accuracy, despite the stupor that seemed to be
clouding his brain.
Only a few months previously the schoolmaster
of the little country school close by had come to
live with them. Jack could recall his wife’s very
words, as she had suggested the arrangement.
“He is such a perfect gentleman ; and, though I
don’t know him, I know his folks well. And
there is really no place in the village for the poor
fellow to board at. Besides, it would be such
company for you, dear.”
The farmer had not wanted any other company
than his wife’s ; but she, with her usual gentle
insistence, had carried her point. “The house is
so quiet ” she had complained, with a soft little
sigh of pathetic significance. Whereat he, too,
had sighed, for it was a bitter disappointment to
him that no children’s voices were to be heard
about the old home.
He tried to remember all that had happened
since; but here his memory failed him, for the
feeling of stupor was beginning to overpower him
at last.
However, just then there came the sound of
approaching footsteps, and a moment later a
man’s figure appeared round the bend of the
garden that hid the house from view.
It was the schoolmaster !
His eyes were carefully scanning the ground, as
if in search of something ; so that he did not see
his host till he was quite close to him. Perceiving
him then, he started perceptibly, and a slight
flush rose to his face.
“ I—er—” he began, but paused as he became
vaguely conscious of an expression in the other’s
eyes that he had never seen there before. For, at
the sound of the voice, the fog lifted, as
if by magic, from Jack Broderibb’s brain. He
became almost fiendishly cool and incisive.
“Perhaps this is what you are looking for,” he
suggested, politely and dispassionately, in chill,
deliberate tones —tones so unlike his ordinary
hearty ones that they seemed to himself those of
a stranger. As ho spoke, he handed the open note
to its owner, who mechanically took it.
He then watched with a strangely impersonal
curiosity the dawning look of terror in the school-
master’s eyes.
“ So the fellow’s a coward into the bargain,” he
mused, half regretfully, as the other’s face, losing
its flush, turned swiftly white—then slowly green.
“Oh Lord ! how in the world am I to kick an
abject thing that looks as if it had already been
well kicked ? ” he asked himself in weary disgust.
His hand was on the traitor’s collar now.
“Pah ! —damn it, man, show a little more fight
—for God’s sake, do !” he said, contemptuously,
to the shaking object he was about to thrash.
* * * *
Later on, at the door of the house, he met the
woman, coming out in all unconsciousness, to look
for him ; her face wearing its usual expression of
madonna-like innocence.
“ Wherever have you been ? ” she asked, smil-
ing.
“I’ve been spoiling your ‘perfect gentleman’s ’
beauty for him, ” he replied, with grim pleasantry.
“He’s out there. You’d better go and help him
fix his nose on again.”
She gave a little shriek and leaned against the
doorway, but rallied almost at once to say : “llow
dare you speak to me like that ? Are you mad ? ”
“I wish he had shown as much spirit as you,”
he said, a little wistfully. “ There’s no fun in
punching feather-pillows.”
He passed on into the house ; and left her there,
shivering with apprehension.
On he walked—right into the little dining-room
at the back, vaguely wondering at his own
apathy; for his only sensations were those of
extreme weariness and slight nausea.
As he entered the room his glance fell on a half-
finished sock that his wife had been knitting for
him when he had kissed her last, just before he
went, out into the garden, and which she had
evidently laid down. Curiously enough, the sheer
domesticity of the incident brought back with a
shock a sense of their past happy relations. After
all, he thought, she could never really be any-
body’s but his ; and perhaps he had been careless
—over-confident. Well, it was a less >n. It
proved that they were right, after all, who said
that even the best of women needed watching.
He would go to her, and
And the end of it was that the schoolmaster
went out of their lives as completely as if he had
never stretched a “gentlemanly” hand to “shake
the pillars of domestic peace ” —as stable now, to
all appearance, as before this unconventionally-
aborted tragedy.
DOROTHY ELTON.
Ontbe Mt. Morgan (Q.) line the drought
is killing the trees by scores. The hills for long
distances are marked with yellow treetops. All
sizes and ages are being affected, and as many of
the trees must be a century or two old, it suggests
that the present drought takes the cake there-
abouts for severity.
Already the rabbit curse begins to dis-
appear in Victoria. The reports of the inspectors
to Lands Minister M ‘Kenzie lately stated that it
is “ now impossible for even experienced trappe: s
to make fair wages, owing to the scarcity of
rabbits.” The rabbit-trade is worth nearly
.£200,000 a year to Victoria.

IiTLIVES THE
Life is Strife,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The measureless March of Ages (
Is marshalled by Force and Fate,
And in Nature’s blood-writ pages
Are threatenings, soon or late,
All beings that love and cherish
The dream of a peaceful life
Shall utterly, utterly perish—
For Creation’s law is strife !
For the blood of most that liveth,
From the strong to the weak and small,
Fate’s merciless slaughter giveth
For life’s food, to one and all;
And the voice of God hath spoken,
With no uncertain speech—
Where Nature’s laws are broken,
Doom comes to all and each.
On Slavery’s sons grown timid,
And Luxury’s sons grown weak,
Sooner or late fierce Nature
Will merciless justice wreak.
It may be a hundred thousand,
It may be a million years,
But she will not pause till her outraged laws
Drown wealcuess in blood and tears !
If Man’s age grew to a million !
What then is a million years ?
Or even a thousand million,
To the milliards of the spheres,
Which were misty flames, ere the seons
Of unknown ages begun ;
And Time grew strong, ere the paeans
Rolled forth for the first-born sun !
Though for a brief Millenium,
Perchance, thro’ its"thousand years,
Strife may seem to sleep—and mankind reap
Life’s joys without blood or tears
And man toiling upward, arriving
At his “ Golden Age ” at last—
See the gentle and loving surviving
The strife of the terrible past,
And believe the prophetic yearning
Of Hope, in the earlier time,
Has conquered all wrong—and as loving as tho’
He inherits his “Golden Prime ” !
That the strife of ages is conquered,
And with reverend, upward eyes
He thanks high Heaven his soul has striven
Till Earth is made Paradise !
And life, at its highest high water,
Seems one wide, calm, sunlit wave—
Yet its certain ebb shall bring slaughter,
For nothing the weak can save !
Yea, though Peace may reign for a little,
Fate can well that pause afford,
And her smile is dour—as she spends that
hour,
In whetting her awful sword !
And ah ! but the sorrowful wisdom
Experience shows us as truth,
Most goodness and meekness, is twin-born
with weakness,
Doomed by Nature and Fate without ruth.
For only the fiercest and strongest,
The longest may struggle and strive,
As Earth, drying to death, sees life’s slow-
ebbing breath,
And they —the last ones to survive !
And so let no man feel certain,
Peace shall conquer, “ God’s Law of Life,”
For the seeing soul, reads in Nature’s scroll,
That its law diurnal, and law Eternal,
Is merciless, merciless Strife !
A.F.W.
Explorations in Civilisation.
BEING THE LETTERS OF AN AUSTRALIAN IN
EXILE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nov. 8. To-day I went to London. London—
what a rich, happy, ancient dress we in Australia
invest it with ! We have heard so much of it
that we expect a city of many marvels—full of St.
Paul’s Cathedrals and Westminster Abbeys, and
great singers and actors, and a House of Commons
where nothing under a political genius may enter,
and a new soil opposed to all known geology, and
a new sky and sun. (Better say nothing about
the English sun in these days. After two months’
residence in London I have seen it twice.)
I bought a paper on the Dover station, and
while so doing a porter forcibly possessed himself
of my bag, and carried it to the train. Arrived at
the train, I found I had no change except one
penny ; all my other money was in sovereigns.
I looked at the penny and at the porter—in
Australia a gift of a penny would be met with
insult —however, I wanted to see the heathen
porter rage, and I gave him the penny. He took
it, touched his hat, and said “Yes, sir—much
erbliged, sir; thank yer, sir,” and drew from a
great pocket, that seemed to take up all the space
of his coat lining, a family purse about half the
size of a brief-bag. In’fhis he carefully deposited
the penny, and snapped the spring back again
with the air of a man who could now defy Fortune.
The train got under way, and I opened the
London daily paper and read. You can’t always
discover the ways of a people by their journalism
—because, in most cases, a paper reflects the
opinions of the one man who runs the paper that
the peonle reads ; but you can infallibly discover
what some of the people are by reading their ad-
vertisements, and here are some of the ads. I
read —
T) EFINED Gentleman wishes accommodation as a
Paying Guest at Tooting.
The man who would call himself a “ refined
gentleman ” need not necessarily be identifiable,
but the self-styled “refined gentleman” who
doesn’t want mere “ board and lodging ” but
“accommodation as a paying guest,” and at a
place with a name like “ Tooting,” is as present
to the eye as if you had his photograph.
I can see that “refined gentleman” and his
spectacles, and the exclusiveness that comes from
the knowledge of his “refined gentlemanliness.”
Also I can imagine his indigestion and his belief in
the utter vulgarity of everybody except himself.
Poor devil!
The next ad. was :
V> EFINED Gentlemen are offered, as paying guests,
residence with a refined lady and gentleman
with a wide knowledge of-English literature and the
finest pronunciation.
Evidently there are more “refined gentlemen”
than the one who wanted to be a paying guest at
Tooting. The “finest pronunciation ” ought to be
handy in collecting the board money of a paying
guest whose luggage consists of bricks.
That reminds me of a girl I knew in Australia.
She was unduly aggressive with the “ Higher
Education for Girls” she had just commenced,
but was otherwise charming.
Speaking of a lady she had never seen, she
said : “I am sure I shall like her. She must be
so refined.”
“ What makes you say that ? ” I asked.
“ Why, she writes her name with a cedilla.”
Which is as good a reason as any other.
I begin to study the , country. There is no
more forest—the few clumps of trees have plainly
been planted by man. The little strips of grass
land —sometimes only an acre or two in extent, are ,
ditched and hedged very carefully. These little
strips are called “ fields. ” Oh! sense of propor-
tion ! where are you ? Only occasionally in the
tree plantations is there a patch that looks like
natural growth ; it seems formal and still —not a '
suggestion of the art that imitates nature, but a
trumpeting forth of the unnatural fact of nature
imitating art.
There is so much in a telling word. It has an
atmosphere—a picture—in it. When, in Aus-
tralia, I read of a “copse,” a “spinney,” a
‘field,” a “wold," a “stile,” a “wood,” a
“forest,” I thought these things would be like
their names. And I find that a “copse” and a
“spinney” are little belts of light timber with
undergrowth, that a “field” may be three acres
of grass land, hedged and ditched ; that a “stile”
is a series of rude steps for'the crossing of a fence,
made for people who are too weak or too drunk
to climb over. A “ wood ”is a plantation of a
dozen acres, and a “forest” is a big “wood.”
Next time the Englishman in Australia smiles
pityingly at a hundred miles of forest of eucalypti
—one of the most graceful, most beautiful trees
God ever made—and talks of English forests to
the disadvantage of Australian natural beauty—
smite him, my brothers; smite him bigly !
The train raced past a surprisingly green pine—
of no vegetable green I had ever seen before, but
exactly similar in tint to those bright green toy
pine trees you find in Noah’s Arks.
Past hideously new towns of terraces, and into
the country again. More Noah’s Ark pines,
small fields and formal hedges, and away on the
landlift to the sky-line windmills grinding wheat,
as in the days of the Saxons. I haven’t seen any
such back-number machines as windmills in a
Protectionist country, anyway. Freetrade
methods, like Freetrade itself, belong to the time
when men believed that the world was flat, or
some period prior to the discovery that iron ships
will float. Freetrade will be a fine policy at about
the time of the Millenium ; meanwhile the wind-
mills grind wheat, when wheat and wind happen
along simultaneously.
At Tonbridge, a fresh-colored young man
entered the compartment, and when we started
he apologetically asked for a match. I gave him
the match, and we talked many things.
He spoke wonderful English; so I was sur-
prised when he said: “I can’t weally believe
you’re an Austwalian. I met an Austwalian at a
place in Gwosvena Squa-ah and he was talkin’
just like an Austwalian, don’t you know—but
you have no more accent that I—aw—have.”
Which reminds me again. Old John Robert-
son, one time Premier of New South Wales, was
a fearful-and wonderful speaker whose voice was
rarely understood. One day he went to a bird-
fancier’s shop in George-street, Sydney, and said
to the keeper:
“ I fay, have you godder barrot ? ”
“ Yes,” said the man. “ Here he is.”
“ I fay, gan he torgue ? ”
“Can he talk—splendid—hear him !”
The parrot talked very warmly.
“ I fay—thatf not bad. How mudge ? ”
“ Ten pound.”
“Den'powgnd ! Thatf a shoggin’ high prife—
gan he torgue well ? ”
“ Well ?” said the fancier (who knew his man).
“ If he couldn’t talk better than you I’d wring his
neck.”
At any rate, I had discovered in that train
journey that I spoke good English because I had
no accent, and talked exactly like a man who Had
several.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Consolation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Strange how powerfully some people influence
us—quickening the brain and stirring it into more
life ! Yes ! I feel like that with Cyril. When
he is near I’m impelled to try to be something
above the common ruck. Bob —sleepy old Bob !
I can’t imagine why I ever married him. I won-
der how 1 learned to love Cyril ? I shouldn’t, of
course. How did it begin ? Unless one makes a
catalogue of the impulses the moment they flash
across the brain the chance is lost. So this new
impulse was borne in me —me, a respectable
married woman ! How cold and prosaic the term
sounds! One always thinks of a “respectable
married woman ” as a creature of the middle-class
verging towards fifty.
But this new impulse is not the humdrum
affection of a married' female with calm conven-
tional habits, but one which devastates and con-
sumes. Will it last more than a season ? Would
it be better to hold to the lesser, or shall I go with
him to-night? Yes, I must go—l promised.
That man’s snore, coming to my ears whilst I
think, nearly maddens me. I’m quite ready—l
shall stand on the verandah and await the hour.
Fifteen minutes till the midnight express passes
Will it bear me outward with him to a glorious
new life ? But no ! there’s nothing to prove that
such passion will last; whilst cold respectability
will endure for life.
How the tiles of the station-house shine in the
moonlight, and the roofs of the unused cars gleam
wetly under its light. Oh ! what a glorious sky !
A blue ocean, flecked with clouds of foam. The
wind is rising, the white waves sweep forward
across the moon. Is that cold moon symbolic r f
my hope, and those clouds ray passions, which
swallow everything. They darken—they grow
blacker, hardly a beam of light is left. That
booming noise—it is the express. I’ll go—there
is time yet. But how hidden the moon is—l
can’t go—l daren’t. Oh God ! have pity ! . . .
I’ll stay here. He swore he’d love me always,
whether I went or not.
The express has whistled. Too late now; too
late. _
I must creep back to my room. How can I ?
That brute is snoring stilL But, perhaps, after
all, Cyril snores too ! I hope he does ; it would
make Bob so much easier to bear.
VIC.
ST. L.
